The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
First Condition of Mixed Modal Syllogisms in the Second Figure
Author states the first of two conditions for the second figure in mixed modal syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the first condition for validity in mixed modal second-figure syllogisms to determine whether a conclusion follows.

Core Idea
A mixed modal second-figure syllogism is valid only if the minor is permanent or the major's negative is convertible.

Mixed modal syllogisms combine premises of different modalities, such as necessity and permanence. In the second figure, special conditions govern validity. This lesson unpacks the first of two conditions, showing when a conclusion necessarily follows and when it fails. Understanding this prevents mistaken inferences in modal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two disjuncts of the first condition
The author states that the condition for valid production is 'permanence of the minor' (that it be dā'ima or ḍarūriyya) OR 'conversion of the soundness of the major' (i.e., its negative is convertible). Recognize these as two alternative sufficient conditions.
The minor is the subject-premise in the second figure; the major is the predicate-premise.
2
Determine the modal types required for the major
The major must be among the six proposition types whose negatives are convertible. If not, the minor must be non-permanent and the major among the seven types with non-convertible negatives, leading to a false conclusion.
The six types include specific modal propositions; the seven are the complements.
3
Test the condition with the given example
Examine the counterexample: minor 'Everything eclipsed is dark as long as it is eclipsed, not permanently' (non-permanent). Major 'Nothing of the moon is dark at the time of quadrature, not permanently' (negative with non-convertible type). The conclusion is false, showing the condition is violated.
The false conclusion demonstrates that failing the condition invalidates the syllogism.
4
Apply the condition to another modal syllogism
Given a mixed modal second-figure syllogism, check: (1) Is the minor dā'ima or ḍarūriyya? (2) Is the major's negative convertible? If either holds, the conclusion may follow; if neither, the argument is likely invalid.
This is a practical test for validity in such syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في المختلطات يلزم النتيجة ضرورية لا دائمة، أو دائمة لا دائمة هذا ، وعلى الله التكلان ؛ فإنه العليم الخبير ( وَفِي) الشكل (الثاني) يشترط شرطان، أحدهما أحد الأمرين : ( إنما شرط إنتاج الشكل الثاني دَوَامُ الصُّغْرَى)، بأن تكون دائمة أو ضرورية، أو انعكاس مسالمة الكبرى) . (حسب الجهة أي كون الكبرى من القضايا الستة التي تنعكس سوالبها، سواء كانت موجبات أو سوالب ، وإلا فالصغرى من الإحدى عشر غير الدائمتين، والكبرى من السبع الغير المنعكسة السوالب، وحينئذ قد يتخلف النتيجة، نحو كل منخسف مظلم ما دام منخسفا لا دائما، أو وقت حيلولة الأرض لا دائما». لا شيء من القمر بمظلم وقت التربيع لا دائما ، مع كذب النتيجة. وهذا الاختلاط الأخص لما لم ينتج لم ينتج ما هو أعم منه .
Chapter on Mixed [Modalities]. The conclusion follows necessarily not permanently, or permanently not permanently, and upon God is reliance; for He is the All-Knowing, the Expert. In the second figure, two conditions are required. One of them is one of two matters: the condition for the second figure's productivity is the permanence of the minor, by being permanent or necessary, or the conversion of the soundness of the major. According to the mode [i.e., the major must be among the six propositions whose negatives are convertible, whether affirmative or negative; otherwise the minor is among the eleven non-permanent, and the major among the seven whose negatives are not convertible; and then the conclusion may fail, such as: 'Everything eclipsed is dark as long as it is eclipsed, not permanently, or at the time of the earth's interposition, not permanently. Nothing of the moon is dark at the time of quadrature, not permanently,' with the conclusion false. And this more specific mixed [syllogism] when it does not produce, what is more general than it does not produce.
Source pages p. 522

Commentary
This passage is from a commentary on the conditions for mixed modal syllogisms in the second figure. The author begins by introducing the topic of mixed modalities. He then states that in the second figure, two conditions are required for a valid conclusion. The first condition itself is one of two matters: either the minor premise is permanent (dā'ima or ḍarūriyya), or the major premise has a convertible negative (i.e., the opposite of the major with the same terms is true). The author explains that the major must belong to the six types of propositions that have convertible negatives. If not, the minor would be among the eleven non-permanent types and the major among the seven types with non-convertible negatives, leading to a false conclusion. An example is given: if the minor is 'everything eclipsed is dark as long as it is eclipsed' (a possible, not permanent) and the major is 'nothing of the moon is dark at the time of quadrature' (a negative with non-convertible type), the conclusion 'nothing of the moon is eclipsed' would be false. The author then notes that if the more specific mixed (with specific conditions) does not yield a conclusion, the more general mixed (with broader conditions) also does not.

Checkpoint
In a mixed modal syllogism in the second figure, what does the first condition require?
A) The minor must be possible and the major necessary.
B) The minor must be permanent or necessary, or the major must have a convertible negative.
C) The major must be permanent and the minor perpetual.
D) The minor must be convertible.

Key Takeaway
For a valid mixed modal syllogism in the second figure, either the minor premise must be permanent (dā'ima/ḍarūriyya) or the major premise's negative must be convertible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a mixed modal second-figure syllogism on an Islamic topic (e.g., wajib, mustahil) where the minor is permanent (dā'ima) and check if the condition is satisfied.
?
Given the syllogism: 'All angels are obedient (permanent). No disobedient being is an angel (possible).' Determine if the first condition for validity holds and explain.

The Syllogism

The Second Condition for Mixed Modal Syllogisms
Author states the second condition for mixed modal syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the second condition for mixing possible premises in the second figure and evaluate whether a given modal syllogism satisfies it by recognizing counterexamples.

Core Idea
A possible premise must be paired with a necessary or conditional major to avoid false conclusions.

When constructing mixed modal syllogisms in the second figure, certain restrictions apply. The second condition governs what type of major may accompany a possible premise. Without this condition, plausible premises can lead to absurd conclusions. Let's examine the condition, the reasoning behind it, and the counterexample that illustrates its necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
The second condition states that a possible premise (mumkina), whether minor or major, must be paired with a necessary (ḍarūriyya) or a conditional (mashrūṭa) major. Note the exception for the possible major: it can only be paired with a necessary major.
2
Examine the counterexample without the condition
Consider a possible major paired with a permanent minor: 'Nothing of the donkey is Zayd's mount permanently' (major) and 'Every donkey is Zayd's mount possibly' (minor). Both premises may be true, but the conclusion would be false. Here the major is not necessary or conditional, violating the condition.
This syllogism is in the second figure because the middle term (Zayd's mount) is the predicate in both premises.
3
Analyze the logical failure
The permanence in the major and possibility in the minor create a conflict: the conclusion would claim something impossible (e.g., a donkey is both permanently not Zayd's mount and possibly Zayd's mount). This shows that without the condition, the premises cannot be simultaneously true while producing a valid conclusion.
The author connects this to the 'more specific mixed' (al-ikhtilāṭ al-akhaṣṣ) and the issue of non-convertible negatives.
4
Generalize the condition
Thus, for any mixed syllogism in the second figure involving a possible premise, ensure that the major is either necessary or conditional. The same applies if the minor is possible – it requires a necessary or conditional major to avoid contradictions.
This condition is one of two matters; the other (not detailed here) involves the major being possible with a necessary minor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(و) الثاني أيضا أحد الأمرين : (كونُ المُمكنة)، سواء كانت صغرى أو كبرى، (مَعَ الضَّرُورِيَّةِ ، أَوْ كُبرى مشروطة) . إذا كانت صغرى حاصل هذا الشرط عدم استعمال الممكنة الصغرى إلا مع الضرورية الكبرى، أو إحدى المشروطتين، أو الممكنة الكبرى إلا مع الضرورية لا غير. بيان ذلك: لولاه لكان الكبرى الممكنة إما مع غير الدائمتين، وقد عرفت أنها لا تنتج في الاختلاط الأخص، وإما مع الدائمة الصغرى ، ولا يصدق النتيجة في قولنا «لا شيء من الحمار بمركوب زيد دائما ، وكل حمار مركوب زيد بالإمكان»، أو كان الصغرى الممكنة إما مع الكبريات السبع الغير المنعكس سوالبها، وقد عرفت أنها لا تنتج مع الأخص ، أو مع البسيطتين العرفية العامة والدائمة، ولا يصدق النتيجة حينئذ في مثل قولنا كل حمار مركوب زيد بالإمكان»، و«لا شيء من الناهق بمركوب زيد دائما ما دام الذات، أو مادام ناهقا»، وهذا موقوف على تخلف الدوام عن الضرورة في الكليات. وأما الممكنة الصغرى مع العرفية الخاصة فعند المتأخرين لا تنتج، فمنهم من بين بأن اللادوام لا دخل له في الإنتاج ؛ لكونه موافقا للصغرى في الكيف، والعرفية العامة غير منتجة ، كما عرفت، ولا يخفى ما فيه؛ فإن عدم إنتاج كل واحد من جزئين انفرادا لا يستلزم عدم إنتاج المجموع من المجموع ؛ فإن للاجتماع ربما يكون حكم لا يكون للأجزاء. ومنهم من قال: إن الإنتاج غير معلوم التحقق، وعند الشيخ إذا كانت الصغرى موجبة ممكنة والكبرى سالبة عرفية خاصة تنتج ممكنة عامة، بينه النصير الطوسي بالخلف، مثلا كل (ب) (ج) بالإمكان»، و«لا شيء من (أ) (ج) ما دام (أ) لا دائما ، فلا شيء من (ب) (أ) بالإمكان»، وإلا فبعض (ب) (أ) بالضرورة، وصدقه مع الكبرى محال، وإلا لزم ضرورية لا دائمة، وهذا لو تم لدل على إنتاجها مطلقة عامة. أجاب صاحب المطالع بأن غاية ما لزم منه صدقُ لا شيء من (ب) (أ) ، وليس للصغرى دخل في ذلك لاستقلال الكبرى في ذلك؛ إذ ذلك
And the second [condition] also is one of two matters: the possible [premise], whether minor or major, with the necessary or a conditional major. If it is minor, the meaning of this condition is that the possible minor is not used except with a necessary major, or one of the two conditionals, or the possible major except with the necessary only. Explanation of that: Otherwise, the possible major would be either with the two non-permanent, and you have known that it does not produce in the more specific mixed, or with the permanent minor, and the conclusion is not true in our saying 'Nothing of the donkey is Zayd's mount permanently, and every donkey is Zayd's mount possibly', or the possible minor would be either with the seven majors whose negatives do not convert, and you have known that it does not produce with the more specific, or with the two simple [premises] the general common and the permanent, and the conclusion is not true then in such as our saying 'Every donkey is Zayd's mount possibly', and 'Nothing of the brayer is Zayd's mount permanently as long as the essence, or as long as it brays', and this depends on the permanence falling short of necessity in universals. As for the possible minor with the special common, according to the later scholars it does not produce. Some of them explained that the non-permanent has no role in production because it agrees with the minor in quality, and the general common does not produce, as you know, and what is in it is not hidden; for the non-production of each of two parts individually does not entail the non-production of the combination from the combination; for the combination may have a ruling that the parts do not have. And some of them said: production is not known to be realized. And according to the Shaykh, if the minor is affirmative possible and the major is negative special common, it produces a general possible. Nasir al-Din al-Tusi demonstrated it by reductio: e.g., 'Every B is C possibly', and 'Nothing of A is C as long as A is, not permanently', then 'Nothing of B is A possibly', otherwise some B is A necessarily, and its truth with the major is impossible, otherwise a necessary not permanent would follow. If this were complete, it would indicate its producing an absolute general. The author of al-Matali' answered that the utmost that follows from it is the truth of 'Nothing of B is A', and the minor has no role in that because the major is independent in that; thus that.
Source pages p. 522

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of conditions for mixed modal syllogisms in the second figure, focusing on the second condition and a subsequent debate. The author states the second condition: a possible premise (mumkina), whether minor or major, must be paired with a necessary (ḍarūriyya) or a conditional (mashrūṭa) major. He explains the justification: without this condition, if the major were possible and the minor were permanent, the conclusion would be false, as shown by the example involving a donkey and Zayd's mount. Similarly, if the minor were possible and the major were among the seven types with non-convertible negatives, or with the general common (urfiyya 'āmma) or permanent, the conclusion would also be false. Then the author addresses the specific case of the possible minor with the special common major (urfiyya khāṣṣa). According to later scholars (muta'akhkhirūn), this combination does not produce a conclusion. Some argue that the non-permanent (lā dā'im) has no role in production because it matches the minor's quality, and the general common is unproductive; however, the author notes a flaw: the non-productivity of each part individually does not entail non-productivity of their combination. Others say that productivity is simply not known. In contrast, the Shaykh (al-Urmawi) holds that if the minor is affirmative possible and the major is negative special common, the conclusion is a general possible (mumkina 'āmma). Nasir al-Din al-Tusi demonstrated this by reductio: assume the opposite, and a contradiction follows. The author of al-Matali' responds that the demonstration only shows the truth of the negative conclusion from the major alone, without needing the minor. Thus the debate highlights the complexity of modal syllogistic logic.

Checkpoint
Why does the second condition forbid pairing a possible major with a permanent minor?
A) Because such a combination never yields a conclusion in any figure.
B) Because the example with the donkey shows a true premise set with a false conclusion.
C) Because the permanent minor is too weak to support a possible major.
D) Because the second figure requires necessary premises only.

Key Takeaway
The second condition ensures that modal mixing in the second figure does not lead to contradictions by requiring a possible premise to be accompanied by a necessary or conditional major.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a valid mixed modal syllogism in the second figure that satisfies the second condition: use a possible minor with a necessary major. For example, 'Every A is B possibly; No C is B necessarily; therefore No C is A possibly' – check whether this meets the condition.
?
Identify the flaw in the following syllogism: 'Nothing of the human is a bird permanently; Every human is a bird possibly.' Does it satisfy the second condition? Explain why the conclusion would be false.

The Syllogism
advanced
12 min
Debate on the Possible Minor with Special Common Major
Author discusses the debate on the possible minor with special common major.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Tusi's reductio argument for the productivity of a possible minor with a special common major and evaluate the objection raised by the author of al-Matali'.

Core Idea
Whether a possible minor with a special common major yields a conclusion is contested; al-Tusi's proof by reductio is challenged by the claim that the major alone suffices.

One of the most debated combinations in modal syllogisms is the pair of a possible minor and a special common major (urfiyya khāṣṣa). Later scholars deny its productivity, while al-Urmawi and al-Tusi defend it. The argument turns on subtle points about permanence and conditionality. We will walk through al-Tusi's reductio and the critique by the author of al-Matali'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the positions
Later scholars (muta'akhkhirūn) hold that a possible minor with a special common major does not produce a conclusion. Some argue that the non-permanent (lā dā'im) has no role, others that productivity is unknown. In contrast, al-Urmawi (the Shaykh) claims it does produce, and al-Tusi provides a demonstration.
Special common (urfiyya khāṣṣa) means 'true as long as the subject exists' – a restricted permanence.
2
Understand the major premise
The special common major is a negative universal proposition: 'Nothing of A is C as long as A is, not permanently' (e.g., 'No brayer is Zayd's mount as long as it brays, but not permanently'). This means the negative holds only during the existence of the subject, not forever.
This differs from the general common (urfiyya 'āmma) which holds at some times, and the permanent (dā'ima) which holds at all times.
3
Reconstruct al-Tusi's reductio
Assume the minor: 'Every B is C possibly' (affirmative universal possible), and the major: 'Nothing of A is C as long as A is, not permanently' (negative universal special common). To prove the conclusion 'Nothing of B is A possibly', assume the opposite: some B is A necessarily. Then that B is necessarily A, so it is C necessarily (since every B is C possibly, but necessity changes modality). This leads to a contradiction with the major, which states that nothing of A is C as long as A is, not permanently. Thus the opposite is impossible, so the conclusion holds.
The reductio assumes that if some B is A necessarily, then it is C necessarily, but the major denies that any A is C even temporarily? The exact steps are compressed; the key is that the demonstration shows a contradiction.
4
Evaluate the objection from al-Matali'
The author of al-Matali' objects that the reductio only proves the conclusion 'Nothing of B is A' (perhaps as a simple negative, not modal) and that this follows from the major alone without needing the minor. In other words, the major already entails the conclusion independently, so the combination does not demonstrate productivity.
This objection targets whether the minor contributes to the inference; if the major alone suffices, the syllogism is not truly mixed.

(و) الثاني أيضا أحد الأمرين : (كونُ المُمكنة)، سواء كانت صغرى أو كبرى، (مَعَ الضَّرُورِيَّةِ ، أَوْ كُبرى مشروطة) . إذا كانت صغرى حاصل هذا الشرط عدم استعمال الممكنة الصغرى إلا مع الضرورية الكبرى، أو إحدى المشروطتين، أو الممكنة الكبرى إلا مع الضرورية لا غير. بيان ذلك: لولاه لكان الكبرى الممكنة إما مع غير الدائمتين، وقد عرفت أنها لا تنتج في الاختلاط الأخص، وإما مع الدائمة الصغرى ، ولا يصدق النتيجة في قولنا «لا شيء من الحمار بمركوب زيد دائما ، وكل حمار مركوب زيد بالإمكان»، أو كان الصغرى الممكنة إما مع الكبريات السبع الغير المنعكس سوالبها، وقد عرفت أنها لا تنتج مع الأخص ، أو مع البسيطتين العرفية العامة والدائمة، ولا يصدق النتيجة حينئذ في مثل قولنا كل حمار مركوب زيد بالإمكان»، و«لا شيء من الناهق بمركوب زيد دائما ما دام الذات، أو مادام ناهقا»، وهذا موقوف على تخلف الدوام عن الضرورة في الكليات. وأما الممكنة الصغرى مع العرفية الخاصة فعند المتأخرين لا تنتج، فمنهم من بين بأن اللادوام لا دخل له في الإنتاج ؛ لكونه موافقا للصغرى في الكيف، والعرفية العامة غير منتجة ، كما عرفت، ولا يخفى ما فيه؛ فإن عدم إنتاج كل واحد من جزئين انفرادا لا يستلزم عدم إنتاج المجموع من المجموع ؛ فإن للاجتماع ربما يكون حكم لا يكون للأجزاء. ومنهم من قال: إن الإنتاج غير معلوم التحقق، وعند الشيخ إذا كانت الصغرى موجبة ممكنة والكبرى سالبة عرفية خاصة تنتج ممكنة عامة، بينه النصير الطوسي بالخلف، مثلا كل (ب) (ج) بالإمكان»، و«لا شيء من (أ) (ج) ما دام (أ) لا دائما ، فلا شيء من (ب) (أ) بالإمكان»، وإلا فبعض (ب) (أ) بالضرورة، وصدقه مع الكبرى محال، وإلا لزم ضرورية لا دائمة، وهذا لو تم لدل على إنتاجها مطلقة عامة. أجاب صاحب المطالع بأن غاية ما لزم منه صدقُ لا شيء من (ب) (أ) ، وليس للصغرى دخل في ذلك لاستقلال الكبرى في ذلك؛ إذ ذلك

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the objection raised by the author of al-Matali' against al-Tusi's demonstration?
A) The reductio uses an invalid figure.
B) The minor premise is not needed because the conclusion follows from the major alone.
C) The special common major cannot be negative.
D) The conclusion should be permanent rather than possible.

Key Takeaway
The debate over the possible minor with special common major reveals that modal syllogistic reasoning is sensitive to subtle differences in modality and that even valid demonstrations can be challenged on grounds of independence of premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Recreate al-Tusi's reductio using a concrete Islamic example: Replace B with 'every intoxicant', C with 'forbidden', A with 'wine'. Minor: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden possibly.' Major: 'Nothing of wine is forbidden as long as it is wine, not permanently.' What conclusion does al-Tusi claim follows? Is the conclusion plausible? Does the major alone entail it?

Critique the later scholars' argument that 'the non-permanent has no role in production because it agrees with the minor in quality'. Using the same example, evaluate whether the combination might have a different logical effect than each part alone.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
The Reductio Argument for Possible Minor and Perpetual Major
Debate on production of mixed modal syllogisms between later scholars, al-Urmawī, and al-Ṭūsī.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Ṭūsī's reductio argument for deriving a possible conclusion from a possible minor and a specific perpetual major, and evaluate al-Urmawī's objection.

Core Idea
Reductio can prove that a possible minor with a perpetual major yields a possible conclusion, but the independence of the major raises doubts.

In this passage, the author presents a debate about mixed modal syllogisms. The later scholars deny that a possible minor (e.g., 'Every B is possibly C') combined with a specific perpetual major (e.g., 'No A is C as long as A') produces any conclusion. However, the Sheikh (al-Urmawī) and al-Ṭūsī argue that it does produce a general possible conclusion. We will examine the reductio proof and the counter-argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and their modalities
The minor premise is an affirmative possible (موجبة ممكنة), e.g., 'Every B is possibly C'. The major premise is a negative specific perpetual (سالبة عرفية خاصة), e.g., 'No A is C as long as A' – meaning it holds at all times when A exists.
The perpetual proposition is stronger than a usual universal negative; it asserts a temporal universality.
2
Understand the claimed conclusion
The conclusion al-Ṭūsī aims to prove is a general possible negative: 'No B is possibly A' (لا شيء من ب أ بالإمكان). It is a possible, not a necessary or perpetual statement.
The conclusion's modality matches the minor premise.
3
Follow the reductio assumption
Assume the contradictory of the conclusion: 'Some B is necessarily A' (بعض ب أ بالضرورة). Combine this with the major: 'No A is C perpetually'. This leads to a contradiction because if some B is necessarily A and A is never C, then some B is necessarily not C, contradicting the minor that every B is possibly C.
The passage says: 'صدقه مع الكبرى محال' – its truth with the major is impossible.
4
Analyze the objection from al-Maṭāliʿ
The author of al-Maṭāliʿ (al-Urmawī) responds that the reductio only proves 'No B is A' (without the modal possibility), and the minor is not needed because the major alone suffices to negate A from everything that differs.
This objection challenges the necessity of the minor premise in the syllogism.
5
Evaluate the debate
The author notes that the non-productivity of two parts individually does not entail non-productivity of their sum; the combination may have a unique ruling.
Consider whether the combination has an effect that neither premise alone can produce.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يصدق النتيجة حينئذ في مثل قولنا كل حمار مركوب زيد بالإمكان»، و«لا شيء من الناهق بمركوب زيد دائما ما دام الذات، أو مادام ناهقا»، وهذا موقوف على تخلف الدوام عن الضرورة في الكليات.
وأما الممكنة الصغرى مع العرفية الخاصة فعند المتأخرين لا تنتج، فمنهم من بين بأن اللادوام لا دخل له في الإنتاج ؛ لكونه موافقا للصغرى في الكيف، والعرفية العامة غير منتجة ، كما عرفت، ولا يخفى ما فيه؛ فإن عدم إنتاج كل واحد من جزئين انفرادا لا يستلزم عدم إنتاج المجموع من المجموع ؛ فإن للاجتماع ربما يكون حكم لا يكون للأجزاء.

ثم اعلم : أنه خالف الشيخ في هذا الحكم ، وقال : الممكنة الصغرى مع المنعكسة السوالب تنتج ممكنة عامة ، إذا كانت الكبرى سالبة بالعكس، مثلا كل (ج) (ب) بالإمكان ، ولا شيء من (أ) (ب) ما دام (أ)»، فعكشنا الكبرى فصار لا شيء من (ب) (أ) ما دام (ب)»، وهو ينتج لا شيء من (ج) (أ) بالإمكان ؛ بناء على رأيه، إلا مع الضرورية ؛ فإنها تنتج ضرورية، وقال: إذا كانت الكبرى موجبة فلا ينتج ؛ لعدم جريان العكس، مثلا لا شيء من (ج) (ب) بالإمكان ، وكل (أ) (ب) ما دام (أ)»، وعكس الكبرى لكونه جزئية غير قابلة لكبروية الشكل الأول ، والصغرى غير قابلة للانعكاس، وكذا لا يجري فيه الخلف أيضا، كما يظهر بأدنى تأمل.
وهذا صحيح فيما عدا المشروطتين ؛ فإن نقيض النتيجة الضرورية مع إحداهما ينتج ضرورية منافية للصغرى، وأيضا في الخاصة منهما، بل وفي العرفية أيضا ينتج ضرورية لادائمة، أو دائمة لادائمة. ثم لعل ما قال الشيخ تحكم؛ فإن الدليل الذي أجرى في إنتاج الممكنة الصغرى في الشكل الأول جارٍ ههنا أيضا؛ لأنا لو فرضنا الصغرى مطلقة لصار المطلقة مع إحدى المنعكسة السوالب فيمكن النتيجة الفعلية، فيصدق الممكنة العامة بالفعل.
هذا، والله أعلم بالصواب.
( وَالنَّتِيجَةُ دَائِمَةٌ إِنْ كَانَ هُنَاكَ دَوَامٌ في إحدى المقدمتين، بأن تكون ضرورية أو دائمة عند المتأخرين، وعند الشيخ ضرورية إن كان هناك ضرورة،
The conclusion is verified then in our saying: 'Every donkey is ridden by Zayd possibly', and 'Nothing that brays is ridden by Zayd perpetually as long as the essence, or as long as it brays', and this depends on perpetuity lagging behind necessity in universals. As for the possible minor with the specific perpetual, according to the later scholars it does not produce. Some of them explained that non-perpetuity has no role in production because it agrees with the minor in quality, and the general perpetual is unproductive, as you know, and what is in it is not hidden; for the non-production of each of two parts individually does not entail non-production of the sum from the sum; for the combination perhaps has a ruling that the parts do not have. And some of them said: production is not known to be realized. According to the Sheikh, if the minor is affirmative possible and the major is negative specific perpetual, it produces a general possible. Al-Nasir al-Tusi demonstrated it by reduction: e.g., 'Every B is possibly C' and 'No A is C as long as A is not perpetual', then 'No B is possibly A'; otherwise some B is necessarily A, and its truth with the major is impossible, otherwise a non-perpetual necessary would follow. If this were complete, it would indicate its production as general absolute. The author of al-Matali' replied that the utmost that follows is 'No B is A', and the minor has no role in that because the major is independent in that; since the truth of the specific perpetual necessitates the negation of its subject from everything that actually differs from it, otherwise a perpetual non-perpetual would follow, and the meaning is that from the two premises each must have a role in that, so ponder it. Then know: He disagreed with the Sheikh in this ruling, and said: The possible minor with the converted negatives produces a general possible, if the major is negative by conversion, e.g. 'Every C is possibly B', and 'No A is B as long as A', so we converted the major and it became 'No B is A as long as B', and it produces 'No C is possibly A' based on his view, except with the necessary; for it produces necessary. And he said: If the major is affirmative, it does not produce, because conversion does not apply, e.g. 'No C is possibly B' and 'Every A is B as long as A', and converting the major because it is particular is not suitable as major of the first figure, and the minor is not convertible, and likewise reduction does not apply to it, as is clear with the slightest reflection. And this is correct except for the two conditional premises; for the contradictory of the necessary conclusion with one of them produces a necessary that contradicts the minor, and also in the specific of them, nay, in the perpetual also it produces a non-perpetual necessary, or a non-perpetual perpetual. Then perhaps what the Sheikh said is arbitrary; for the proof that was applied in the production of the possible minor in the first figure applies here as well; for if we suppose the minor absolute, then the absolute with one of the converted negatives would make the actual conclusion possible, so the general possible is verified actually. This, and Allah knows best what is correct. (And the conclusion is perpetual if there is perpetuity in one of the two premises, by being necessary or perpetual according to the later scholars, and according to the Sheikh it is necessary if there is necessity.
Source pages p. 523

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on mixed modal syllogisms (المختلطات) — syllogisms whose premises have different modalities. The author first discusses the case of a possible minor premise (موجبة ممكنة) with a specific perpetual major (سالبة عرفية خاصة). He reports that the later scholars (المتأخرون) deny that such a combination yields a conclusion. Some argue that 'non-perpetuity' (اللادوام) is irrelevant because it agrees with the quality of the minor, and since the general perpetual (العرفية العامة) is already unproductive, this combination is also unproductive. The author criticizes this reasoning, noting that the non-productivity of two parts individually does not entail non-productivity of their combination, since the combination may have a unique ruling. Others simply say that the productivity is unknown. In contrast, the Sheikh (Sirāj al-Dīn al-Urmawī) holds that this combination does produce a general possible (ممكنة عامة). Al-Ṭūsī demonstrates this by reduction (الخلف): assuming the minor 'Every B is possibly C' and the major 'No A is C as long as A', he deduces 'No B is possibly A'; otherwise a contradiction arises. The author of al-Maṭāliʿ (al-Urmawī himself) responds that the reduction only proves 'No B is A', not that it is possible, and that the minor is not actually needed because the major alone already negates the subject from everything different. The author then transitions to a new section (فصل في المختلطات) where he reports a different view: the possible minor with the converted negatives (المنعكسة السوالب) produces a general possible when the major is negative after conversion. He explains that if the major is affirmative, no conclusion follows because conversion would not yield a universal major for the first figure. The author then critiques the Sheikh's position as arbitrary, arguing that the same reduction used for the first figure applies here if the minor is taken as absolute. Finally, the author cites the matn: the conclusion is perpetual if there is perpetuity in one premise — necessary or perpetual according to later scholars, or necessary according to the Sheikh. Throughout, the passage shows the technical debates about modal syllogisms and the role of conversion and reductio.

Key Terms
الممكنة العامة
العرفية الخاصة
الخلف
العكس

According to al-Ṭūsī's reductio argument, what does a possible minor (كل ب ج بالإمكان) with a specific perpetual major (لا شيء من أ ج ما دام أ) produce?
A) A necessary universal conclusion
B) No conclusion at all
C) A general possible conclusion (لا شيء من ب أ بالإمكان)
D) An absolute perpetual conclusion

Key Takeaway
The reductio argument shows that a possible minor and a specific perpetual major can yield a possible conclusion, but the debate with al-Urmawī questions whether the conclusion involves the minor's contribution.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Construct a reductio argument for the following: minor premise 'Every substance is possibly non-eternal' (كل جوهر ممكن لا أزلي), major premise 'No accident is non-eternal perpetually' (لا شيء من العرض بلا أزلي ما دام هو). Determine, following al-Ṭūsī's method, whether a conclusion follows, and if so, what it is.

The Syllogism

Conversion in Mixed Modal Syllogisms: Possible Minor with Converted Perpetual Major
Application of conversion to mixed modal syllogisms to produce conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply conversion (الأكس) to a mixed modal syllogism with a possible minor and a perpetual major to determine if a conclusion follows, and identify when conversion fails.

Core Idea
Converting the major premise can rescue a mixed modal syllogism, allowing a possible conclusion, but only if the major is negative.

In the continuation of the passage, the author presents an alternative method for mixed modal syllogisms: using conversion to transform the perpetual major into a form suitable for the first figure. This approach, attributed to the Sheikh (al-Urmawī), produces a conclusion when the major is negative. We will explore the conversion operation and its limitations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original syllogism before conversion
Consider the premises: minor 'Every C is possibly B' (موجبة ممكنة), major 'No A is B as long as A' (سالبة عرفية خاصة). This does not directly yield a conclusion, as the later scholars hold.
The variables differ from the previous lesson: here the major is 'No A is B' instead of 'No A is C'.
2
Apply conversion to the major premise
Convert the major 'No A is B as long as A' to 'No B is A as long as B'. This is an immediate inference that preserves truth under specific conditions for perpetual propositions.
The conversion yields a universal negative, suitable as a major premise in the first figure.
3
Check the new syllogism after conversion
Now we have: major 'No B is A as long as B' (subject B, predicate A), minor 'Every C is possibly B' (subject C, predicate B). This is a first-figure syllogism with middle term B.
The first figure allows a conclusion from such premises.
4
Determine the conclusion
By the rules of the first figure with modalities, the conclusion is 'No C is possibly A' (ممكنة عامة سالبة). The author states it is produced based on the Sheikh's view, except when the major is necessary, then the conclusion is necessary.
The passage says 'وهو ينتج لا شيء من (ج) (أ) بالإمكان'.
5
Test when the major is affirmative
If the major is affirmative (e.g., 'Every A is B as long as A'), conversion fails because the major becomes particular ('Some B is A'), which cannot serve as a universal major in the first figure. Also, the minor may not be convertible, and reductio does not apply.
The author says 'إذا كانت الكبرى موجبة فلا ينتج'.

Commentary

The author first discusses the case of a possible minor premise (موجبة ممكنة) with a specific perpetual major (سالبة عرفية خاصة). Al-Ṭūsī demonstrates this by reduction (الخلف): assuming the minor 'Every B is possibly C' and the major 'No A is C as long as A', he deduces 'No B is possibly A'; otherwise a contradiction arises. The author of al-Maṭāliʿ (al-Urmawī himself) responds that the reduction only proves 'No B is A', not that it is possible, and that the minor is not actually needed because the major alone already negates the subject from everything different. The author then transitions to a new section (فصل في المختلطات) where he reports a different view: the possible minor with the converted negatives (المنعكسة السوالب) produces a general possible when the major is negative after conversion. He explains that if the major is affirmative, no conclusion follows because conversion would not yield a universal major for the first figure. The author then critiques the Sheikh's position as arbitrary, arguing that the same reduction used for the first figure applies here if the minor is taken as absolute. Finally, the author cites the matn: the conclusion is perpetual if there is perpetuity in one premise — necessary or perpetual according to later scholars, or necessary according to the Sheikh. Throughout, the passage shows the technical debates about modal syllogisms and the role of conversion and reductio.

Key Terms
الممكنة العامة
العرفية الخاصة
العكس
الخلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the conversion method for mixed modal syllogisms, what must be the quality of the major premise for a conclusion to follow?
A) It must be affirmative
B) It must be negative
C) It must be necessary
D) It must be perpetual

Key Takeaway
Conversion can transform a mixed modal syllogism into a valid first-figure inference, but only when the major premise is negative; affirmative majors render conversion ineffective for producing a conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the syllogism: minor 'Every intoxicant is possibly prohibited' (كل مسكر ممنوع بالإمكان), major 'No wine is prohibited perpetually' (لا شيء من الخمر بممنوع ما دام الخمر). Apply conversion to the major and determine whether a conclusion follows. If so, state it.
?
Why does the author say that when the major is affirmative (e.g., 'Every A is B perpetually'), conversion does not yield a universal major? Explain using the condition for conversion of affirmative A propositions.

Critique of al-Shaykh on Mixed Modal Syllogisms
Author criticizes al-Shaykh's ruling on mumkina sughra with mun'akisa sawalib

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which a mixed modal syllogism with a possible minor and converted negative major yields a valid conclusion, and evaluate the disagreement between the author and al-Shaykh.

Core Idea
Not all mixed modal syllogisms are valid; the permissibility of conversion determines the result.

In this lesson, we examine a critical discussion on mixed modal syllogisms. The author challenges al-Shaykh's ruling that a possible minor with converted negative major produces a general possible conclusion, except when the major is necessary. We will dissect the example and the conditions for validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and their modal qualifiers
In the example, the minor is 'Every J is B possibly' (mumkina) and the major is 'No A is B as long as it is A' (a particular conditional, 'urfiyya khassa). Note that the major is a conditional with a temporal qualifier.
The major is a conditional proposition, not a simple categorical.
2
Convert the major premise
Apply conversion to the major: from 'No A is B as long as it is A' to 'No B is A as long as it is B'. Check if this conversion is valid according to the rules of conditional propositions.
Conversion of conditionals is not always straightforward; here it is permissible because the conditional is universal negative.
3
Determine the conclusion according to al-Shaykh
Al-Shaykh held that with the converted major, the conclusion is 'No J is A possibly' (possible general), unless the major is necessary, in which case the conclusion is necessary.
This is the ruling being critiqued.
4
Examine the author's objection
The author argues that this ruling is arbitrary because if the minor is taken as absolute, the same proof used in the first figure applies. He also notes exceptions when both premises are conditioned (musharrata).
The author suggests the ruling is not universally applicable.
5
Evaluate the validity of the syllogism
Consider whether the conversion is permissible and whether the conclusion follows necessarily. The author suggests that the general possible conclusion holds only under certain conditions, and al-Shaykh's view may be incomplete.
This step requires modal reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في المختلطات
صدق العرفية الخاصة مستلزم لسلب موضوعه عن كل ما يغايره بالفعل، وإلا لزم دائمة لا دائمة، ومعنى أن يحصل من المقدمتين أن يكون لكل منهما دخل في ذلك، فتأمل فيه.
ثم اعلم : : أنه خالف الشيخ في هذا الحكم، وقال: الممكنة الصغرى مع المنعكسة السوالب تنتج ممكنة عامة، إذا كانت الكبرى سالبة بالعكس، مثلا كل (ج) (ب) بالإمكان، ولا شيء من (أ) (ب) ما دام (أ)»، فعكشنا الكبرى فصار لا شيء من (ب) (أ) ما دام (ب)»، وهو ينتج لا شيء من (ج) (أ) بالإمكان؛ بناء على رأيه، إلا مع الضرورية؛ فإنها تنتج ضرورية، وقال: إذا كانت الكبرى موجبة فلا ينتج؛ لعدم جريان العكس، مثلا لا شيء من (ج) (ب) بالإمكان، وكل (أ) (ب) ما دام (أ)»، وعكس الكبرى لكونه جزئية غير قابلة لكبروية الشكل الأول، والصغرى غير قابلة للانعكاس، وكذا لا يجري فيه الخلف أيضا، كما يظهر بأدنى تأمل.
وهذا صحيح فيما عدا المشروطتين؛ فإن نقيض النتيجة الضرورية مع إحداهما ينتج ضرورية منافية للصغرى، وأيضا في الخاصة منهما، بل وفي العرفية أيضا ينتج ضرورية لادائمة، أو دائمة لادائمة. ثم لعل ما قال الشيخ تحكم؛ فإن الدليل الذي أجرى في إنتاج الممكنة الصغرى في الشكل الأول جارٍ ههنا أيضا؛ لأنا لو فرضنا الصغرى مطلقة لصار المطلقة مع إحدى المنعكسة السوالب فيمكن النتيجة الفعلية، فيصدق الممكنة العامة بالفعل.
هذا، والله أعلم بالصواب.
Chapter on mixed [syllogisms]. The truth of the particular conditional [urfiyya khassa] necessitates the negation of its subject from everything that differs from it in actuality, otherwise a permanent non-permanent would be necessary, and its meaning is that from the two premises each has an input in that, so ponder it. Then know: that he disagreed with al-Shaykh in this ruling, and said: the possible minor with the converted negatives produces a general possible, if the major is negative by conversion, for example: every J is B possibly, and no A is B as long as it is A, so we converted the major and it became no B is A as long as it is B, and it produces no J is A possibly, based on his view, except with the necessary; for it produces necessary. And he said: if the major is affirmative, it does not produce, because conversion does not apply, for example: no J is B possibly, and every A is B as long as it is A, and the conversion of the major because it is particular is not suitable for being major in the first figure, and the minor is not suitable for conversion, and likewise the reductio does not apply here either, as appears with minimal reflection. This is correct except for the two conditioned [premises]; for the contradictory of the necessary conclusion with one of them produces a necessary contrary to the minor, and also in the particular of them, and even in the conditional it produces a necessary non-permanent, or a permanent non-permanent. Then perhaps what al-Shaykh said is arbitrary; for the proof that was conducted in producing the possible minor in the first figure is also valid here; for if we assume the minor absolute, then the absolute with one of the converted negatives makes the actual result possible, so the general possible is true in actuality. This, and Allah knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 524

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of a specific ruling in mixed modal syllogisms. The author begins by stating that the truth of a 'urfiyya khassa (a particular conditional statement with a temporal qualification) necessitates that its subject be separated from everything that actually differs from it, otherwise a contradiction would arise. Then he introduces a disagreement with al-Shaykh (likely Ibn Sina or Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) regarding the combination of a possible minor premise and a converted negative major premise. According to al-Shaykh, this combination yields a general possible conclusion when the major is negative after conversion, except if the major is necessary, in which case the conclusion is necessary. An example is given with variables J, B, A: 'Every J is B possibly' and 'No A is B as long as it is A' converts to 'No B is A as long as it is B', leading to 'No J is A possibly'. If the major is affirmative, no conclusion follows because conversion does not work properly. The author then notes that this is correct except for the cases when the premises are 'conditioned' (musharrata), where the contradictory of the necessary conclusion produces a necessary contrary to the minor. He criticizes al-Shaykh's view as arbitrary, arguing that the same proof used in the first figure for possible minor premises applies here as well: if we take the minor as absolute, then the absolute with one of the converted negatives can produce an actual result, making the general possible true. The passage ends with a note that Allah knows best.

الممكنة
المنعكسة السوالب
الشيخ
الضرورية

Key Takeaway
The validity of mixed modal syllogisms depends on the permissibility of conversion and the modal interaction; al-Shaykh's ruling is not universally accepted.

The Rule for Permanent Conclusion in Mixed Modal Syllogisms
Rule for conclusion modality in mixed modal syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the modality of the conclusion in a mixed modal syllogism when one premise is permanent or necessary, and identify when the existence qualifier is removed.

Core Idea
Permanent premise yields permanent conclusion; otherwise conclusion mirrors minor minus existence qualifier.

In mixed modal syllogisms, the modality of the conclusion is not arbitrary. The classical rule states that if one premise is permanent or necessary, the conclusion becomes permanent. But what exactly counts as permanent? And what happens if both premises are non-permanent? This passage from the commentary on al-Sullam al-'Ulum explains the rule and its nuances, including the removal of the existence qualifier when no premise is permanent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion rule
Find the statement: 'والنتيجة دائمة إن كان هناك دوام في إحدى المقدمتين'. Understand that if one premise is permanent (including necessary), the conclusion is permanent.
2
Distinguish 'permanent' and 'necessary'
Note that for later scholars, 'permanent' includes necessary, but for al-Shaykh, it depends: if necessity exists, conclusion is necessary; if permanence, conclusion is permanent.
3
Handle the case without permanence
When neither premise is permanent, the conclusion is like the minor premise but with the condition of existence removed. Why? Because existence qualifier matches the other premise and doesn't affect conversion.
4
Apply to conditioned propositions
If the minor is conditioned (musharrata), the conclusion is conditional ('urfiyya). This shows how the rule extends to different modal types.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالنَّتِيجَةُ دَائِمَةٌ إِنْ كَانَ هُنَاكَ دَوَامٌ في إحدى المقدمتين، بأن تكون ضرورية أو دائمة عند المتأخرين، وعند الشيخ ضرورية إن كان هناك ضرورة، أو دائمة إن كان دوام. (وَإِلَّا)، أي وإن لم يكن في إحدى المقدمتين دوام (فَكَالصُّغْرَى)، أي فالنتيجة كالصغرى، (مَحْذُوفًا عَنْهَا قَيْدُ الْوُجُودِ)؛ لكونه موافقا للمقدمة الأخرى في الكيف، ولقيد الوجود فيها في عدم الانعكاس، فلا دخل له في الإنتاج، فتأمل، (وَالضَّرُورَةِ)، فإذا كان الصغرى مشروطة تكون النتيجة عرفية .
(وَفِيهِ مَا فِيهِ)؛ فإنا بينا أن السالبة الضرورية تنعكس كنفسها، وكذا المشروطة، فحينئذ تكون ضرورية مع الضرورية، ولا يحذف قيد الضرورة، إذا كان الكبرى مشروطة، كذا في الحاشية. وهذا إنما يجري فيما إذا كان الكبرى سالبة، وأما إذا كانت موجبة فلا تنعكس إلا جزئية غير قابلة لكبروية الشكل الأول، فالأولى أن يحال على إنتاج الممكنة والحينية الممكنة في صغرى الشكل الأول، ويستدل بالخلف.
والتحقيق: أن الضرورية إن أخذتْ بالمعنى الأعم فينتج ضرورية، وإلا فدائمة؛ إذ لا يصدق في قولنا لا شيء من الحمار بفرس بالضرورة، وكل مرکوب زید فرس بالضرورة» ينتج لا شيء من الحمار بمركوب زيد بالضرورة . وأما المشروطة فقد ظهر لك أنها لا تنعكس كنفسها، سواء أخذت الضرورة أعم أو أخص .
والسِّرُّ في إنتاج هذا الشكل كما ذكر عند وجود هذه الشرائط، أما إذا كانت الصغرى دائمة المطلقة العامة، أو ضرورية مع الممكنة فإن الكبرى مع حاكمة بأن نسبة الأوسط إلى الأكبر بالإطلاق أو بالإمكان، والصغرى قد حكمت بأن نسبة الأوسط إلى الأصغر منافية لها، فيجب أن يكون ذات الأصغر غير ذات الأكبر، كيف، وإن اتحدتا يلزم اجتماع المتنافيين، فيجب
And the conclusion is permanent if there is permanence in one of the two premises, that is, if it is necessary or permanent according to the later [scholars], and according to al-Shaykh it is necessary if there is necessity, or permanent if there is permanence. (And if not), i.e., if there is no permanence in one of the two premises, (then it is like the minor), i.e., the conclusion is like the minor, (with the condition of existence removed from it); because it is in agreement with the other premise in quality, and with the condition of existence in it in non-conversion, so it has no input in production, so ponder, (and [with the condition of] necessity); so if the minor is conditioned, the conclusion is conditional. (And in it there is what there is); for we have shown that the necessary negative reflects as itself, and likewise the conditioned, so then it is necessary with the necessary, and the condition of necessity is not removed, if the major is conditioned, as in the gloss. And this only applies when the major is negative; but if it is affirmative, it does not reflect except as a particular, not suitable for being major in the first figure, so it is better to refer to the production of the possible and the temporal possible in the minor of the first figure, and be guided by reductio. And the verification: if the necessary is taken in the more general sense, it produces necessary, otherwise permanent; for it is not true in our saying 'No donkey is a horse necessarily, and every Zayd's mount is a horse necessarily' produces 'No donkey is Zayd's mount necessarily'. As for the conditioned, it has become clear to you that it does not reflect as itself, whether the necessity is taken general or specific. And the secret of producing this figure as mentioned when these conditions exist: if the minor is permanent absolute general, or necessary with the possible, then the major judges that the relation of the middle to the larger is absolute or possible, and the minor has judged that the relation of the middle to the smaller is incompatible with it, so it is necessary that the entity of the smaller is not the entity of the larger; how, if they were united, the two incompatibles would combine, so it is necessary.
Source pages p. 525

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn line stating the rule for the conclusion in mixed modal syllogisms: if one premise is permanent (da'ima) or necessary (daruriyya), the conclusion is permanent; otherwise it is like the minor premise but with the conditions of existence and necessity removed. The author explains that the condition of existence is removed because it agrees with the other premise in quality and in non-conversion, so it does not affect the production. He then critiques this view (وَفِيهِ مَا فِيهِ) by pointing out that necessary negatives and conditioned propositions reflect as themselves, so when the major is negative and necessary, the conclusion should be necessary without removing the necessity condition. However, this only works for negative majors; for affirmative majors, conversion only yields a particular that cannot serve as major in the first figure, so one must rely on reductio. The verification (التحقيق) is given: if 'necessary' is taken in the broader sense (including permanent), it produces necessary; otherwise it produces permanent. An example is provided: 'No donkey is a horse necessarily' and 'Every Zayd's mount is a horse necessarily' yields 'No donkey is Zayd's mount necessarily'. The conditioned proposition does not reflect as itself regardless. Finally, the secret (السر) of production is explained: when the minor is permanent or necessary and the major is absolute or possible, the major's judgment of the relation between middle and larger is absolute or possible, while the minor's judgment of the relation between middle and smaller is incompatible, so the smaller must differ from the larger, otherwise contradictions would occur.

Key Terms
دائمة
ضرورية
مشروطة
عرفية

A) It becomes possible
B) It becomes like the minor with existence qualifier removed
C) It becomes necessary
D) It becomes conditional

Key Takeaway
In mixed modal syllogisms, a permanent premise forces the conclusion to be permanent; without permanence, the conclusion mirrors the minor but drops the existence qualifier.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the Kalam argument: 'The world is temporally originated (ḥādith). Everything temporally originated has a Creator (wājib).' Identify which premise is permanent (necessary). Then state the modality of the conclusion according to the rule.

Refining the Rule: Objections, Verification, and the Secret of Production
Critical refinement of the modal conclusion rule

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the refined rule for modal conclusions by explaining the objection regarding conversion of necessary negatives and the verification that distinguishes broad and strict necessity.

Core Idea
Necessary negatives reflect as themselves, refining when necessity is retained; broad vs. strict necessity determines the conclusion's modality.

The simple rule is not without controversy. The commentator points out that necessary negatives and conditioned propositions convert as themselves, so when the major is negative and necessary, the conclusion should retain necessity. But for affirmative majors, conversion fails, requiring reductio. The verification (التحقيق) resolves the confusion by distinguishing between the broad and strict senses of necessity, and provides an example. Finally, the secret of production explains the logical mechanism when premises have different modalities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find the phrase 'وَفِيهِ مَا فِيهِ' and understand that the commentator is critiquing the removal of the necessity condition when the major is negative.
2
Understand the objection's basis
The objection relies on the fact that necessary negatives (السالبة الضرورية) and conditioned propositions (المشروطة) reflect as themselves. Therefore, when the major is negative and necessary, the conclusion should also be necessary, not just permanent or with the condition removed.
3
Note the limitation
This only works when the major is negative. If the major is affirmative, conversion yields only a particular, which cannot serve as major in the first figure. Then one must resort to reductio or use production of possible and temporal possible in the minor.
4
Study the verification
The verification (التحقيق) distinguishes between 'necessary' in the broad sense (including permanent) and strict sense. If broad, conclusion is necessary; if strict, conclusion is permanent. An example is given: 'No donkey is a horse necessarily' and 'Every Zayd's mount is a horse necessarily' yields a necessary conclusion.
5
Understand the secret of production
When the minor is permanent/necessary and the major is absolute/possible, the middle term's relation to the larger is absolute or possible, while the minor's judgment of the middle's relation to the smaller is incompatible, forcing the entities to differ. If they were the same, contradictions would arise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the conclusion is permanent if there is permanence in one of the two premises, that is, if it is necessary or permanent according to the later [scholars], and according to al-Shaykh it is necessary if there is necessity, or permanent if there is permanence. (And if not), i.e., if there is no permanence in one of the two premises, (then it is like the minor), i.e., the conclusion is like the minor, (with the condition of existence removed from it); because it is in agreement with the other premise in quality, and with the condition of existence in it in non-conversion, so it has no input in production, so ponder, (and [with the condition of] necessity); so if the minor is conditioned, the conclusion is conditional. Source pages p. 525

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn line stating the rule for the conclusion in mixed modal syllogisms: if one premise is permanent (da'ima) or necessary (daruriyya), the conclusion is permanent; otherwise it is like the minor premise but with the conditions of existence and necessity removed. The author explains that the condition of existence is removed because it agrees with the other premise in quality and in non-conversion, so it does not affect the production. He then critiques this view (وَفِيهِ مَا فِيهِ) by pointing out that necessary negatives and conditioned propositions reflect as themselves, so when the major is negative and necessary, the conclusion should be necessary without removing the necessity condition. However, this only works for negative majors; for affirmative majors, conversion only yields a particular that cannot serve as major in the first figure, so one must rely on reductio. The verification (التحقيق) is given: if 'necessary' is taken in the broader sense (including permanent), it produces necessary; otherwise it produces permanent. An example is provided: 'No donkey is a horse necessarily' and 'Every Zayd's mount is a horse necessarily' yields 'No donkey is Zayd's mount necessarily'. The conditioned proposition does not reflect as itself regardless. Finally, the secret (السر) of production is explained: when the minor is permanent or necessary and the major is absolute or possible, the major's judgment of the relation between middle and larger is absolute or possible, while the minor's judgment of the relation between middle and smaller is incompatible, so the smaller must differ from the larger, otherwise contradictions would occur.

Key Terms

المشروطة
الحينية الممكنة
الضرورية بالمعنى الأعم
دائمة المطلقة العامة

A) Permanent
B) Necessary
C) Possible
D) Conditional

Key Takeaway
The verification resolves the modal rule: broad necessity yields necessary conclusion; strict necessity yields permanent; conditioned propositions do not reflect as themselves.

Modal Conditions in First Figure Syllogisms
Explains removal of modality qualifiers in first figure conclusions and critiques a view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the conclusion's modality in a first figure categorical syllogism given the modalities of the premises, according to the rules and corrections discussed.

Core Idea
Necessity in the conclusion does not automatically follow from modality conflicts; perpetuity is the default.

In classical Islamic logic, the rules for syllogisms become more intricate when premises carry different modalities—perpetuity, necessity, possibility, or conditionality. This passage from Bahr al-'Ulum examines how the qualifiers are removed or retained in the first figure, and corrects a standard view. By working through its reasoning, you will learn to handle modal syllogisms with precision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the standard rule for modality removal
The passage states that if neither premise is perpetual, the conclusion is like the minor premise with the qualifier of existence removed. This is the baseline rule for the first figure.
This rule is the starting point; exceptions follow.
2
Examine the exception for necessity and the debate
The author discusses a critique: earlier commentators argued that necessary negatives convert as themselves, so necessity should not be removed when the major is conditional. Understand why this only applies when the major is negative.
If the major is affirmative, it converts only as a particular, which cannot serve as a major in the first figure.
3
Work through the verification example
The author gives an example: from 'No donkey is a horse by necessity' and 'Every ridden animal of Zayd is a horse by necessity', the conclusion is 'No donkey is a ridden animal of Zayd by necessity'. Identify the premises and conclusion.
This shows that when necessity is taken broadly, the conclusion is necessary; otherwise, perpetual.
4
Understand the 'secret' of production
When a perpetual or necessary premise is combined with a possible premise, the relations are incompatible, forcing a perpetual denial. But the necessity of that denial is an extra condition not guaranteed by the incompatibility.
This explains why necessity does not automatically follow from a modal conflict.
5
Apply the distinction to an Islamic legal example
Consider a fiqh example: 'All intoxicants are forbidden by necessity' and 'This substance is an intoxicant possibly'. The conclusion 'This substance is forbidden' is perpetual, not necessary, because the possibility of the minor adds uncertainty.
This illustrates the practical implication of the modal rules.

(وَفِيهِ مَا فِيهِ) ؛ فإنا بينا أن السالبة الضرورية تنعكس كنفسها، وكذا المشروطة، فحينئذ تكون ضرورية مع الضرورية، ولا يحذف قيد الضرورة، إذا كان الكبرى مشروطة، كذا في الحاشية». وهذا إنما يجري فيما إذا كان الكبرى سالبة ، وأما إذا كانت موجبة فلا تنعكس إلا جزئية غير قابلة لكبروية الشكل الأول، فالأولى أن يحال على إنتاج الممكنة والحينية الممكنة في صغرى الشكل الأول ، ويستدل بالخلف. والتحقيق: أن الضرورية إن أخذتْ بالمعنى الأعم فينتج ضرورية، وإلا فدائمة ; إذ لا يصدق في قولنا لا شيء من الحمار بفرس بالضرورة، وكل مرکوب زید فرس بالضرورة» ينتج لا شيء من الحمار بمركوب زيد بالضرورة . وأما المشروطة فقد ظهر لك أنها لا تنعكس كنفسها، سواء أخذت الضرورة أعم أو أخص . والسِّرُّ في إنتاج هذا الشكل كما ذكر عند وجود هذه الشرائط ، أما إذا كانت الصغرى دائمة المطلقة العامة ، أو ضرورية مع الممكنة فإن الكبرى مع حاكمة بأن نسبة الأوسط إلى الأكبر بالإطلاق أو بالإمكان، والصغرى قد حكمت بأن نسبة الأوسط إلى الأصغر منافية لها، فيجب أن يكون ذات الأصغر غير ذات الأكبر، كيف، وإن اتحدتا يلزم اجتماع المتنافيين ، فيجب أن يسلب الأكبر عن الأصغر دائما، وأما أن ذلك السلب ضروري فأمر زائد لا يعطيه هذا التنافي، وقس عليه عكس ذلك.
Or perpetual if there is perpetuity. (And if not), i.e., if perpetuity is not in one of the two premises, (then the conclusion is like the minor premise), i.e., the conclusion is like the minor premise, (with the qualifier of existence removed from it); because it agrees with the other premise in quality, and with the qualifier of existence in it in terms of non-conversion, so it has no role in production, so ponder. (And the qualifier of necessity). For example, if the minor premise is conditional, the conclusion is conventional. There is some debate about it; for we have shown that the necessary negative converts as itself, likewise the conditional, so then it becomes necessary with necessity, and the qualifier of necessity is not removed if the major premise is conditional, as in the gloss. And this applies only when the major premise is negative; as for when it is affirmative, it converts only as a particular that cannot serve as a major in the first figure, so it is better to refer to the production of the possible and the possible temporal in the minor of the first figure, and to argue by reduction. The verification: if necessity is taken in the broader sense, it produces a necessary conclusion; otherwise, a perpetual one; for it is not true in our statement 'Nothing of the donkey is a horse by necessity' and 'Every ridden animal of Zayd is a horse by necessity' – it produces 'Nothing of the donkey is a ridden animal of Zayd by necessity'. As for the conditional, it has become clear to you that it does not convert as itself, whether necessity is taken as broader or more specific. The secret of production in this figure is as mentioned when these conditions exist. For example, if the minor is the general absolute perpetual, or necessary with the possible, then the major judges that the relation of the middle to the major is by absoluteness or possibility, while the minor has judged that the relation of the middle to the minor is incompatible with it, so the essence of the minor must be other than the essence of the major; indeed, if they were identical, the two incompatibles would coexist. Therefore, the major must be denied of the minor perpetually. That this denial is necessary is an extra matter that this incompatibility does not provide; and analogously for the opposite.
Source pages p. 525

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the rules of the first figure of categorical syllogism when premises have different modalities (perpetual, necessary, possible, conditional). The author begins by stating the standard rule: if neither premise is perpetual, the conclusion is like the minor premise with the qualifier of existence removed, because it matches the other premise in quantity and has no role in production. Then he notes an exception regarding the qualifier of necessity, and discusses a critique: previous commentators argued that the necessary negative converts as itself, so when the major is conditional, the qualifier of necessity should not be removed. However, the author restricts this to when the major is negative; if affirmative, it converts only as particular, which cannot serve as a major in the first figure. He suggests using reduction to first figure with possible premises. The author then gives his own verification: if 'necessary' is taken broadly, the conclusion is necessary; otherwise perpetual. He gives an example: from 'Nothing of the donkey is a horse by necessity' and 'Every ridden animal of Zayd is a horse by necessity', it follows that 'Nothing of the donkey is a ridden animal of Zayd by necessity'. He notes the conditional does not convert as itself. Finally, he explains the 'secret' underlying the production: when a perpetual or necessary premise is combined with a possible premise, the middle term's relation to the major as absolute or possible conflicts with its relation to the minor as opposite, so the essences must be different, leading to a perpetual denial of the major of the minor. However, that this denial is necessary is an additional claim not guaranteed by the mere incompatibility. The passage demonstrates a meticulous approach to modal logic, correcting overly simplistic rules.

Key Takeaway
In modal syllogisms, the conclusion's modality is not simply the weaker of the premises; the specific interaction of qualifiers determines whether necessity or perpetuity follows.

Mixed Modal Syllogisms: Rule for Production in the First Figure
Examines mixed premises combining actual and descriptive modalities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the modality of the conclusion in a mixed modal syllogism of the first figure by applying the rule of incompatibility of relations between the middle term's relation to the major's description and to the minor's essence.

Core Idea
The conclusion's modality mirrors the major premise's relational modality.

This passage delves into the intricate details of mixed modal syllogisms, specifically when one premise is actual and the other descriptive, or possible with conditional. The author establishes the key rule: the modality of the conclusion—whether perpetual, temporal, or otherwise—is determined by the relation of the middle to the major's description in the major premise. Understanding this rule is crucial for correctly evaluating syllogisms that combine different modal strengths, a common occurrence in Islamic theological arguments where premises may be drawn from revelation (certain) or empirical observation (contingent).

Guided Path
1
Identify premise types and modalities
Determine which premise is actual (fi'liyya, unmodalized) and which is descriptive (wasfiyya, modalized regarding the subject's description) or possible/conditional. For example, the minor may be actual and the major descriptive.
The minor premise is usually actual in these mixed cases.
2
Analyze the major premise's relational judgment
In the major premise, the middle term's description is related to the major term's description with a specific modality (e.g., perpetual or necessary). Recognize this as the 'relational modality' that governs the conclusion.
If the major is descriptive, it judges that the middle's description relates to the major's description by perpetuity or necessity.
3
Check incompatibility with the minor's essence
The relational modality of the major premise is incompatible with the relation of the middle's description to the essence (dhat) of the minor term. This incompatibility means the middle cannot simultaneously relate to both the major's description and the minor's essence under that modality.
This is the key logical opposition (munafat) that drives the conclusion.
4
Determine the separation modality
Because of the incompatibility, the description of the major term must be separate (munfakk) from the essence of the minor term with the same modality as the major's relational modality. If the major's relation is perpetual, the separation is perpetual; if temporal, then temporal.
The conclusion thus denies the major's description of the minor with that modality.
5
Handle non-perpetual cases and extract the general rule
If the major's relation is not perpetual (i.e., limited), the separation is not perpetual either. The author summarizes two types of incompatibility: (a) between the relation in the major to the major's essence and the relation in the minor to the minor's essence, indicating different essences and yielding a perpetual denial; (b) between the descriptive relation of the middle to the major's description and its relation to the minor's essence, showing the major's description does not apply to the minor's essence with that modality.
This formalizes the rule for production in first-figure mixed modal syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في المختلطات. وأما إذا كان الصغرى الفعلية غيرهما مع إحدى الوصفيات أو الممكنة مع إحدى المشروطتين فإن الكبرى حاكمة بأن نسبة وصف الأوسط إلى وصف الأكبر بالدوام، على الشق الأول ، وبالضرورة، على الثاني، وبالجملة منافية لنسبته إلى ذات الأصغر ، فلا بد أن يكون وصف الأكبر منفكا عن ذات الأصغر بالجهة، التي نسب بها الأوسط إليها، إن دائما فدائما، وإن وقتا فوقتا ، أما إن كان لا دائما لا يكون انفكاك الأكبر عنه لادائما؛ لأن لادوام نسبة وصف الأوسط إليها غير مناف لنسبته إلى وصف الأكبر، وكذا انفكاك عن ذات الأصغر ، لا يلزم أن يكون ضروريا؛ فإن غاية ما لزم من انتفائها إمكان صدق وصف الأكبر عليها، ولا يلزم منه إمكان انتساب وصف الأوسط إليها، بالنسبة الموجهة التي انتسب بها إلى وصف الأكبر؛ فإن هذه لما صدق عليه الأكبر. وأيضا إمكان الملزوم لا يستلزم إمكان الأكبر النسبة إنما هي اللازم، إذا كان محالا بالغير، كما مر غير مرةٍ. ولعلك تنبهت من ههنا أن ضابطة إنتاج هذا الشكل أحد الأمرين: إما منافاة النسبة المتحققة في الكبرى إلى ذات الأكبر للنسبة المتحققة في الصغرى إلى ذات الأصغر؛ ليدل على مغايرة الذاتين، ويلزم دوام سلب الأكبر عما يصدق عليه الأصغر، وإما منافاة نسبة وصف الأوسط إلى وصف الأكبر التي تضمنها الكبرى الوصفية لنسبته إلى ذات الأصغر ; ليدل على عدم صدق وصف الأكبر على ذات الأصغر بالجهة التي انتسب بها إليها الأوسط، لا كما ظن التفتازاني أن منافاة نسبة وصف الأوسط إلى وصف الأكبر لنسبته إلى ذات الأصغر.
Section on Mixed Moods. As for when the minor premise is actual and the other is one of the descriptive [premises], or when the possible [premise] is with one of the two conditional [premises]: then the major premise judges that the relation of the description of the middle to the description of the major is perpetual, on the first branch, and by necessity, on the second; and in general, it is incompatible with its relation to the essence of the minor. Therefore, the description of the major must be separate from the essence of the minor according to the modality by which the middle was related to it; if [the relation was] perpetual, then perpetually; if temporal, then temporally. But if it is [the relation is] not perpetual, the separation of the major from it will not be not-perpetual; because the non-perpetuity of the relation of the description of the middle to it is not incompatible with its relation to the description of the major. Similarly, separation from the essence of the minor does not necessitate that it be necessary; for the utmost that follows from its absence is the possibility of the description of the major applying to it, and from that does not follow the possibility of the description of the middle applying to it with the modal relation by which it was related to the description of the major; for this [description] is applied to the major when it is true. Also, the possibility of the antecedent does not entail the possibility of the major; the relation is entailed only if the impossible is something else, as has been mentioned many times. Perhaps you have realized from here that the rule for production in this figure is one of two things: either the incompatibility of the relation that is actualized in the major to the essence of the major with the relation actualized in the minor to the essence of the minor – to indicate the difference of the two essences, and it necessitates the perpetual denial of the major from that of which the minor is true; or the incompatibility of the relation of the description of the middle to the description of the major which the descriptive major includes with its relation to the essence of the minor – to indicate that the description of the major is not true of the essence of the minor with the modality by which the middle was related to it, not as al-Taftazani supposed: that the incompatibility of the relation of the description of the middle to the description of the major with its relation to the essence of the minor.
Source pages p. 526

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of mixed modal syllogisms in the first figure. The author begins by defining the scope: when one premise is actual ('fi'liyya', i.e., a simple assertoric proposition) and the other is descriptive ('wasfiyya', i.e., a modal proposition about the subject's description), or when a possible premise is combined with a conditional premise. In such cases, the major premise judges that the relation of the description of the middle term to the description of the major term is either perpetual (if the descriptive premise is a perpetual) or necessary (if it is a conditional). This relation is incompatible with the relation of the middle's description to the essence (dhat) of the minor term. Consequently, the description of the major must be separated from the essence of the minor with the same modality as the middle's relation: if perpetual, then perpetually; if temporal, then temporally. However, if the relation is not perpetual (i.e., not always), the separation is not perpetual. The reason is that the non-perpetuity of the middle's relation to the minor is not incompatible with its relation to the major's description. Moreover, separation from the essence of the minor does not entail necessity; the negation of the relation only implies that it is possible that the major's description applies to the minor, but not that the middle's description applies with the same modal relation. The author emphasizes that possibility of the antecedent (the middle's description applying) does not entail possibility of the consequent (the major's description with that modality). He then summarizes the rule for production in this figure as two possibilities: (1) incompatibility of the relation in the major (to the major term's essence) with that in the minor (to the minor term's essence), which requires the essences to be different and thus a perpetual denial; (2) incompatibility of the descriptive major's relation of the middle's description to the major's description with its relation to the minor's essence, which shows that the major's description does not apply to the minor's essence with that modality. The author contrasts his view with that of al-Taftazani, who had a different understanding of the incompatibility.

وصفية
منافاة
انفكاك

Key Takeaway
In mixed modal syllogisms of the first figure, the conclusion denies the major's description of the minor with the same modality as the major premise's relational judgment.
