Mixed Syllogisms and the Foundation for the Third Figure Rule
Explains how modal relations create repugnance, setting up the third figure rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two conditions of repugnance that determine the productivity of the third figure in mixed syllogisms.

Core Idea
The third figure's rule is one of two possible repugnances, not just one.

This passage explains the foundation for the third figure's productive rule in mixed syllogisms, showing how the mode of the middle term's relation determines the conclusion's modality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mixed syllogism scenario
The author discusses cases where the minor premise is actual (fi'liyya) and the major is descriptive (wasfiyya), or where the minor is possible (mumkina) and the major conditional (mashruta). Recognize the modal combination.
The major premise rules the relation of the middle's attribute to the major's attribute.
2
Understand the repugnance relation
The relation of the middle to the major's attribute is repugnant (munafi) to the middle's relation to the minor's essence. This repugnance forces the major's attribute to be separate from the minor's essence in the same mode (permanent or temporary).
If the relation is not permanent, the separation is not permanent either.
3
Trace the logical consequence
The separation does not imply necessity; only possibility. The possibility of the major's truth does not entail possibility of the middle's attribution to the minor in the same modal relation.
This is because the middle's attribution to the major is a necessary consequent, not a logical equivalence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في المختلطات
أن يسلب الأكبر عن الأصغر دائما، وأما أن ذلك السلب ضروري فأمر زائد لا يعطيه هذا التنافي، وقس عليه عكس ذلك.
وأما إذا كان الصغرى الفعلية غيرهما مع إحدى الوصفيات أو الممكنة مع إحدى المشروطتين فإن الكبرى حاكمة بأن نسبة وصف الأوسط إلى وصف الأكبر بالدوام، على الشق الأول ، وبالضرورة، على الثاني، وبالجملة منافية لنسبته إلى ذات الأصغر ، فلا بد أن يكون وصف الأكبر منفكا عن ذات الأصغر بالجهة، التي نسب بها الأوسط إليها، إن دائما فدائما، وإن وقتا فوقتا ، أما إن كان لا دائما لا يكون انفكاك الأكبر عنه لادائما ؛ لأن لادوام نسبة وصف الأوسط إليها غير مناف لنسبته إلى وصف الأكبر، وكذا انفكاك عن ذات الأصغر ، لا يلزم أن يكون ضروريا؛ فإن غاية ما لزم من انتفائها إمكان صدق وصف الأكبر عليها، ولا يلزم منه إمكان انتساب وصف الأوسط إليها، بالنسبة الموجهة التي انتسب بها إلى وصف الأكبر؛ فإن هذه لما صدق عليه الأكبر. وأيضا إمكان الملزوم لا يستلزم إمكان الأكبر النسبة إنما هي اللازم، إذا كان محالا بالغير، كما مر غير مرةٍ.
ولعلك تنبهت من ههنا أن ضابطة إنتاج هذا الشكل أحد الأمرين: إما منافاة النسبة المتحققة في الكبرى إلى ذات الأكبر للنسبة المتحققة في الصغرى إلى ذات الأصغر؛ ليدل على مغايرة الذاتين، ويلزم دوام سلب الأكبر عما يصدق عليه الأصغر، وإما منافاة نسبة وصف الأوسط إلى وصف الأكبر التي تضمنها الكبرى الوصفية لنسبته إلى ذات الأصغر ؛ ليدل على عدم صدق وصف الأكبر على ذات الأصغر بالجهة التي انتسب بها إليها الأوسط، لا كما ظن التفتازاني أن منافاة نسبة وصف الأوسط إلى وصف الأكبر لنسبته إلى ذات الأصغر ضابطة هذا الشكل ؛ فإنه غلط فاحش هكذا ينبغي أن يفهم هذا المقام، والله أعلم بحقيقة الحال .
Section on Mixed Syllogisms: That the major is always denied of the minor, but that this denial is necessary is an extra matter that this repugnance does not give; and analogize to its converse. And if the actual minor is other than these two with one of the descriptive [premises] or the possible with one of the conditional [premises], then the major premise rules that the relation of the attribute of the middle to the attribute of the major is permanent in the first branch, and necessary in the second, and in sum repugnant to its relation to the essence of the minor. So inevitably the attribute of the major is separate from the essence of the minor in the mode by which the middle was related to it: if permanently then permanently, and if temporarily then temporarily. But if it is not permanent, then the separation of the major from it is not permanent; because the non-permanence of the relation of the attribute of the middle to it is not repugnant to its relation to the attribute of the major. Similarly, separation from the essence of the minor does not necessarily entail that it is necessary; for the utmost that follows from its negation is the possibility of the truth of the attribute of the major about it, and it does not entail the possibility of attribution of the attribute of the middle to it in the modal relation by which it was related to the attribute of the major; because this [attribute] is that of which the major is true. Also, the possibility of the necessary condition does not entail the possibility of the major – the relation is only the necessary consequent, if it is impossible due to another, as has passed many times. And perhaps you have noticed from here that the productive rule of this figure is one of two things: either the repugnance of the relation realized in the major premise to the essence of the major from the relation realized in the minor premise to the essence of the minor, to indicate the difference of the two essences, and then the permanent denial of the major from that of which the minor is true necessarily follows; or the repugnance of the relation of the attribute of the middle to the attribute of the major, which the descriptive major premise includes, to its relation to the essence of the minor, to indicate the non-truth of the attribute of the major about the essence of the minor in the mode by which the middle was related to it – not as al-Taftazani supposed that the repugnance of the relation of the attribute of the middle to the attribute of the major to its relation to the essence of the minor is the rule of this figure; for that is a gross error. Thus ought this station to be understood, and God knows the truth of the situation.
Source pages p. 526

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory and corrective commentary on mixed syllogisms (al-mukhtalitat) and the rule for the third figure. The author begins by discussing the scenario where the major is always denied of the minor, noting that the necessity of that denial is an additional condition not given by mere repugnance. He then considers cases where the minor premise is actual (fi'liyya) with a descriptive (wasfiyya) major, or possible (mumkina) with a conditional (mashruta) major. In these, the major premise asserts that the relation of the middle term's attribute to the major's attribute is permanent or necessary, and this relation is repugnant to the middle's relation to the minor's essence. Consequently, the major's attribute must be separate from the minor's essence in the same mode (permanent or temporary). If the relation is not permanent, the separation is not permanent either, because non-permanence of the middle's relation to the minor is not repugnant to its relation to the major. Similarly, separation from the minor's essence does not imply necessity; the utmost is possibility of the major's truth, not possibility of the middle's attribution. The author then derives the productive rule for the third figure: either (1) repugnance between the relation in the major to the major's essence and the relation in the minor to the minor's essence, indicating distinct essences and yielding permanent denial of the major from what the minor is true of; or (2) repugnance between the relation of the middle's attribute to the major's attribute (in the descriptive major) and its relation to the minor's essence, indicating that the major's attribute is not true of the minor's essence in the mode by which the middle was related. The author explicitly corrects al-Taftazani's mistake: Taftazani thought the rule is only the second repugnance, but it is both, or either. This is a gross error. The passage ends with a prayer.

Key Terms
مختلطات
تنافي
ضابطة إنتاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the mixed syllogism scenario where the minor is actual and the major is descriptive, what does the major premise rule?
A) The middle's attribute is permanently related to the major's attribute.
B) The middle's attribute is necessarily related to the minor's essence.
C) The major is always separate from the minor.
D) The minor is possible.

Key Takeaway
The repugnance between the middle's relations determines the mode of separation of the major from the minor, not the necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the third figure syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden (major premise: descriptive); this new drink is intoxicant (minor: actual).' Identify which type of repugnance (essence-to-essence or attribute-to-essence) is present and why.

The Syllogism
advanced
6 min
The Correct Productive Rule for the Third Figure and Taftazani's Error
Derives the two repugnance conditions and corrects Taftazani's mistake.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two possible repugnance conditions for the third figure and correctly identify Taftazani's error.

Core Idea
Taftazani's error was limiting the rule to one repugnance; the correct rule allows either of two.

This passage formally states the productive rule for the third figure as one of two possible repugnances, and explicitly corrects al-Taftazani's mistake of limiting it to only one.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two possible repugnances
First repugnance: between the relation in the major premise (major's relation to its own essence) and the relation in the minor premise (minor's relation to its own essence) – this indicates distinct essences and yields permanent denial of the major from the minor. Second repugnance: between the middle's attribute relation to the major's attribute (in descriptive major) and its relation to the minor's essence – this indicates the major's attribute is not true of the minor's essence in that mode.
Either repugnance suffices for productivity.
2
Contrast with Taftazani's error
Al-Taftazani thought only the second repugnance is the rule, but the author stresses it is one of two. Taftazani's view is a gross error (ghalat fahish).
The author emphasizes that both repugnances are valid productive conditions.
3
Apply the corrected rule
To check a third figure syllogism, determine which repugnance is present. If neither, the syllogism is not productive.
This is crucial for modal syllogisms with mixed premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في المختلطات
أن يسلب الأكبر عن الأصغر دائما، وأما أن ذلك السلب ضروري فأمر زائد لا يعطيه هذا التنافي، وقس عليه عكس ذلك.
وأما إذا كان الصغرى الفعلية غيرهما مع إحدى الوصفيات أو الممكنة مع إحدى المشروطتين فإن الكبرى حاكمة بأن نسبة وصف الأوسط إلى وصف الأكبر بالدوام، على الشق الأول ، وبالضرورة، على الثاني، وبالجملة منافية لنسبته إلى ذات الأصغر ، فلا بد أن يكون وصف الأكبر منفكا عن ذات الأصغر بالجهة، التي نسب بها الأوسط إليها، إن دائما فدائما، وإن وقتا فوقتا ، أما إن كان لا دائما لا يكون انفكاك الأكبر عنه لادائما ؛ لأن لادوام نسبة وصف الأوسط إليها غير مناف لنسبته إلى وصف الأكبر، وكذا انفكاك عن ذات الأصغر ، لا يلزم أن يكون ضروريا؛ فإن غاية ما لزم من انتفائها إمكان صدق وصف الأكبر عليها، ولا يلزم منه إمكان انتساب وصف الأوسط إليها، بالنسبة الموجهة التي انتسب بها إلى وصف الأكبر؛ فإن هذه لما صدق عليه الأكبر. وأيضا إمكان الملزوم لا يستلزم إمكان الأكبر النسبة إنما هي اللازم، إذا كان محالا بالغير، كما مر غير مرةٍ.

The Syllogism
intermediate

Conditions for Productivity of the Third Figure
Author states the condition for the third figure's productivity and explains the result for descriptive and non-descriptive premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition for the third figure's productivity and determine the conclusion type for descriptive vs non-descriptive premises.

Core Idea
The third figure's productivity depends on the minor premise's modality like the first figure.

We have seen the conditions for the first figure. The third figure shares the same requirement for the minor premise regarding modality. Additionally, the conclusion is not always like the major; it differs based on whether the premises are descriptive or not. Let's unpack these distinctions from the source text.

Guided Path
1
Recall the first figure condition
The first figure requires the minor premise to be affirmative and actual (not negative or conditional) for productivity. The third figure has the same condition.
2
Apply the condition to the third figure
Check that the minor premise is not descriptive or conditional; otherwise the minor term is not subsumed under the middle, and no conclusion follows.
3
Non-descriptive premises result
For non-descriptive premises (actual, possible, etc.), the conclusion has the same modality as the major premise, because the major rules for all individuals of the middle and the minor says the minor and middle meet in some individuals.
4
Descriptive premises result
If one premise is descriptive (where the subject is considered under its attribute), the conclusion is like the converse of the minor premise, not like the major. The major's ruling applies to all instances of the middle's attribute, so the conclusion mirrors the minor's temporal scope.
5
Handle temporal qualifications
If the middle is permanent, the major is permanent; if temporary, the conclusion is restricted. If the major is generally conditional, the result is restricted by non-permanence if the converse of the minor has non-permanence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فعلية
شرط إنتاج (وَفِي) الشكل (الثَّالِثِ) يشترط (ما) اشترط (فِي الْأَوَّلِ(١)) ، من حسب الجهة الصغرى ، وإلا لم يندرج الأصغر تحت الأوسط، فلا يلزم النتيجة ، (والنتيجة) في هذا الشكل أيضا كَالْكُبْرَى فِي غَيْرِ الْوَصْفِيَّاتِ) الأربع؛ لأن الكبرى حاكمة بثبوت الأكبر لأفراد الأوسط، وقد حكمت الصغرى أن الأصغر الأوسط (٢) ملتقيان في بعض الأفراد، فيكون الأكبر أيضا ثابتا لبعض أفراد الأصغر بالجهة التي ثبت بها ] (٣) الأكبر لأفراد الأوسط.
أزمنة
(وَإِلَّا فَعَكْسُ الصُّغْرَى)، أي وإن كان من إحدى الوصفيات فالنتيجة كعكس الصغرى؛ لأن الكبرى حكمت بالأكبر لأفراد الأوسط في جميع وصف الأوسط، فلا بد أن يثبت للأصغر أيضا في أزمنة الأوسط؛ إذ قد حكمت الصغرى بأنهما ملتقيان في فرد منه، فذلك الفرد فرد الأوسط، فإن ثبت الأوسط دائما ثبت الأكبر دائما، وإن وقتا فوقتا ، لكن لا يكون مطلقا، بل حال كونه (مَحْذُوفًا عَنْهُ لَادَوَامُهُ) ؛ لكونه سالبة، ولا دخل له في الإنتاج. ولعلك تعرف بما عرفت في الشكل الأول أن الكبرى إذا كانت مشروطة عامة يلزم تقييد النتيجة باللادوام، إن كان في عكس الصغرى لادوام؛ لكون وصف الأوسط غير دائم، فبفوت الشرط يكون الأكبر أيضا غير دائم.
Practical: The condition for productivity in the third figure is what was conditioned in the first, regarding the modality of the minor premise; otherwise the minor is not subsumed under the middle, so the conclusion does not follow. And the result in this figure is also like the major in the four non-descriptive [premises]; because the major rules the affirmation of the major for individuals of the middle, and the minor ruled that the minor and the middle meet in some individuals, so the major is also affirmed for some individuals of the minor in the mode by which the major was affirmed for individuals of the middle. Times. Otherwise, it is the converse of the minor; meaning if it is one of the descriptive [premises], then the result is like the converse of the minor; because the major ruled the major for individuals of the middle in all of the attribute of the middle, so it must also be affirmed for the minor in the times of the middle; since the minor ruled that they meet in an individual of it, that individual is an individual of the middle. If the middle is permanent then the major is permanent; if temporary then temporary. But it is not absolute; rather in the state of being deleted from it its non-permanence; because it is negative, and it has no role in production. And perhaps you know from what you learned in the first figure that if the major is generally conditional, it necessitates restricting the result with non-permanence, if in the converse of the minor there is non-permanence; because the attribute of the middle is not permanent, so by the loss of the condition, the major is also not permanent.
Source pages p. 527

Commentary
This passage explains the conditions for the third figure's productivity and the form of its conclusion. The author begins by stating the condition: the minor premise must have the same modality requirement as in the first figure; otherwise the minor is not subsumed under the middle and no conclusion follows. Next, the result is explained: for non-descriptive premises (the four types: actual, possible, etc.), the conclusion is like the major premise in modality and quantity, because the major affirms the major term for all individuals of the middle, and the minor states that the minor and middle meet in some individuals, so the major applies to some individuals of the minor in the same mode. The phrase 'Times' (أزمنة) likely introduces a temporal qualification. For descriptive premises (where the middle's attribute is considered), the result is the converse of the minor (i.e., an immediate inference from the minor). The reason: the major rules the major for all individuals of the middle in all instances of the middle's attribute, so it must also be affirmed for the minor in the times when the middle is present. The meeting individual is an individual of the middle, so if the middle is permanent, the major is permanent; if temporary, then temporary. However, the conclusion is not absolute; it is 'deleted from it its non-permanence' because the minor may be negative, which does not affect production. Finally, the author recalls from the first figure that if the major is a general conditional, the result must be restricted by non-permanence if the converse of the minor has non-permanence, because the middle's attribute is not permanent.

Key Takeaway
The third figure shares the first figure's modality condition for the minor, and the conclusion mirrors either the major (non-descriptive) or the converse of the minor (descriptive).

The Syllogism

Third Figure Syllogism: Conditions and Modal Conclusion
Author explains modal conditions for third figure syllogism and its conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the conclusion of a third figure categorical syllogism based on the modality of its premises.

Core Idea
Third figure conclusion mirrors major in non-descriptive, but resembles converse of minor in descriptive premises.

The third figure of categorical syllogism has its own validity conditions and conclusion rules. This passage explains how the modality of premises—whether they are descriptive (waṣfīyyāt) or not—determines the form and modality of the conclusion. It also highlights the closeness of the third figure to the first figure in logical conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term
In the third figure, the middle term is the subject of both premises. Locate the term that appears as subject in each premise.
For example, in 'Every human is rational; every human is animal; therefore some animal is rational', the middle term is 'human'.
2
Check the minor premise's modality
The minor premise must have a modality that allows the minor term to fall under the middle term. If not, the syllogism is invalid.
The condition is the same as in the first figure: the minor premise's modality must be such that the subject (minor) is included under the middle.
3
Determine if premises are descriptive (waṣfīyyāt)
Descriptive propositions involve temporal or conditional descriptions of the subject. If the premises are not descriptive, the conclusion is like the major premise in form and modality.
Non-descriptive examples: 'Every human is rational' (no time condition). Descriptive: 'Every human is rational while alive'.
4
If descriptive, conclusion is converse of the minor
In descriptive syllogisms, the major premise judges the major term for individuals of the middle in all descriptions of the middle. The conclusion must then be equivalent to the converse of the minor premise, with the same modality restricted to the times when the middle term holds.
The author explains that the conclusion is stated with removal of perpetuity if the major is negative or conditioned.
5
Apply the rule from the first figure
If the major premise is generally conditioned, the conclusion is restricted to non-perpetuity when the conversion of the minor involves non-perpetuity. This is similar to the first figure's treatment of mixed modalities.
The third figure is close to the first in conditions and production, as they both involve actual meeting of the middle with the minor and all individuals of the middle being judged in one premise.

Source Text

الشكل الثالث شرط إنتاج (وَفِي) الشكل (الثَّالِثِ) يشترط (ما) اشترط (فِي الْأَوَّلِ) ، من حسب الجهة الصغرى ، وإلا لم يندرج الأصغر تحت الأوسط، فلا يلزم النتيجة ، (والنتيجة) في هذا الشكل أيضا كَالْكُبْرَى فِي غَيْرِ الْوَصْفِيَّاتِ) الأربع؛ لأن الكبرى حاكمة بثبوت الأكبر لأفراد الأوسط، وقد حكمت الصغرى أن الأصغر والأوسط ملتقيان في بعض الأفراد، فيكون الأكبر أيضا ثابتا لبعض أفراد الأصغر بالجهة التي ثبت بها الأكبر لأفراد الأوسط. (وَإِلَّا فَعَكْسُ الصُّغْرَى)، أي وإن كان من إحدى الوصفيات فالنتيجة كعكس الصغرى؛ لأن الكبرى حكمت بالأكبر لأفراد الأوسط في جميع وصف الأوسط، فلا بد أن يثبت للأصغر أيضا في أزمنة الأوسط؛ إذ قد حكمت الصغرى بأنهما ملتقيان في فرد منه، فذلك الفرد فرد الأوسط، فإن ثبت الأوسط دائما ثبت الأكبر دائما، وإن وقتا فوقتا ، لكن لا يكون مطلقا، بل حال كونه (مَحْذُوفًا عَنْهُ لَادَوَامُهُ) ؛ لكونه سالبة، ولا دخل له في الإنتاج. ولعلك تعرف بما عرفت في الشكل الأول أن الكبرى إذا كانت مشروطة عامة يلزم تقييد النتيجة باللادوام، إن كان في عكس الصغرى لادوام؛ لكون وصف الأوسط غير دائم، فبفوت الشرط يكون الأكبر أيضا غير دائم. فصل في المختلطات (وَمَضْمُوما إِلَيْه لادوام الكبرى) ؛ للاندراج البين، فقد ظهر أن هذا الشكل قريب إلى الأول في الشرائط والإنتاج. وضابطة الشكلين كونهما مشتملين على ملاقاة الأوسط للأصغر بالفعل ؛ ليكون الأصغر فردا للأوسط، وكون جميع أفراد الأوسط محكوما عليها في إحدى المقدمتين؛ ليندرج أفراد موضوع الصغرى تحت موضوع الكبرى ؛ ليسري حكم الأكبر إلى أفراد الأصغر، على طبق حكم الكبرى والعلم الحق عند علام الغيوب.
The third figure. Condition for production (and in) the (third) figure, what is required (in the first) is required, regarding the modality of the minor, otherwise the minor does not fall under the middle, so the conclusion does not follow. (And the conclusion) in this figure is also like the major in the non-descriptive (propositions) because the major judges the affirmation of the major term to individuals of the middle, and the minor has judged that the minor and middle meet in some individuals, so the major term is also affirmed for some individuals of the minor with the modality by which the major term was affirmed for individuals of the middle. (Otherwise, it is the converse of the minor), i.e. if it is one of the descriptive (propositions), the conclusion is like the converse of the minor; because the major judged the major term for individuals of the middle in all description of the middle, so it must be affirmed for the minor also in the times of the middle; since the minor judged that they meet in an individual of it, so that individual is an individual of the middle. If the middle is perpetual, the major is perpetual; if temporal, temporal; but it is not absolute, but rather in the state of being (removed from it its perpetuity) because it is negative, and it has no role in production. And you may know from what you know in the first figure that if the major is generally conditioned, it necessitates restricting the conclusion to non-perpetuity, if in the converse of the minor there is non-perpetuity; because the description of the middle is not perpetual, so by the loss of the condition, the major is also non-perpetual. Chapter on mixed (syllogisms). (And added to it is the non-perpetuity of the major) because of the obvious inclusion. It has become clear that this figure is close to the first in conditions and production. And the rule of the two figures is that they involve the meeting of the middle with the minor in actuality, so that the minor is an individual of the middle, and all individuals of the middle are judged upon in one of the two premises, so that the individuals of the subject of the minor are included under the subject of the major, so that the judgment of the major term extends to the individuals of the minor, in accordance with the judgment of the major. And true knowledge is with the Knower of the Unseen.
Source pages p. 527

Commentary
This passage discusses the third figure of the categorical syllogism and its conditions. The author is expanding on the conditions required for a valid syllogism in the third figure, which are the same as those for the first figure regarding modality. The minor premise must have the appropriate modality so that the minor term falls under the middle term; otherwise, the conclusion does not follow. Regarding the conclusion, in the third figure, when the premises are not 'descriptive' (i.e., not temporally qualified), the conclusion is like the major premise, since the major judges the major term for all individuals of the middle, and the minor says the minor and middle meet in some individuals, so the major term applies to some individuals of the minor with the same modality. However, if the premises are descriptive (e.g., involving time), the conclusion is like the converse of the minor premise. Because the major judges the major term for individuals of the middle in all descriptions of the middle, but the meeting is only in a particular individual of the middle. If the middle is perpetual, the major is perpetual; if temporal, temporal. But the conclusion is not absolute; it is stated with the removal of perpetuity if the major is negative. The author then notes that from the first figure we know that if the major is generally conditioned, the conclusion must be restricted to non-perpetuity if the conversion of the minor involves non-perpetuity. The section ends with a 'chapter on mixed syllogisms' which underscores that the third figure is close to the first in conditions and production. The common rule for both figures is that they involve actual meeting of the middle with the minor, so the minor is an individual of the middle, and all individuals of the middle are judged in one premise, so that the subject of the minor falls under the subject of the major, allowing the judgment of the major term to apply to the minor. For example, in a third figure syllogism: 'Every human is rational (major premise), every human is an animal (minor premise), therefore some animal is rational'—here the middle term 'human' meets the minor 'animal' in actuality, and the major 'rational' is judged for all humans.

The third figure's conclusion mirrors the major premise in non-descriptive cases, but when premises are descriptive, it follows the converse of the minor, reflecting the modal conditions of the syllogism.

The Syllogism

Five Modal Conditions for the Fourth Figure
Author enumerates five conditions for valid fourth-figure syllogisms with modal premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and apply the five modal conditions required for a valid fourth-figure categorical syllogism, using examples from Islamic logic.

Core Idea
The fourth figure's productivity depends on strict modal constraints to avoid false conclusions.

The author of the matn avoided the fourth figure due to its unnaturalness. Here, the commentator fills in five crucial modal conditions for its validity. These conditions govern actuality of premises, convertibility of negatives, and special perpetual propositions. Understanding them prevents invalid syllogisms like those involving the moon and eclipse.

Guided Path
1
Identify condition 1: Actuality of both premises
The first condition requires that both premises be actual (necessary or actual), not possible. The commentator gives a counterexample: if the minor is possible ('Every donkey is ridden by Zayd potentially') and the major is necessary ('No horse is a donkey necessarily'), the conclusion is false. Similarly if the premises are swapped.
This condition prevents using purely possible modal propositions in the fourth figure.
2
Identify condition 2: Convertibility of negative propositions
In moods other than the first two, any negative premise must be convertible. The commentator provides an example: 'No moon is eclipsed necessarily at quadrature not always' and 'Everything having an eclipse is a moon necessarily' have true premises but a false conclusion because the negative is not convertible.
This restricts the sixth and seventh moods to special propositions that allow conversion of particular negatives.
3
Identify condition 3: Two options for the third mood
In the third mood only, either the minor is perpetual (dā'ima) or the major is from propositions with convertible negatives. Counterexample: 'No illuminated thing is eclipsed as long as illuminated not always' and 'Every moon is illuminated at quadrature not always' have true premises but false conclusion.
This condition ensures that the particular negative can be handled.
4
Identify condition 4: Major must be from convertible negatives in moods 6 and 8
For the sixth and eighth moods, the major must be from the set of propositions with convertible negatives, because these moods are more general than the third and the other options do not suffice.
Since the minor cannot be perpetual here, the major must compensate.
5
Identify condition 5: Minor must be from the two special propositions in mood 8
In the eighth mood, the minor must be one of the two special propositions (al-khāṣṣatayn). This is because the syllogism works by reversing the order and then converting the conclusion, which only works with these special propositions.
The commentator adds that if the minor is a general conditional and the major is special, the first figure can still produce the conclusion.

The possible proposition is not used; because it is true that 'Every donkey is ridden by Zayd potentially' and 'No horse is a donkey necessarily', and the conclusion is false. And if the major premise is replaced by our statement 'Every braying animal is a donkey necessarily', likewise if the possible proposition is made the major premise and the minor premise is 'No riding animal is a braying animal always', the conclusion is also false. And it should be, according to those who consider the possible proposition productive in the minor of the first figure, not to require actuality in the first two moods, as is obvious, and the investigation is as previously. The second: that the negative proposition used in other than the first two moods is convertible; because it is true that 'No moon is eclipsed necessarily at the time of quadrature not always' and 'Everything having an eclipse is a moon necessarily', with the conclusion false, and likewise if it is said 'Every eclipsed thing has an eclipse necessarily' and 'No moon is eclipsed necessarily at the time of quadrature not always'. The ancients, since they did not find their conversion, judged these two moods to be barren, and also the eighth. The third: in the third only, one of two things: either that the minor premise is from one of the two perpetual [propositions], or that the major premise is from the propositions with convertible negatives; because it is true that 'No illuminated thing is eclipsed as long as it is illuminated not always' and 'Every moon is illuminated at the time of quadrature not always', and the conclusion is false. The fourth: in the sixth and eighth, the major premise must be from the propositions with convertible negatives, because they are more general than the third, so neither of the two mentioned options is sufficient in them. And since the minor premise is not from the two perpetual propositions because of what you know of the requirement of conversion of the negative, and you will know from the requirement that the minor premise in the eighth is from one of the two special [propositions], it is necessary that the major premise be from the convertible negatives. The fifth: in the eighth only, the minor premise must be from the two special [propositions]; for this mood is evident there by reversing the order, then reversing the conclusion. So it is necessary that the minor premise be such that if it is made the major, it produces one of the two special [propositions] through the first figure. And that is only possible in the two special [propositions], as they said. And we say: If the minor premise is a general conditional and the major premise is from one of the two special [propositions], and the order is reversed, it produces one of the two special [propositions] through the first figure. And it has been known from what was mentioned that in this mood it is also not possible to compose from the perpetual and necessary major premises that are both true, otherwise when reversing the order it would necessitate a necessary or perpetual that is not perpetual. So its major premise is confined to one of the four descriptive [propositions].

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the conditions for the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism when modality (جهة) is considered. The author first notes that the fourth figure is close to the first in conditions and productivity, but then states that the original author (المصنف) omitted it due to its unnaturalness. Here, the commentator fills in the five modal conditions.

The first condition requires actuality (فعلية) of both premises; possible premises are not allowed. The commentator gives counterexamples: if the minor is possible and the major is necessary, or vice versa, the conclusion can be false.

The second condition requires that negative propositions used in moods other than the first two (i.e., moods that involve particular negatives) be convertible. The commentator provides an example with the moon and eclipse to show that if the negative is not convertible, the syllogism fails. This condition restricts the seventh and sixth moods to special propositions (خاصتين).

The third condition applies only to the third mood and offers two options: either the minor is perpetual (دائمة) or the major is from propositions with convertible negatives. A counterexample is given.

The fourth condition applies to the sixth and eighth moods: the major must be from convertible negatives because these moods are more general than the third.

The fifth condition is specifically for the eighth mood: the minor must be from the two special propositions. The commentator explains that this mood works by reversing the order and then the conclusion, and then gives his own addition: if the minor is a general conditional (مشروطة عامة) and the major is special, the first figure can produce the conclusion.

Throughout, the commentator uses concrete modal examples (e.g., lunar eclipse) to illustrate when conclusions become false, showing the necessity of the conditions.

Five Conditions for Modal Syllogism Moods
Author enumerates five conditions for valid moods in mixed modal syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which modal proposition types are required for each mood (third, sixth, seventh, eighth) in first-figure mixed modal syllogisms and explain why each condition is necessary.

Core Idea
Specific moods require specific modal premise types; otherwise the syllogism is sterile.

Modal syllogisms involve premises with modal qualifiers like 'perpetual', 'necessary', or 'possible'. Not every combination of modal premises yields a valid conclusion. This passage from a classical logic text enumerates five conditions that must be met for certain moods to be productive. By mastering these conditions, you will be able to assess the validity of arguments in fields like fiqh and kalam where modal reasoning is common.

Guided Path
1
Identify the moods discussed
Locate the mood numbers mentioned in the passage: third, sixth, seventh, and eighth moods. These are moods of the first figure in modal logic.
2
Extract the condition for each mood
For each mood, find the specific condition stated. For example, the seventh requires the major from 'al-khāṣṣatayn', the third has a disjunctive condition, etc.
The conditions are listed as 'الثالث', 'الرابع', 'الخامس' etc.
3
Understand the logical reasoning behind each condition
Consider why each condition is imposed. For instance, conversion of negatives is needed because without it, the conclusion may be false, as shown by the moon eclipse example.
The example in the passage shows that if the major does not convert, a false conclusion follows.
4
Apply a condition to a fiqh example
Take a known fiqh syllogism involving modal terms (e.g., 'Every intoxicant is forbidden perpetually') and test whether it meets the conditions for a given mood.
Use the condition from the third mood: either the minor is perpetual or the major's negative converts.

ويلزم من هذا الشرط أن يكون الكبرى في الضرب السابع، والصغرى في الضرب السادس من إحدى الخاصتين؛ إذ غيرهما لا ينعكس في السالبة الجزئية، والقدماء لما لم يجدوا انعكاسهما حكموا بعقم هذين الضربين، بل الثامن أيضا.
الثالث: في الثالث فقط، أحد الأمرين: إما كون الصغرى من إحدى الدائمتين، وإما كون الكبرى من القضايا المنعكسة السوالب ؛ لصدق «لا شيء من المنور بمنخسف ما دام منورا لا دائما وكل قمر منور وقت التربيع لا دائما ، وكذب النتيجة .
الرابع: في السادس والثامن كون الكبرى من القضايا المنعكسة السوالب لأنهما أعم من الثالث ، فلا فد فيهما من أحد الأمرين المذكورين أيضا. ولما لم يكن الصغرى من الدائمتين لما عرفت من اشتراط انعكاس السالبة ، وستعرف من اشتراط كون الصغرى في الثامن من إحدى الخاصتين وجب كون الكبرى من المنعكسة السوالب.
الخامس في الثامن فقط كون الصغرى من الخاصتين ؛ فإن هذا الضرب بيِّنَ ثَمَّ بعكس الترتيب، ثم عكس النتيجة، فلا بد من كون الصغرى بحيث لو جعلت كبرى أنتجت إحدى الخاصتين، ولا يتأتى ذلك إلا في الخاصتين، كذا قالوا. ونحن نقول : إن كان الصغرى مشروطة عامة والكبرى من إحدى الخاصتين، وعُكس الترتيب ينتج بالشكل الأول إحدى الخاصتين. وقد علم مما ذكر أنه لا يمكن في هذا الضرب أيضا التأليف من الكبرى الدائمة والضرورية الصادقتين، وإلا لزم عند عكس الترتيب ضرورية أو دائمة لادائمة ، فانحصر كبراه في إحدى الوصفيات الأربع.
And it follows from this condition that the major premise in the seventh mood, and the minor premise in the sixth mood, must be from one of the two special types; because others do not convert in the particular negative, and the ancients, since they did not find their conversion, ruled that these two moods are sterile, and even the eighth also.
Third: In the third only, one of two things: either the minor premise is from one of the two perpetuals, or the major premise is from the propositions whose negatives are convertible; because 'Nothing that is illuminated is eclipsed as long as it is illuminated, not perpetually, and every moon is illuminated at the time of quadrature, not perpetually' is true, and the conclusion is false.
Fourth: In the sixth and eighth, the major premise must be from the propositions whose negatives are convertible, because they are more general than the third, so there is no benefit in them from one of the two mentioned things. And since the minor premise is not from the two perpetuals, as you know from the condition of conversion of the negative, and you will know from the condition that the minor in the eighth is from one of the two special types, it became necessary that the major is from the convertible negatives.
Fifth: In the eighth only, the minor premise must be from the two special types; for this mood becomes clear there by reversing the order, then converting the conclusion, so the minor must be such that if it were made major, it would produce one of the two special types, and that is only possible in the two special types, as they said. And we say: If the minor is a general conditional and the major is from one of the two special types, and the order is reversed, it produces one of the two special types in the first figure. And it has been known from what was mentioned that it is not possible in this mood also to compose from the perpetual and necessary major premises that are both true, otherwise it would necessitate, when the order is reversed, a necessary or perpetual not-perpetual, so its major is restricted to one of the four descriptive types.
Source pages p. 529

Commentary
This passage lists five conditions for valid moods in mixed modal syllogisms, specifically for moods in the first figure. It begins by stating a condition for the seventh and sixth moods: their major and minor must be from 'the two special types' (al-khāṣṣatayn), likely referring to propositions that are not perpetual or necessary but rather possible or specific. The ancients considered these moods sterile because the required conversion was not possible. Then it discusses the third mood, offering two options: either the minor is from the 'two perpetuals' (al-dā'imatayn) or the major is from propositions whose negatives convert. An example is given: 'Nothing illuminated is eclipsed while illuminated, not perpetually, and every moon is illuminated at quadrature, not perpetually' – this yields a false conclusion, showing the need for proper conversion. For the fourth condition, in the sixth and eighth moods, the major must be from convertible negatives because these moods are more general. Since the minor is not perpetual due to conversion requirements, the major must be convertible. For the fifth condition, in the eighth mood alone, the minor must be from the two special types. This is because the mood is clarified by reversing order and converting the conclusion. The author adds a personal note: if the minor is a general conditional and the major from the special types, reversing order in the first figure produces the desired result. The passage concludes that the major in this mood cannot be perpetual and necessary both true, as that would lead to an impossible conclusion, so the major is restricted to one of the four 'descriptive types' (al-waṣfiyyāt). Throughout, the author uses technical terminology of modal logic, referencing principles like conversion of negatives and the behavior of perpetual and necessary propositions. A fiqh example: Consider 'Every intoxicant is forbidden perpetually' and 'No nabidh is an intoxicant at some time' – the negations must convert properly for validity.

The Syllogism

Determining Conclusions in Mixed Modal Syllogisms (First Figure)
Author details the type of conclusion for each mixed modal mood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the correct conclusion modality for each mood of a first-figure mixed modal syllogism based on the modal types of the premises.

Core Idea
The conclusion of a mixed modal syllogism follows specific rules depending on the mood and premise modalities.

In Islamic logic, modal syllogisms combine premises of different modalities (e.g., perpetual, necessary, temporal). The author details the type of conclusion that results for each mood in the first figure. Understanding these rules is key to evaluating modal arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first two moods
For moods 1 and 2, if the minor premise is from the perpetuals (dā'ima) or the general types (ʿurfiyya ʿāmma) and the major's negatives convert, the conclusion is an absolute temporal (ḥīniyya muṭlaqa). If the minor is from the special types (ʿurfiyya khāṣṣa), the conclusion is a temporal non-perpetual (ḥīniyya lā dā'ima).
The condition on the major's negatives converting ensures that conversion operations can be applied if needed.
2
Handle the case of true premises
Avoid composing a syllogism from two perpetuals or from a major perpetual unless the minor converts properly. Otherwise, the only route is to reverse the order and convert the conclusion or the major to reduce to the third figure.
This ensures the syllogism remains productive.
3
Determine the conclusion for mood 3
If one premise is perpetual (dā'ima), the conclusion is perpetual. Otherwise, it follows the conversion of the minor with necessity removed and restricted by the condition of existence.
The minor in mood 3 is negative, so its non-perpetuality is affirmative and productive.
4
Determine conclusions for moods 4 and 5
If the negative premise is perpetual or necessary, the conclusion is perpetual. Otherwise, it follows the conversion of the minor with necessity and non-perpetuality removed.
The minor in these moods is affirmative, making its non-perpetuality a sterile absolute general negative.
5
Identify conclusions for moods 6, 7, and 8
Mood 6 yields a general particular (ʿurfiyya ʿāmma) by conversion of the minor. Mood 7 follows conversion of the minor plus the non-perpetuality of the major via conversion of the major. Mood 8 gives a special particular (ʿurfiyya khāṣṣa) by reversing order and converting the conclusion.
These results rely on reducing to the first figure or using conversions.

Section on mixed modalities
And the conclusion in the first two moods is an absolute temporal proposition, if the minor is from one of the two perpetuals or the two general ones, with the condition that the major be from those whose negatives convert; and a temporal non-perpetual, if the minor is from one of the two special types under that condition. But it is necessary that then a syllogism with true premises should not be composed from one of the two perpetuals, or the major from one of them, with the condition that the minor be from those whose negatives convert; otherwise, it is an absolute general by reversing the order, then converting the conclusion, or converting the major, to return to the third figure, so it produces the desired conclusion.
And in the third mood, a perpetual conclusion, if one of the two premises is perpetual; otherwise, like the conversion of the minor, with necessity removed and restricted by the condition of existence, if it is by conversion of the minor. And in the fourth and fifth, a perpetual conclusion, if the negative is perpetual or necessary; otherwise, it is the conversion of the minor, with necessity and non-perpetuality removed, if it is by conversion of the minor. The reason why non-perpetuality is not removed in the third and not restricted in these two moods is that the minor in the third is negative, so its non-perpetuality is affirmative, and it is productive in this figure, so it produces with the major a non-perpetual conclusion; because it makes the composition with it a first mood, unlike these two moods, because the minor is affirmative, so its non-perpetuality is an absolute general negative, and it is sterile in this figure.
And in the sixth, a general particular proposition by conversion of the minor also, and in the seventh, like the conversion of the minor, with the non-perpetuality of the major added by conversion of the major, and in the eighth, a special particular proposition by reversing the order, then converting the conclusion. Commentary
This section opens by defining the conclusions for each mixed modal syllogism mood in the first figure. The passage begins with the first two moods: if the minor is from the perpetuals or generals and the major's negatives convert, the conclusion is an absolute temporal proposition (ḥīniyya muṭlaqa). If the minor is from the special types under the same condition, the conclusion is a temporal non-perpetual (ḥīniyya lā dā'ima). However, a true premise combination should not use two perpetuals or a major from them unless the minor converts properly; otherwise, the only route is to reverse order and convert the conclusion or major to reduce to the third figure. For the third mood, the conclusion is perpetual (dā'ima) if one premise is perpetual, else it follows the conversion of the minor with necessity removed and restricted by existence. For the fourth and fifth moods, the conclusion is perpetual if the negative premise is perpetual or necessary; otherwise, it follows conversion of the minor with necessity and non-perpetuality removed. The reasoning given is that in the third mood, the minor is negative, so its non-perpetuality is affirmative and productive (yielding a non-perpetual conclusion from the first mood), whereas in the fourth and fifth, the minor is affirmative, making its non-perpetuality a sterile absolute general negative. For the sixth mood, the conclusion is a general particular (ʿurfiyya ʿāmma) by conversion of the minor; the seventh follows suit but adds the non-perpetuality of the major via its conversion; the eighth yields a special particular (ʿurfiyya khāṣṣa) by reversing order and converting the conclusion. The passage then begins explaining why these results hold, but it is cut off. An Islamic example: For a perpetual conclusion, consider 'Every body is temporally originated perpetually' and 'Every accident is a body conditionally' – the conclusion must be perpetual. The author's detailed conditions show the complexity of modal logic in the Islamic tradition, where premises vary in necessity, perpetuity, and temporality.

Determining Conclusion Modalities in Mixed Syllogisms: First Two Moods
Rules for conclusions in first two moods of mixed syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the correct conclusion modality for the first two moods of mixed syllogisms based on the type of minor premise and the convertibility of the major's negatives.

Core Idea
In mixed syllogisms, the conclusion's modality depends on the minor premise type and major's negative convertibility.

Mixed syllogisms combine premises of different modalities, such as permanent and temporal. This lesson focuses on the first two moods of the third figure, where the author lays out precise rules for deriving the conclusion. Mastering these rules will sharpen your ability to evaluate arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence that mix necessity and contingency.

Guided Path
1
Classify the minor premise
Determine whether the minor premise is from the permanent/general type (دائمة or عامة) or from the particular type (خاصة). The type dictates the initial conclusion modality.
2
Check convertibility of the major's negatives
Verify if the major premise's negative propositions convert (i.e., if its converse is valid). This condition is required for the standard rule to apply.
3
Apply the rule for permanent/general minor
If the minor is permanent/general and the major's negatives convert, the conclusion is absolute temporal (حينية مطلقة).
4
Apply the rule for particular minor
If the minor is particular and the major's negatives convert, the conclusion is temporal non-permanent (حينية لا دائمة).
5
Handle the exception when condition fails
If the convertibility condition fails, the syllogism must be transformed: reverse the order, convert the result or the major to revert to a first-figure mood, yielding an absolute general conclusion.

Chapter on Mixed Syllogisms. The result in the first two moods is absolute temporal, if the minor is from one of the permanent or general [premises], under the condition that the major is such that its negatives convert; and temporal non-permanent, if the minor is from one of the particular [premises] with that condition, but then it should not be composed from one of the permanent [premises] a syllogism with true premises, or the major from one of them, under the condition that the minor is such that its negatives convert; otherwise, [it is] absolute general by reversing the order, then converting the result, or converting the major, so that it returns to the third figure, and it produces the desired result. In the third mood: permanent, if one of the two premises is permanent; otherwise, like the converse of the minor, with necessity removed from it and restricted by the condition of existence, if it is by conversion of the minor. In the fourth and fifth: permanent, if the negative is permanent or necessary; otherwise, like the converse of the minor, with necessity and non-permanence removed from it, if it is by conversion of the minor. And the reason why non-permanence is not removed in the third and is not restricted in these two moods is because the minor in the third is negative, so its non-permanence is affirmative, and it is productive in this figure, so it produces with the major non-permanence, because it makes the composition with it a first mood, unlike these two moods, because the minor is affirmative, so its non-permanence is a negative absolute general, which is sterile in this figure. In the sixth: conventional general, also by conversion of the minor. In the seventh: like the converse of the minor, with the non-permanence of the major added to it by conversion of the major. In the eighth: conventional particular, by reversing the order, then converting the result. And the reason for the production of these results in this figure: as for the first two moods, if they are such that negatives convert, then the major judges the conjunction of the attributes of the major and middle in individuals, and similarly the minor judges the conjunction of the attributes of the middle and minor in all individuals of the middle, some of which are individuals of the major; so the juxtaposition of the attributes of the minor and major follows in it, so the affirmation of the major for some of the minor follows at the time it is minor. Then if the major is from one of the particular [premises], it has judged the separation of the two attributes from those individuals, because the two attributes of the particular [premises] are accidental attributes separating from the essence; and the minor has judged that the individuals of the middle are individuals of the minor; so the separation of the attribute of the major from some essence of the minor is necessary, which is non-permanence. And if the minor is from one of the particular [premises] and the major as supposed, the major judges the necessity of the attribute of the middle for the major in individuals, and the minor judges the separation of the attributes of the major and minor from all individuals of the middle, which are some individuals of the minor; so the separation of the major in them is necessary absolutely. So it has become clear that the temporal non-permanent is necessary, if one of the two premises is from one of the particular [premises], not as some claimed that the result is absolute temporal, because non-permanence is a negative that does not convert; for the non-productivity of a proposition alone does not entail its non-productivity when it is part of another proposition. As for the third mood: it is either that the minor is permanent; then it judges that the essence of the middle is not the essence of the minor, and the major, being actual, judges that the essence of the major is part of the essence of the middle; so the essence of the major is not the essence of the minor; so the attribute of the major is denied of the essence of the minor permanently. Or that only the major is permanent; then it has judged that the attribute of the middle is necessary for the essence of the major, and the minor...
Source pages p. 530

Commentary
This passage is a detailed expansion of the rules for determining the conclusion in mixed syllogisms (المختلطات), specifically within the third figure. The author enumerates eight moods and explains the resulting modality for each. 

For the first two moods, the conclusion is absolute temporal (حينية مطلقة) when the minor premise is from the permanent or general type and the major's negatives convert. If the minor is from the particular type, the conclusion is temporal non-permanent (حينية لا دائمة). The author cautions against forming a valid syllogism with one permanent premise in this case; otherwise, a general absolute conclusion is reached by reversing the order and converting the result or the major to revert to a first-figure mood. 

In the third mood, the conclusion is permanent (دائمة) if either premise is permanent; otherwise, it is the converse of the minor with necessity removed and restricted to existence. In the fourth and fifth moods, the conclusion is also permanent if the negative premise is permanent or necessary; otherwise, it is the converse of the minor with necessity and non-permanence removed. The author explains that in the third mood the minor is negative, so its non-permanence is affirmative and productive, unlike in the fourth and fifth where the minor is affirmative and its non-permanence would be a sterile negative. 

The sixth mood yields a conventional general (عرفية عامة) conclusion via conversion of the minor. The seventh yields the converse of the minor plus the non-permanence of the major via conversion of the major. The eighth yields a conventional particular (عرفية خاصة) by reversing order and converting the result. 

The author then provides the logical justification: for the first two moods, the premises together force the conjunction of attributes, leading to the affirmation of the major for some of the minor at that time. When the major is from the particular type, its attribute is separable from the essence, so the conclusion is non-permanent. If the minor is particular, the separation is absolute, yielding necessary non-permanence. The author refutes a mistaken view that the result is always absolute temporal, noting that non-permanence is a negative and not convertible. 

For the third mood, if the minor is permanent, the major is denied of the minor permanently; if only the major is permanent, then the middle is necessary for the major, and the minor... (the text breaks off). 

Throughout, the author uses the standard logical variables (الأكبر, الأوسط, الأصغر) and stresses that the results depend on the interplay of modalities and the structure of the syllogism.

D) Conventional general (عرفية عامة)

Key Takeaway
In mixed syllogisms, the conclusion's modality is determined by the type of minor premise and the convertibility of the major's negatives; a particular minor yields a temporal non-permanent conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Examine the following mixed syllogism: Major: 'Every just action is rewarded by Allah (permanent).' Minor: 'Some actions are just at the time of performance (particular temporal).' Assuming the major's negatives convert, determine the conclusion modality using the rules for the first two moods. Justify your answer based on the passage.
?
Create a valid mixed syllogism of the first two moods using Islamic examples (e.g., from Fiqh or Kalam) where the minor is permanent/general and state the conclusion. Then modify the minor to a particular type and explain how the conclusion changes.

The Syllogism

General Conditions for Non-Permanent Conclusions in Mixed Syllogisms
Author examines conditions under which mixed premises yield non-permanent conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a mixed syllogism yields a non-permanent (lā-dawām) conclusion based on the modality of its premises.

Core Idea
In mixed syllogisms, the modality of premises determines if the conclusion is permanent or non-permanent.

This passage examines mixed syllogisms (المختلطات) where premises have different modalities—permanent, descriptive, actual, and special types. The key question is when the conclusion is non-permanent (lā-dawām), i.e., it holds only as long as the subject exists. We will extract the initial conditions that lead to such conclusions, focusing on premises from the 'two special types'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise types
Recognize the three modalities mentioned: permanent (دائمة), descriptive (وصفية), actual (فعلية), and the 'two special types' (خاصتين) likely meaning accidental or separable attributes. Understand that each modality affects how judgments are formed.
Permanent affirms a predicate always; descriptive indicates an essential attribute; actual refers to an existential condition.
2
Understand the conclusion types
Distinguish between permanent (دائمة) and non-permanent (لادوام). A non-permanent conclusion means the major description is separated from some of the minor's essence as long as the minor exists. It is a negative modal conclusion.
Non-permanent is not the same as absolute negative; it is restricted temporally.
3
Apply condition when major is from special types
If the major premise is from one of the two special types (accidental), it judges that the two descriptions (of major and middle) separate from the individuals. The minor premise (being normal) shows that middle's individuals are minor's individuals. Result: major description separates from some of the minor's essence, yielding a non-permanent negative conclusion.
The conclusion is اللادوام.
4
Apply condition when minor is from special types
If the minor premise is from the special types, it judges separation of the descriptions of major and middle from all individuals of the middle (which are some of the minor). The major premise (normal) judges that the middle description is necessary for the major. This also forces separation of the major from the minor definitely, again yielding a non-permanent conclusion.
Author emphasizes that this is definite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حاكمة باجتماع وصفي الأكبر والأوسط في الأفراد، وكذا الصغرى باجتماع وصفي الأوسط والأصغر في جميع أفراد الأوسط التي أفراد الأكبر بعضها (۱) ، فيلزم مقارنة وصفي الأصغر والأكبر فيه، فلزم ثبوت الأكبر لبعض الأصغر حين هو أصغر . ثم إن كان الكبرى من إحدى الخاصتين فقد حكمت بمفارقة الوصفين عن تلك الأفراد ؛ لأن وصفي الخاصتين من الأعراض المفارقة عن الذات، وقد حكم الصغرى بكون أفراد الأوسط أفرادا للأصغر، فوجب مفارقة وصف الأكبر عن بعض ذات الأصغر ، وهو اللادوام ، وإن كانت الصغرى من إحدى الخاصتين والكبرى كما فرضت فالكبرى حکمت بلزوم وصف الأوسط للأكبر في الأفراد، والصغرى حكمت بمفارقة وصفي الأكبر والأصغر عن جميع أفراد الأوسط، التي هي بعض أفراد الأصغر ، فلزم مفارقة الأكبر فيها قطعا. فقد ظهر أن الحينية اللادائمة لازمة، إذا كان إحدى المقدمتين من إحدى الخاصتين، لا كما زعم البعض أن النتيجة حينية مطلقة ؛ لأن اللادوام سالبة غير منعكسة ؛ فإن عدم إنتاج قضية انفرادا لا يستلزم عدم إنتاجها حين كونها جزء لقضية أخرى. وأما في الضرب الثالث فلا يخلو إما أن يكون الصغرى دائمة، فحينئذ حاكمة بأن ذات الأوسط غير ذات الأصغر ، والكبرى لكونها فعلية حكمت هي بأن ذات الأكبر من جملة ذات الأوسط، فذات الأكبر غير ذات الأصغر، فوصف الأكبر مسلوب عن ذات الأصغر دائما. وإما أن يكون الكبرى فقط دائمة، وحينئذ قد حكمت بأن وصف الأوسط لازم لذات الأكبر، والصغرى لكونها وصفية حكمت بمنافاة وصف الأصغر لوصف الأوسط في ذاته، فلزم مغايرة ذات الأكبر لذات الأصغر، وإلا اجتمع وصف الأوسط ووصف الأصغر ، فلزم سلب وصف الأكبر عن ذات الأصغر دائما. وإما أن لا يكون واحدة منهما دائمة، وحينئذ فالكبرى لكونها وصفية حكمت بلزوم وصف الأوسط لوصف الأكبر في ذاته التي هي من جملة ذات الأوسط، والصغرى حكمت بمنافاة وصف الأصغر لوصف الأوسط، وإذا نافى اللازم الشيء فالملزوم أيضا نافاه ، فوصف الأكبر مناف لوصف الأصغر، فلزم سلب الأكبر عن ذات الأصغر ما دام الأصغر. ثم يقول هذا العبد إن الصغرى إذا كانت من إحدى الخاصتين فحينئذ ذات الأوسط من جملة ذات الأصغر ، والكبرى حاكمة بصدق وصف الأكبر بعقد الوضع عليها، فيلزم صدق وصف الأكبر على بعض ذات الأصغر، فحينئذ يصدق اللادوام في البعض في النتيجة ، فافهم. وأما الرابع والخامس فإن كان الكبرى دائمة فقد حكمت بتغاير ذاتي الأكبر والأوسط، والصغرى حاكمة بأن الأوسط والأصغر ملتقيان في ذات، تلك الذات، أي عن فتلك الذات مغايرة لذات الأكبر بديهة فيلزم سلبه عن تلك الذات، بعض الأصغر دائما ، وإن لم يكن دائمة فهي لكونها وصفية حاكمة بالمنافاة بين وصفي الأكبر والأوسط، فلا يجتمع مع وصف الأكبر في ذات، فما دام يصدق وصف الأوسط على ذات الأصغر يكون الأكبر مسلوبا، فالنتيجة كعكس الصغرى لازمة فظهر من ههنا أن الصغرى إن كانت من إحدى الخاصتين فالنتيجة مقيدة باللادوام وقولهم اللاوام سالبة مطلقة، وهي غير منتجة، ففساد ؛ لأن حكم الاجتماع والانفراد مما يختلف.
Judging by the conjunction of the descriptions of the major and the middle in individuals, and likewise the minor by the conjunction of the descriptions of the middle and the minor in all individuals of the middle which are some of the individuals of the major, so the conjunction of the descriptions of the minor and major follows in it, so the affirmation of the major for some of the minor when it is minor follows. Then if the major premise is from one of the two special types, it has judged the separation of the two descriptions from those individuals; because the descriptions of the two special types are accidental accidents separating from the essence, and the minor has judged that the individuals of the middle are individuals of the minor, so separation of the description of the major from some essence of the minor is necessary, and that is the non-permanent. And if the minor premise is from one of the two special types and the major premise as assumed, then the major premise has judged the necessity of the description of the middle for the major in individuals, and the minor has judged the separation of the descriptions of the major and minor from all individuals of the middle, which are some individuals of the minor, so the separation of the major from them definitely follows. So it has become clear that the non-permanent existential conclusion is necessary, when one of the two premises is from one of the two special types, not as some claim that the conclusion is absolute existential, because non-permanent is a negative non-convertible; for the non-production of a proposition individually does not entail its non-production when it is part of another proposition. As for the third figure, it is either that the minor is permanent, then it judges that the essence of the middle is not the essence of the minor, and the major being actual judges that the essence of the major is from the whole of the essence of the middle, so the essence of the major is not the essence of the minor, so the description of the major is negated from the essence of the minor always. Or that the major alone is permanent, then it has judged that the description of the middle is necessary for the essence of the major, and the minor being descriptive has judged the incompatibility of the description of the minor with the description of the middle in its essence, so the difference of the essence of the major from the essence of the minor follows; otherwise the description of the middle and the description of the minor would combine, so the negation of the description of the major from the essence of the minor always follows. Or that neither of them is permanent, then the major being descriptive judges the necessity of the description of the middle for the description of the major in its essence which is from the whole of the essence of the middle, and the minor judges the incompatibility of the description of the minor with the description of the middle, and if the necessary is incompatible with a thing, then the necessitating is also incompatible with it, so the description of the major is incompatible with the description of the minor, so the negation of the major from the essence of the minor follows as long as the minor exists. Then this servant says: if the minor is from one of the two special types, then the essence of the middle is from the whole of the essence of the minor, and the major judges the truth of the description of the major by placing it upon it, so the truth of the description of the major for some essence of the minor follows, then the non-permanent is true for some in the conclusion, so understand. As for the fourth and fifth, if the major is permanent, it has judged the difference of the essences of the major and middle, and the minor judges that the middle and minor meet in an essence, that essence, i.e., so that essence is different from the essence of the major by necessity, so the negation of it from that essence, some of the minor always follows; and if it is not permanent, being descriptive it judges the incompatibility between the descriptions of the major and middle, so it does not combine with the description of the major in an essence, so as long as the description of the middle is true of the essence of the minor, the major is negated, so the conclusion is like the converse of the minor necessary. So it has become clear from here that if the minor is from one of the two special types, the conclusion is restricted to non-permanent, and their statement 'the non-permanent is absolute negative' and it is non-productive, so it is corrupt; because the judgment of conjunction and separation differs.
Source pages p. 531

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on mixed syllogisms (المختلطات), specifically addressing when premises of different types (permanent, actual/descriptive, special) produce non-permanent (اللادوام) conclusions. The author follows the order of cases: first, when the major premise is from one of the 'two special types' (possibly meaning accidental or separable attributes), it forces separation of the major description from the minor's essence, yielding a non-permanent negative conclusion. Second, when the minor premise is from the special types, the major premise (as normally assumed) implies the opposite, but the minor's separative nature again yields a definite non-permanent separation. Third, the third figure (al-darb al-thalith) is examined with three subcases: (a) minor permanent → major actual → essence of major not identical to minor → permanent negation; (b) major permanent alone → minor descriptive → permanent negation due to incompatibility; (c) neither permanent → both descriptive → incompatibility transfers to the major, yielding negation as long as the minor persists. The author then adds a note from his own pen ('this servant') clarifying that when the minor is from the special types, the conclusion's non-permanent applies to some individuals. Finally, the fourth and fifth figures (presumably the fourth and fifth moods of the mixed syllogism) are analyzed: if the major is permanent, it forces difference of essences leading to permanent negation; if not permanent and descriptive, it yields a conclusion that is the converse of the minor. The author concludes that when the minor is from the special types, the conclusion is indeed non-permanent, contrary to those who claim it is absolute negative (which would be unproductive). The key logical principle here is that the nature of the premises (permanent/descriptive/actual) determines whether the syllogism yields a permanent or non-permanent conclusion. The commentary uses examples from the logical tradition (not specified here) to illustrate the interaction of essential and accidental attributes.

Key Terms

دائمة
خاصتين
لادوام

According to the passage, what happens when the major premise is from one of the two special types (khāṣṣatayn)?

Key Takeaway
When one premise is from the special (accidental) types, the conclusion is necessarily non-permanent (lā-dawām).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Consider this mixed syllogism: 'Every muʼmin (believer) is righteous while believing (special type, accidental). Some muʼmin are wealthy. Is the conclusion 'Some wealthy are righteous while believing' permanent or non-permanent? Explain using the conditions from this lesson.

Third Figure Mixed Syllogisms: Subcases and Beyond
Author examines conditions for non-permanent conclusions in mixed syllogisms, focusing on third figure subcases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the conclusion type (permanent or non-permanent) for each subcase of the third figure mixed syllogism, and also for the fourth and fifth figures as outlined by the author.

Core Idea
In the third figure, the modality of premises (permanent, descriptive, actual) dictates whether the conclusion is permanent or non-permanent, with three distinct subcases.

The passage then turns to the third figure (الضرب الثالث) of mixed syllogisms, analyzing three subcases: (1) minor permanent, (2) major permanent alone, (3) neither permanent. Each subcase yields a different type of conclusion. Additionally, the author addresses the fourth and fifth figures and offers a final clarification regarding non-permanent conclusions. We will unpack these details systematically.

Guided Path
1
Subcase 1: Minor permanent, major actual
When the minor is permanent (دائمة), it judges that the essence of the middle (أوسط) is not the essence of the minor (أصغر). The major premise, being actual (فعلية), judges that the essence of the major (أكبر) is part of the essence of the middle. Hence, the major's essence is distinct from the minor's essence, leading to a permanent negation of the major description from the minor's essence (always).
Permanent negation: the major is never predicated of the minor.
2
Subcase 2: Major permanent alone, minor descriptive
The major permanent judges that the description of the middle is necessary for the essence of the major. The minor descriptive (وصفية) judges that the description of the minor is incompatible with the description of the middle in essence. From these, it follows that the major's essence is different from the minor's essence, otherwise the incompatible descriptions would combine. Thus, permanent negation of the major from the minor again follows.
Again permanent negation, because the incompatibility is essential.
3
Subcase 3: Neither permanent (both descriptive or other)
If neither premise is permanent, e.g., both descriptive: the major (descriptive) judges that the description of the middle is necessary for the description of the major. The minor (descriptive) judges that the description of the minor is incompatible with the description of the middle. Incompatibility transfers: if the necessary (lazim) is incompatible with something, the necessitating (malzum) is also incompatible. So the major description is incompatible with the minor description. Hence, the major is negated from the minor as long as the minor exists (non-permanent negation).
Non-permanent (لادوام): negation holds only during the existence of the minor.
4
Fourth and fifth figures and final remark
For the fourth and fifth figures (الرابع والخامس), the passage explains: if the major is permanent, it judges the essences of major and middle are different; the minor judges that middle and minor meet in an essence, which is thus different from the major's essence, leading to permanent negation. If the major is not permanent (descriptive), it judges incompatibility between descriptions of major and middle, so as long as the middle describes the minor, the major is negated, giving a conclusion that is the 'converse of the minor' (عكس الصغرى). The author concludes that when the minor is from the special types, the conclusion is indeed non-permanent, contrary to those who claim it is absolute negative and unproductive.
The author emphasizes that conjunction and separation (اجتماع وانفراد) differ, so the non-permanent conclusion is productive, not absolute.

Source pages p. 531

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on mixed syllogisms (المختلطات), specifically addressing when premises of different types (permanent, actual/descriptive, special) produce non-permanent (اللادوام) conclusions. The author follows the order of cases: first, when the major premise is from one of the 'two special types' (possibly meaning accidental or separable attributes), it forces separation of the major description from the minor's essence, yielding a non-permanent negative conclusion. Second, when the minor premise is from the special types, the major premise (as normally assumed) implies the opposite, but the minor's separative nature again yields a definite non-permanent separation. Third, the third figure (al-darb al-thalith) is examined with three subcases: (a) minor permanent → major actual → essence of major not identical to minor → permanent negation; (b) major permanent alone → minor descriptive → permanent negation due to incompatibility; (c) neither permanent → both descriptive → incompatibility transfers to the major, yielding negation as long as the minor persists. The author then adds a note from his own pen ('this servant') clarifying that when the minor is from the special types, the conclusion's non-permanent applies to some individuals. Finally, the fourth and fifth figures (presumably the fourth and fifth moods of the mixed syllogism) are analyzed: if the major is permanent, it forces difference of essences leading to permanent negation; if not permanent and descriptive, it yields a conclusion that is the converse of the minor. The author concludes that when the minor is from the special types, the conclusion is indeed non-permanent, contrary to those who claim it is absolute negative (which would be unproductive). The key logical principle here is that the nature of the premises (permanent/descriptive/actual) determines whether the syllogism yields a permanent or non-permanent conclusion. The commentary uses examples from the logical tradition (not specified here) to illustrate the interaction of essential and accidental attributes.

General Rule for Mixed Syllogisms
Author introduces mixed syllogisms and derives rules for descriptive and non-persistent premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the modal status and polarity of the conclusion in a mixed syllogism given the modal types of the premises.

Core Idea
In mixed syllogisms, the modal status of premises dictates the conclusion's modality and polarity.

Mixed syllogisms (المختلطات) combine a descriptive premise (وصفية) with a non-persistent premise (غير دائمة). The author explains how the modal properties of the premises determine whether the conclusion is negative or positive, and persistent or non-persistent. Understanding this is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence where premises may have different temporal scopes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modal status of each premise
Determine which premise is descriptive (holds as long as the subject exists) and which is non-persistent (not always true). In the passage, either the major or minor may be descriptive.
A descriptive premise is called 'وصفية'.
2
Apply the rule for the case where one premise is descriptive and the other non-persistent
If the major is descriptive and the minor is non-persistent, the description of the major is negated from the essence of the minor perpetually (negative persistent conclusion). If neither is persistent, the major is negated from the minor as long as the minor exists (negative non-persistent conclusion).
The passage says: 'فلزم سلب الأكبر عن ذات الأصغر دائما' vs 'ما دام الأصغر'.
3
Recognize the special case when the minor is one of the two special premises
If the minor is from the two special premises (where the middle is part of the minor's essence), then the conclusion becomes positive non-persistent: the major is affirmed for some part of the minor.
The author says: 'إذا كانت الصغرى من إحدى الخاصتين... يصدق اللادوام في البعض في النتيجة'.
4
Formulate the conclusion with the correct modality
Based on the case, write the conclusion as either 'No S is P' (negative) or 'Some S is P' (positive), and specify whether it holds 'always' (دائما) or 'as long as S exists' (ما دام الأصغر).
