Commentary
This passage is an explanation of mixed syllogisms (المختلطات), which combine a descriptive (وصفية) and a non-persistent premise. The author first states a rule: when both premises are such that one is descriptive and the other not persistent, a negative conclusion follows. He explains that if the major is descriptive and the minor is not persistent, the major rules incompatibility between the middle and minor descriptions, leading to perpetual negation of the major from the minor. This is a detailed case analysis of mixed syllogisms, showing how the modal status of premises affects the conclusion.

Fourth and Fifth Mixed Syllogisms: Persistence of the Major
Author analyzes the fourth and fifth mixed syllogisms, considering whether the major is persistent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the conclusion of a fourth or fifth mixed syllogism by analyzing whether the major premise is persistent.

Core Idea
In mixed syllogisms, the persistence of the major determines whether the conclusion is perpetual or restricted.

This lesson delves into the fourth and fifth types of mixed syllogisms, where the author examines two subcases based on whether the major premise is persistent (دائمة) or not. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly deriving the conclusion's modality, especially when dealing with special premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two subcases
The passage distinguishes between when the major premise is persistent (دائمة) and when it is not. Note that persistence here means the proposition holds for all times.
2
Analyze the persistent major case
If the major is persistent, it asserts otherness (تغاير) of the essences of the major and middle terms. The minor premise asserts that the middle and minor meet in an essence. Since that essence is distinct from the major's essence, the major is always negated from that part of the minor, yielding a perpetual negative conclusion.
The conclusion is: some of the minor is always not the major (سلب دائما).
3
Analyze the non-persistent major case
If the major is not persistent, it is descriptive and indicates incompatibility between the descriptions of the major and middle. Thus, wherever the middle applies to the minor, the major is negated; the conclusion is restricted to the duration of the middle's applicability—a non-persistent negative.
The conclusion is like the converse of the minor: the negation holds as long as the middle holds.
4
Consider the special premises
When the minor is one of the two special premises (الخاصتين), the conclusion is further restricted to non-persistence (لادوام). The author criticizes the claim that a non-persistent absolute negative is unproductive, arguing it is valid because the rules of combination and separation differ.
Special premises refer to cases where the middle is part of the minor's essence, affecting the modality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الرابع والخامس فإن كان الكبرى دائمة فقد حكمت بتغاير ذاتي الأكبر والأوسط، والصغرى حاكمة بأن الأوسط والأصغر ملتقيان في ذات، تلك الذات مغايرة لذات الأكبر بديهة فيلزم سلبه عن تلك الذات، بعض الأصغر دائما، وإن لم يكن دائمة فهي لكونها وصفية حاكمة بالمنافاة بين وصفي الأكبر والأوسط، فلا يجتمع مع وصف الأكبر في ذات، فما دام يصدق وصف الأوسط على ذات الأصغر يكون الأكبر مسلوبا، فالنتيجة كعكس الصغرى لازمة فظهر من ههنا أن الصغرى إن كانت من إحدى الخاصتين فالنتيجة مقيدة باللادوام وقولهم اللادوام سالبة مطلقة، وهي غير منتجة، ففساد؛ لأن حكم الاجتماع والانفراد مما يختلف.
As for the fourth and fifth [mixed syllogisms], if the major is persistent, then it has ruled the otherness of the essences of the major and the middle, and the minor rules that the middle and the minor meet in an essence; that essence is evidently other than the essence of the major, so it is necessary to negate it from that essence, which is some of the minor always. And if it is not persistent, then because it is descriptive, it rules the incompatibility between the descriptions of the major and the middle, so it does not combine with the description of the major in an essence, so as long as the description of the middle is true of the essence of the minor, the major is negated, so the conclusion is like the converse of the minor, necessary. So it appears from here that if the minor is one of the two special [premises], the conclusion is restricted to non-persistence, and their statement 'the non-persistent is an absolute negative' is unproductive, so it is corrupt; because the rule of combination and separation differs.
Source pages p. 532

Commentary
This passage discusses the fourth and fifth types of mixed syllogisms. The author considers two subcases: First, if the major premise is persistent (دائمة), it asserts that the essences of the major and middle are different. The minor premise asserts that the middle and minor meet in an essence (i.e., the middle is predicated of some part of the minor). That essence is distinct from the major's essence, so the major is necessarily negated from that part of the minor, and this negation is perpetual (دائما). Second, if the major is not persistent, then because it is descriptive, it rules incompatibility between the major and middle descriptions. Thus, wherever the middle applies to the minor, the major is negated, resulting in a conclusion that is the converse of the minor (i.e., the negation of the major holds as long as the middle holds). The author then notes that if the minor is one of the two special premises (where the middle is part of the minor's essence), the conclusion becomes non-persistent (مقيدة باللادوام). He criticizes the view that a non-persistent absolute negative is unproductive, calling it corrupt, because the rules of combination and separation differ (i.e., the conclusion depends on the specific modal status).

The Sixth, Seventh, and Eighth Mixed Syllogisms
Author explains the sixth, seventh, and eighth mixed syllogisms and their conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate the sixth, seventh, and eighth mixed syllogisms and reconstruct their conclusions using the principles of accompaniment, incompatibility, and non-persistence.

Core Idea
Mixed syllogisms transfer incompatibility from the middle to the major under temporal conditions.

The author details three types of mixed syllogisms, each with a unique combination of premises: the sixth uses accompaniment in the major and incompatibility in the minor; the seventh uses incompatibility in the major and identity in the minor; the eighth uses accompaniment in the major and incompatibility in the minor. Understanding these is crucial for grasping modal arguments in Islamic logic, especially when premises involve non-persistent propositions. This lesson dissects each syllogism step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises of the sixth mixed syllogism
The major premise rules that the middle description necessarily accompanies the major description (luzum). The minor premise rules that the middle and minor descriptions are incompatible (munafaat) in some essences of the middle.
2
Apply non-persistence and the principle of incompatibility transfer
Since the minor is true of that part by non-persistence, that part is the essence of the minor. Then, using the principle that incompatibility of the necessary (middle) necessitates incompatibility of its necessitator (major), we derive that in that part the major and minor are incompatible.
3
Derive the conclusion of the sixth syllogism
Thus, some of the minor is not major as long as it is minor – a particular negative non-persistent proposition.
4
Analyze the seventh mixed syllogism
The major rules incompatibility between major and middle in an essence, but both are true of it at two different times. The minor rules that the essence of the middle is the essence of the minor. Therefore, in that essence (which is part of the minor) there is incompatibility between minor and major at times when the middle holds, but not always.
5
Analyze the eighth mixed syllogism
The major rules that the middle necessarily accompanies the major in an essence. The minor rules that the minor description is incompatible with the middle description in its essence. That essence is the essence of the minor by non-persistence, so minor and major are incompatible in it, though both are truly predicated of it at different times. The conclusion is a negative non-persistent proposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في السادس فلأن الكبرى حاكمة بلزوم وصف الأوسط لوصف الأكبر، والصغرى حكمت بالمنافاة بين وصفي الأوسط والأصغر في بعض ذوات الأوسط، ولما كان الأصغر يصدق عليها بحكم اللادوام كان ذلك البعض ذات الأصغر، فيجب المنافاة بين وصفي الأكبر والأصغر فيها؛ لأن منافاة اللازم تستلزم منافاة منافاة الملزوم، فيلزم صدق بعض الأصغر ليس أكبر ما دام أصغر.
وأما في السابع فلأن الكبرى حكمت بالمنافاة بين وصفي الأكبر والأوسط في ذات، مع صدقهما عليها في زمانين، والصغرى حكمت بأن ذات الأوسط ذات الأصغر، ولا بد من المنافاة بين وصف الأصغر ووصف الأكبر في نفس ذات الأوسط التي هي بعض ذات الأصغر في أوقات صدق الأوسط لا دائما، فلأن صدق الأوسط بالفعل لصدق الوجودية اللادائمة، وإن صدق في زمان الوصف صدق الحينية اللادائمة.
وأما في الثامن فلأن الكبرى قد حكمت بلزوم وصف الأوسط لوصف الأكبر في ذات، والصغرى بمنافاة وصف الأصغر لوصف الأوسط في ذاته، وتلك الذات ذات الأصغر بحكم اللادوام، فوصف الأصغر والأكبر متنافيان فيها، مع صدقهما عليها، وهو النتيجة. والله أعلم بحقيقة الحال.
(1) كذا في الأصل، وفي هامشه «بعض» بدل «نفس»، مشيرا إلى أنه نسخة مختلفة.
As for the sixth [mixed syllogism], because the major rules the necessary accompaniment of the description of the middle with the description of the major, and the minor has ruled the incompatibility between the descriptions of the middle and the minor in some of the essences of the middle, and since the minor is true of it by the rule of non-persistence, that part is the essence of the minor, so incompatibility between the descriptions of the major and the minor is necessary in it; because incompatibility of the necessary necessitates incompatibility of the necessitator, so some of the minor being not major follows as long as it is minor. As for the seventh, because the major has ruled the incompatibility between the descriptions of the major and the middle in an essence, with both being true of it at two times, and the minor has ruled that the essence of the middle is the essence of the minor, and there must be incompatibility between the description of the minor and the description of the major in the very essence of the middle which is some of the essence of the minor at times of the truth of the middle not always, because the truth of the middle actually is for the truth of the non-persistent existential, and if it is true at the time of description, the truth of the non-persistent conditional. As for the eighth, because the major has ruled the necessary accompaniment of the description of the middle with the description of the major in an essence, and the minor rules the incompatibility of the description of the minor with the description of the middle in its essence, and that essence is the essence of the minor by the rule of non-persistence, so the description of the minor and the major are incompatible in it, with both being true of it, and that is the conclusion. And God knows best the reality of the situation.
(1) Thus in the original, and in its margin 'some' instead of 'very', indicating a different manuscript.
Source pages p. 533

Commentary
This passage explains the sixth, seventh, and eighth mixed syllogisms in detail. For the sixth: The major premise asserts that the middle description necessarily accompanies the major. The minor premise rules the incompatibility between the middle and minor descriptions in some essences of the middle. Since the minor is true of that part (by non-persistence), that part is the essence of the minor. Therefore, the major and minor descriptions are incompatible there, because if the necessary (middle) is incompatible with something, its necessitator (major) is also incompatible. The conclusion is that some of the minor is not major as long as it is minor (i.e., a negative non-persistent). For the seventh: The major rules incompatibility between major and middle descriptions in an essence, but both are true of it at two different times (i.e., they are temporally alternating). The minor rules that the essence of the middle is the essence of the minor. Thus, in that very essence (which is part of the minor), there is incompatibility between minor and major at times when the middle is true, but not always. The reason is that the truth of the middle is actual due to the truth of the non-persistent existential proposition, and if it is true at a time of description, it follows the non-persistent conditional. For the eighth: The major rules the middle necessarily accompanies the major in an essence. The minor rules incompatibility between minor and middle descriptions in the middle's essence. That essence is the minor's essence by non-persistence, so minor and major are incompatible there, even though both are truly predicated of it (at different times). The conclusion is a negative non-persistent. A footnote mentions a variant reading: 'some' (بعض) instead of 'very' (نفس) in the margin.

Key Terms
السادس (al-sādis): The sixth type of mixed syllogism, where the major rules accompaniment and the minor rules incompatibility. [Note: Refers to a numerical classification in this commentary.]
منافاة اللازم تستلزم منافاة الملزوم (munāfāt al-lāzim tastalzim munāfāt al-malzūm): The principle that if the necessary (a concomitant) is incompatible with a thing, then its necessitator (that which it accompanies) is also incompatible. [Note: Standard logical principle.]
الوجودية اللادائمة (al-wujūdiyya al-lā dāʾima): A non-persistent existential proposition; one that holds at some times but not all. [Note: Standard modal classification.]
الحينية اللادائمة (al-ḥīniyya al-lā dāʾima): A non-persistent temporal proposition; one that holds only at a specific time. [Note: Less common term; appears in this commentary.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

The Sixth Figure of Categorical Syllogism
Explains the sixth, seventh, and eighth figures of the categorical syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the logical reasoning of the sixth figure of the categorical syllogism and verify its validity.

Core Idea
In the sixth figure, incompatibility between minor and major follows from incompatibility with the middle that necessarily implies the major.

In this lesson, we delve into the sixth figure of the categorical syllogism as explained in the source text. The passage describes a unique syllogistic form where the major premise establishes a necessary connection between the middle and major terms, while the minor premise notes an incompatibility between the middle and minor terms in some individuals. The conclusion is derived by leveraging the concept of non-permanence (al-lā-dawām).

Guided Path
1
Identify the major premise
The major premise states that the middle term's description necessarily accompanies the major term's description: 'الكبرى حاكمة بلزوم وصف الأوسط لوصف الأكبر'.
This is a conditional-like necessary connection.
2
Identify the minor premise
The minor premise judges incompatibility between the middle and minor descriptions in some individuals of the middle: 'الصغرى حكمت بالمنافاة بين وصفي الأوسط والأصغر في بعض ذوات الأوسط'.
Incompatibility means they cannot coexist in the same instance.
3
Understand non-permanence
The minor is true of those individuals by non-permanence, so that 'some' is actually an individual of the minor.
Non-permanence allows the minor to apply at some times but not others.
4
Apply the logical principle
The text uses the principle: 'منافاة اللازم تستلزم منافاة منافاة الملزوم' (incompatibility of the necessary entails incompatibility of the necessitated).
This links the premises to the conclusion.
5
Derive the conclusion
The conclusion is that some minor is not major as long as it is minor: 'فيجب المنافاة بين وصفي الأكبر والأصغر فيها'.
The temporal qualification 'as long as it is minor' is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في السادس فلأن الكبرى حاكمة بلزوم وصف الأوسط لوصف الأكبر، والصغرى حكمت بالمنافاة بين وصفي الأوسط والأصغر في بعض ذوات الأوسط، ولما كان الأصغر يصدق عليها بحكم اللادوام كان ذلك البعض ذات الأصغر، فيجب المنافاة بين وصفي الأكبر والأصغر فيها؛ لأن منافاة اللازم تستلزم منافاة منافاة الملزوم، فيلزم صدق بعض الأصغر ليس أكبر ما دام أصغر .
وأما في السابع فلأن الكبرى حكمت بالمنافاة بين وصفي الأكبر والأوسط في ذات ، مع صدقهما عليها في زمانين ، والصغرى حكمت بأن ذات الأوسط ذات الأصغر، ولا بد من المنافاة بين وصف الأصغر ووصف الأكبر في نفس ذات الأوسط التي هي بعض ذات الأصغر في أوقات صدق الأوسط لا دائما ، فلأن صدق الأوسط بالفعل لصدق الوجودية اللادائمة ، وإن صدق في زمان الوصف صدق الحينية اللادائمة.
(1)
وأما في الثامن فلأن الكبرى قد حكمت بلزوم وصف الأوسط لوصف الأكبر في ذات، والصغرى بمنافاة وصف الأصغر لوصف الأوسط في ذاته، وتلك الذات ذات الأصغر بحكم اللادوام، فوصف الأصغر والأكبر متنافيان فيها، مع صدقهما عليها، وهو النتيجة . والله أعلم بحقيقة الحال.
(1) كذا في الأصل ، وفي هامشه «بعض» بدل «نفس»، مشيرا إلى أنه نسخة مختلفة.
As for the sixth: because the major premise judges that the middle term's description necessarily accompanies the major term's description, and the minor premise judges incompatibility between the descriptions of the middle and minor in some individuals of the middle. And since the minor is true of them by virtue of non-permanence, that 'some' is an individual of the minor, so incompatibility between the descriptions of the major and minor is necessary there, because incompatibility of the necessary entails incompatibility of the necessary of the necessitated. Therefore some minor is not major as long as it is minor.
As for the seventh: because the major premise judges incompatibility between the descriptions of the major and middle in an individual, while they are both true of it at two times, and the minor premise judges that the individual of the middle is an individual of the minor. Incompatibility between the description of the minor and the description of the major is necessary in the very individual of the middle that is some individual of the minor at times when the middle is true, not always, because the truth of the middle is actual due to the truth of the non-permanent existential, and if true at the time of description, it is the truth of the non-permanent 'when'-proposition.
As for the eighth: because the major premise has judged that the middle's description necessarily accompanies the major's description in an individual, and the minor judges incompatibility of the minor's description with the middle's description in its individual, and that individual is an individual of the minor by non-permanence. So the descriptions of the minor and major are incompatible in it, while they are both true of it, and that is the conclusion. And God knows best the reality of the matter.
(1) Thus in the original, and in its margin 'بعض' instead of 'نفس', indicating a different copy.
Source pages p. 533

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on three specific figures (sixth, seventh, eighth) of the categorical syllogism. The author explains why each figure yields a valid conclusion. For the sixth figure: the major premise states a necessary connection between middle and major (if middle, then major), and the minor states an incompatibility between middle and minor in some instances. Since the minor applies not permanently, the 'some' instances where the middle is incompatible are themselves instances of the minor (because the minor is true of them). Therefore, by the principle that what is incompatible with the necessary is incompatible with the necessitated, those instances of the minor are not major as long as they are minor. For the seventh figure: the major states incompatibility between major and middle in one individual at different times, and the minor identifies that individual as part of the minor. Since the middle is true at one time but not necessarily permanently, there must be a time when the middle's truth coincides with the minor's description, and at that time the major and minor are incompatible, yielding the conclusion. For the eighth figure: the major states a necessary connection between middle and major, and the minor states incompatibility between minor and middle in the individual. By non-permanence, that individual is an instance of the minor, so the minor and major are incompatible in it, though both can be true of it (at different times or aspects). This yields the conclusion. The footnote clarifies a textual variant in the margin.

The Syllogism

The Seventh Figure of Categorical Syllogism
Explains the sixth, seventh, and eighth figures of the categorical syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the seventh figure of the categorical syllogism and identify the role of temporal truth in its validity.

Core Idea
The seventh figure uses incompatibility between major and middle at different times to derive a temporal incompatibility between minor and major.

This lesson examines the seventh figure, where the major premise states incompatibility between the major and middle descriptions in an individual, but they are true at different times. The minor premise identifies that individual as belonging to the minor. The conclusion establishes a temporal incompatibility between the minor and major.

Guided Path
1
Identify the major premise
The major premise judges incompatibility between the major and middle descriptions in a single individual, though both are true at different times: 'الكبرى حكمت بالمنافاة بين وصفي الأكبر والأوسط في ذات، مع صدقهما عليها في زمانين'.
The same individual cannot have both descriptions simultaneously.
2
Identify the minor premise
The minor premise judges that the individual of the middle is an individual of the minor: 'والصغرى حكمت بأن ذات الأوسط ذات الأصغر'.
This links the middle and minor.
3
Understand non-permanence in the middle's truth
The middle is true of that individual actually but not permanently (non-permanent existential), so at times when the middle is true, the individual is described by the middle.
The minor is true of that individual by non-permanence as well.
4
Derive the temporal incompatibility
In the very individual that is both an instance of the middle (at some time) and an instance of the minor, there must be incompatibility between the minor and major descriptions at those times, because the major is incompatible with the middle that is present.
The conclusion is that the minor and major are incompatible in that individual at times when the middle is true, but not always.

As for the seventh: because the major premise judges incompatibility between the descriptions of the major and middle in an individual, while they are both true of it at two times, and the minor premise judges that the individual of the middle is an individual of the minor. The Eighth Figure of Categorical Syllogism
Explains the sixth, seventh, and eighth figures of the categorical syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the eighth figure derives a conclusion from a necessary connection between middle and major and an incompatibility between minor and middle.

Core Idea
The eighth figure combines necessary accompaniment with incompatibility to yield a conclusion of incompatibility between minor and major in an individual.

This lesson covers the eighth figure, where the major premise states a necessary connection between the middle and major descriptions in an individual, and the minor premise states incompatibility between the minor and middle descriptions in that same individual. By non-permanence, that individual is an instance of the minor, leading to incompatibility between minor and major in that individual.

Guided Path
1
Identify the major premise
The major premise judges that the middle's description necessarily accompanies the major's description in an individual: 'الكبرى قد حكمت بلزوم وصف الأوسط لوصف الأكبر في ذات'.
This is a necessary connection in a specific individual.
2
Identify the minor premise
The minor premise judges incompatibility between the minor's description and the middle's description in that same individual: 'والصغرى بمنافاة وصف الأصغر لوصف الأوسط في ذاته'.
The minor is incompatible with the middle in that individual.
3
Determine the individual's belonging to the minor
By non-permanence, that individual is an individual of the minor: 'وتلك الذات ذات الأصغر بحكم اللادوام'.
The minor applies to that individual at some times.
4
Derive the conclusion
Since the middle necessarily implies the major in that individual, and the minor is incompatible with the middle, the minor must also be incompatible with the major in that individual.
The conclusion is that minor and major are incompatible in that individual, even though both can be true of it (at different times or aspects).

The Syllogism

The Five Premise Combinations of Conditional Conjunctive Syllogism
Lists possible premise types for the conditional conjunctive syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five possible premise combinations of a conditional conjunctive syllogism and recognize the shared complete part condition.

Core Idea
Conditional conjunctive syllogisms mix conditionals, disjunctives, and categoricals in five specific ways.

Just as the categorical syllogism uses only categorical premises, the conditional conjunctive syllogism introduces conditionals (if-then) and disjunctives (either-or). The author lists exactly five combinations of these proposition types. Understanding these combinations is the first step to constructing and analyzing conditional syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition
The conditional conjunctive syllogism contains at least one conditional (muttaṣila) or disjunctive (munfaṣila) premise. This distinguishes it from the categorical syllogism.
2
Identify the five combinations
Find and list the five combinations explicitly given in the passage: (1) two conditionals, (2) two disjunctives, (3) a categorical and a conditional, (4) a categorical and a disjunctive, (5) a conditional and a disjunctive.
Each combination is illustrated with an example from the text.
3
Check the shared part condition
The author states that productivity requires the two premises to share a complete part (juzʾ tāmm), meaning a whole proposition or term that connects them. Verify this in the examples.
For instance, in combination (1), the middle proposition 'daytime exists' is shared.
4
Confirm figures are analogous
Notice that the four figures of the categorical syllogism also apply here, with the first figure being the principal one. This will be elaborated later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في القياس الاقتراني الشرطي
ولما فرغ المصنف عن القياس الاقتراني الحملي شرع في القياس الاقتراني الشرطي، وقال: (ثُمَّ الشَّرْطِيُّ يتركب من متصلتين) كلّما نحو كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود، وكلَّما كان النهار موجودا فالعالم مضيئ» ، أَوْ مُنْفَصِلَتَيْن)، نحو العدد إما فرد وإما زوج، والزوج إما زوج الزوج وإما زوج الفرد» ، أو حملية ومتصلة) . نحو «كلما كان زيد إنسانا كان حيوانا، وكل حيوان جسم»، (أو حملية ومنفصلة). نحو «العدد إما زوج وإما د، وكل فرد غير منقسم بمتساويين» ، أو متصلة ومنفصلة). نحو «كلما كان الشيء مركبا من الوحدات كان عددا ، ودائما العدد إما زوج وإما فرد». وَيَنْعَقِدُ فِيهِ الْأَشْكَالُ الْأَربعةُ ، والعمدة الأول). كما في الحملي. وَالْمَطْبُوعُ مِنْهُ اشْتِرَاكُ الْمُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ فِي جُزْء تام، وشرائط الإنتاح وحال النتيجة فيه كما في الحملية.
Chapter on the Conditional Conjunctive Syllogism.
When the author finished the categorical conjunctive syllogism, he began the conditional conjunctive syllogism, and said: 'Then the conditional [syllogism] is composed of two conditionals [universal] – e.g., 'Whenever the sun is rising, then daytime exists' and 'Whenever daytime exists, then the world is illuminated' – or two disjunctives – e.g., 'A number is either even or odd' and 'The even is either evenly-even or evenly-odd' – or a categorical and a conditional – e.g., 'Whenever Zayd is a human, he is an animal' and 'Every animal is a body' – or a categorical and a disjunctive – e.g., 'A number is either even or odd' and 'Every odd is not divisible into two equals' – or a conditional and a disjunctive – e.g., 'Whenever something is composed of units, it is a number' and 'Always a number is either even or odd'. And the four figures occur in it, and the first is the principal one, as in the categorical. And the natural thing in it is the sharing of both premises in a complete part, and the conditions for productivity and the state of the conclusion in it are as in the categoricals.'
Source pages p. 534

Commentary
This passage introduces the conditional conjunctive syllogism (al-qiyās al-iqṭirānī al-sharṭī), which is a syllogism composed of at least one conditional premise. The author first explains that after completing the categorical conjunctive syllogism, the text moves to the conditional type. Five possible combinations of premises are listed: 1) two universal conditional (muttaṣila) propositions, e.g., 'Whenever the sun is rising, day exists' and 'Whenever day exists, the world is illuminated'; 2) two disjunctive (munfaṣila) propositions, e.g., 'A number is either even or odd' and 'The even is either evenly-even or evenly-odd'; 3) a categorical and a conditional, e.g., 'Whenever Zayd is a man, he is an animal' and 'Every animal is a body'; 4) a categorical and a disjunctive; and 5) a conditional and a disjunctive. The author then notes that the four figures of the categorical syllogism apply here as well, with the first figure being the primary one. The condition for productivity is that the two premises share a complete part (juzʾ tāmm), meaning a whole proposition or term, and the conditions for productivity and the nature of the conclusion are analogous to categorical syllogisms.

Key Terms
القياس الاقتراني الشرطي
متصلة
منفصلة

D) A categorical and a conditional

Key Takeaway
A conditional conjunctive syllogism must have at least one conditional or disjunctive premise, and the premises must share a complete part for the conclusion to be productive.

Construct your own example of a conditional conjunctive syllogism using two disjunctive premises from fiqh (e.g., concerning types of contracts).

Problematizing the First Figure of Conditional Syllogism
Raises a doubt about the first figure's productivity and critiques a proposed solution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structural flaw in a conditional syllogism that appears to have true premises but a false conclusion, and evaluate the adequacy of the proposed solution (denying the necessity of the major premise).

Core Idea
A false conclusion from true premises reveals a flaw in the syllogism, not just in the classification of premises.

This passage tackles a doubt (shakk) about the productivity of the first figure in conditional syllogisms. The doubt is that from two seemingly true conditional premises, a false conclusion follows. The proposed solution – that the major premise is not necessary but accidental – is then rejected. We'll walk through the logical structure and see why the solution fails.

Guided Path
1
Restate the claimed principle
The author states that in the first figure of conditional syllogisms (two conditional premises sharing a middle term in the antecedent), if both premises are necessary (luzūmiyya), the conclusion should also be necessary. This is the principle under doubt.
2
Examine the counterexample
Identify the two premises: (1) 'Whenever two is odd, it is a number' and (2) 'Whenever it is a number, it is even'. Both are considered true (the first is vacuously true since the antecedent is false, but in classical logic, the conditional is true). The conclusion is 'Whenever two is odd, it is even', which the author says is false (because there is no necessary connection between oddness and evenness).
3
Analyze the proposed solution
The solution says that the major premise (2) is not necessary (luzūmiyya) but merely accidental (ittifāqiyya). That is, the connection between 'being a number' and 'being even' is not necessary; it just happens that two is even. So the major is not necessary, so the principle does not apply.
4
Critique the solution
The author argues that even if the major is accidental, it becomes a specific accidental (ittifāqiyya khāṣṣa) because the antecedent and consequent are both true (two is a number and two is even). But that doesn't help; the conclusion 'Whenever two is odd, it is even' has a false antecedent, so the conclusion is false. The agreement of the consequent with the antecedent in the specific instance does not guarantee the productivity of the syllogism.
The author says 'the agreement of an agreeing party is necessary, and here the conclusion is necessarily false.'
5
Identify the deeper issue
The real problem is that the first figure of conditional syllogisms requires the premises to be truly necessary in terms of meaning, not just coincidentally true. The example shows that even if both premises are true, the conclusion can be false because the antecedent of the minor is false and the connection in the major is not universal. The solution misses this point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنتاج اللزوميتين لزومية في الشكل الأول بين وههنا شك، وهو أنه يصدق كلما كان الاثنان فردا كان عددا، وكلما كان عددا كان زوجا، مع كذب النتيجة، وهي قولنا «كلما كان الاثنان فردا كان زوجا» . (وَحَلُهُ - كَمَا قِيلَ : مَنْعُ كَوْنِ الْكُبْرَى لُزُومِيَّةَ ، وَإِنَّمَا هِي اتفاقية ) . وهذا لا ينفع ؛ فإن الكبرى لو كانت اتفاقية لكانت اتفاقية خاصة لصدق المقدم والتالي، وهي النتيجة، لكن الإنتاج غير معتبر ؛ فإن موافقة موافق الموافق ضرورية، وههنا يلزم كذب النتيجة .
So the production of two necessary [premises] yields a necessary [conclusion] in the first figure, but here there is a doubt: that it is true that 'Whenever two is odd, it is a number' and 'Whenever it is a number, it is even', while the conclusion is false, namely our statement 'Whenever two is odd, it is even'. Its solution – as is said: the denial that the major is necessary, rather it is accidental. But this is not useful; for if the major were accidental, it would be a specific accidental because of the truth of the antecedent and consequent, and that is the conclusion. But the productivity is not considered; for the agreement of an agreeing party is necessary, and here the conclusion is necessarily false.

Commentary
This passage critically examines a doubt regarding the first figure of the conditional conjunctive syllogism. The author states that if both premises are necessary conditional propositions (luzūmiyya), the conclusion should also be necessary. However, a counterexample is presented: 'Whenever two is odd, it is a number' (which is true in the sense of a false antecedent? Actually, 'two is odd' is false, but the conditional as a whole can be true if we consider it as a material implication? The logicians treat 'كلما' as a universal conditional, and the example is problematic because 'two is odd' is false, so the conditional is vacuously true. But the author's point is that the premises are true while the conclusion 'Whenever two is odd, it is even' is false (since two is not odd). The proposed solution – that the major premise 'Whenever it is a number, it is even' is not a necessary conditional (luzūmiyya) but an accidental conditional (ittifāqiyya) – is then criticized. The author argues that even if it were accidental, it would be a specific accidental (ittifāqiyya khāṣṣa) because the antecedent and consequent are both true (since 'two is a number' and 'two is even' are both true), but the conclusion 'Whenever two is odd, it is even' has a false antecedent, so the conclusion is false. The rejection points out that the agreement of an agreeing party (muwāfiq) – meaning the consequent agreeing with the antecedent – is not sufficient for productivity; the syllogism still yields a false conclusion, so the first figure's claim to produce a necessary conditional from necessary premises is challenged.

The productivity of a conditional syllogism depends on the premises being truly necessary in meaning; denying necessity does not salvage a false conclusion.

Resolving the Doubt on Two Implicative Conditionals
Doubt and multiple responses on conditional syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the dialectical exchange (shakk, jawab, ilzam) concerning the productivity of two implicative conditionals in the first figure and assess the strengths of the responses.

Core Idea
A syllogism with impossible antecedents may fail even if both conditionals are true; distinguishing implicative from coincidental is key.

In this passage, the author raises a powerful doubt against the claim that two implicative conditionals (luzumiyyatayn) always produce an implicative conclusion in the first figure. The doubt uses a seemingly valid example that yields a false conclusion. The text then presents and critiques several solutions, including an 'ilzam' (obligatory argument) from al-Hashiya and the author's own denial of the minor premise. By studying this exchange, you will learn how logicians defend productive rules against counterexamples and sharpen your ability to detect when a conditional is truly implicative versus merely coincidental.

Guided Path
1
Identify the doubt (shakk)
Locate the counterexample: the minor 'Whenever two is odd, it is a number' and major 'Whenever it is a number, it is even' are both true implicatives, yet the conclusion 'Whenever two is odd, it is even' is false. This appears to contradict the rule that two implicatives yield an implicative in the first figure.
The false conclusion shows that something is wrong with the syllogism or the classification of the premises.
2
Analyze the first response and its rebuttal
The first response denies that the major is implicative, claiming it is coincidental. The author rebuts: if it were coincidental, it would be a specific coincidence where both antecedent and consequent are true (i.e., the conclusion), but then the conclusion would be true, yet the example shows it false. So the response fails.
This shows that simply labeling a premise as coincidental does not resolve the logical issue.
3
Understand the ilzam (obligatory) response
Al-Hashiya offers an alternative: replace the major with 'Whenever it is a number, it exists' (implicative) and add 'Whenever it exists, it is even', which would be productive. This is an 'ilzam' because it forces the doubter to accept a conclusion based on his own premises, even if the arguer does not believe them.
Ilzam is a dialectical strategy: you argue from the opponent's assumptions, not your own.
4
Examine the author's own solution
The author proposes denying the universal minor: the number-ness of two-odd is an impossible state, and impossibilities are uncaused, so existence is not necessary. Hence the minor is false as a universal conditional. The particular version does not help because the major also fails for the same reason.
This solution hinges on the fact that the antecedent 'two is odd' is impossible, making the conditional not truly implicative.
5
Evaluate the final answer on the major
Another response denies the universality of the major: among the estimations of number-ness of two, oddness and evenness are not necessary. If one insists on the major's universality, the conclusion would be true; if the major is taken as a specific coincidental, the minor's antecedent becomes impossible, making the syllogism non-productive.
The passage concludes that such syllogisms (with impossible antecedents) are useless; productivity only applies to proper coincidentals.

Source Text

(1) كذا في حمد الله، وفي بحر العلوم غير معلل. (2) كذا في الأصل، وفيما بين سطوره إشارة إلى نسخة "يلتزم" بدل يلزم.
Thus the productivity of two implicatives is an implicative in the first figure, but here there is a doubt: It is true that 'Whenever two is odd, it is a number' and 'Whenever it is a number, it is even', while the conclusion is false, which is our statement 'Whenever two is odd, it is even'. Its solution - as it was said: denial that the major is implicative, rather it is coincidental. This does not help; for if the major were coincidental, it would be a specific coincidental due to the truth of the antecedent and consequent, and that is the conclusion, but productivity is not considered; for the agreement of agreeing with the agreeing is necessary, and here the falsehood of the conclusion follows. And it is answered to this solution that our statement 'Whenever it is a number, it exists' is implicative, for number-ness is dependent on existence, and likewise 'Whenever it exists, it is even', and it is productive by your claim for what you denied. An indication that the answer is obligatory, and his doctrine was the doctrine of the doubter, and he, insofar as he is a doubter, does not accept the productivity of two implicatives as an implicative, so he is not, by his claim, to answer by proving the denied premise in this manner, rather the way is obligation. Thus in 'al-Hashiya'. And it is not far to say: The conclusion of the mentioned syllogism in the speech of the answerer is either implicative or not; on the second, the doubt is accomplished by this; on the first, the first syllogism becomes from two implicatives, so the refutation is accomplished by the first. I say: And you may deny the universal minor, for we do not accept that the number-ness of two-odd is an effect of existence; because impossibilities are not caused, and the number-ness of two-odd is among the estimations of number-ness of two, and on this estimation existence is not necessary, so the universal minor is not true. And the particular only yields a particular that is not suitable for the occurrence of the major either. And it is not far to say in answer to the original problem: The universality of the major is denied; for among the estimations of number-ness of two, oddness and evenness are not necessary upon it, and if the necessity of evenness on that estimation is claimed, the truth of the conclusion follows, and if it is taken as a specific universal coincidental, it prevents the necessity of the minor being coincidental; for its antecedent is impossible. And this kind of syllogism is non-productive, not useful; that is only when they are both coincidental. (1) Thus in Hamd Allah, and in Bahr al-Ulum 'not caused'. Commentary
This passage addresses a doubt (shakk) about the productivity of two implicative conditionals (luzumiyyatayn) in the first figure of the conditional conjunctive syllogism. The doubt is raised using the example: 'Whenever two is odd, it is a number' (minor) and 'Whenever it is a number, it is even' (major) lead to the conclusion 'Whenever two is odd, it is even', which is false because two is not odd. This appears to show that two true implicative premises can yield a false conclusion, challenging the claim that the first figure is productive. The text then presents and discusses several answers. First, a proposed solution: deny that the major is implicative, claiming it is merely coincidental (ittifaqiyya). This is rejected because if it were coincidental, it would still be a specific coincidence where both antecedent and consequent are true, and the conclusion would be true, but here the conclusion is false. The rejection notes that the agreement of agreeing with the agreeing is necessary, but here falsehood follows. Second, an answer from 'al-Hashiya' suggests an alternative: the major is actually 'Whenever it is a number, it exists' (which is implicative, since number depends on existence), followed by 'Whenever it exists, it is even', which would be productive. This is an argument by obligation (ilzam) directed at the doubter, who does not accept the productivity of two implicatives anyway. Third, the author (presumably the commentator) offers his own solution: deny the universal minor premise, because the number-ness of two-odd is an impossibility, and impossibilities are not caused, so existence is not necessary. Hence the minor is false, and the syllogism is not sound. Fourth, another answer: deny the universality of the major; for in different estimations of number-ness of two, oddness and evenness are not necessary, and if one claims necessity, the conclusion would be true, or if the major is taken as a specific universal coincidental, the minor's being coincidental is prevented because its antecedent is impossible. The passage concludes that this kind of syllogism (where the antecedent is impossible) is non-productive and useless; productivity only occurs when both premises are coincidental in a proper sense? Actually the text says 'when they are both coincidental' but that might be a misreading. Overall, the passage illustrates the nuanced discussion of conditional syllogisms and the importance of distinguishing implicative from coincidental conditionals.

Distinguishing Necessary and Accidental Conditionals in Syllogistic Productivity
Author refutes skepticism about conditional syllogism productivity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between necessary (luzumiyya) and accidental (ittifaqiyya) conditionals and determine whether a conditional syllogism is validly productive based on the nature of its premises.

Core Idea
Only necessary conditionals yield certain knowledge; accidental conditionals bind only in dialectic.

In this passage, the author refutes a skeptic who claims that a conditional syllogism about number, existence, and evenness is not productive. The key is understanding when a conditional proposition expresses a necessary connection (luzum) versus a mere accidental conjunction (ittifaq). This distinction determines whether the syllogism yields a necessary conclusion or only a dialectical binding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main syllogism
Find the two conditional premises and the conclusion in the Arabic. The syllogism is: 'Whenever a number, it exists; whenever it exists, it is even; therefore whenever two is odd, it is even.'
The conclusion is about the impossible case of 'two odd'.
2
Classify each conditional as luzumiyya or ittifaqiyya
The author argues both premises are necessary (luzumiyya) because numberhood depends on existence. The skeptic claims they are accidental (ittifaqiyya) because the connection does not hold in impossible cases.
The classification affects whether the syllogism is productive.
3
Explain why only necessary conditionals yield productivity
A conditional syllogism is productive only if the premises are necessary. Accidental conditionals only produce a true conclusion if both antecedents happen to be true, which is not certain.
This is the core of the skeptic's objection.
4
Analyze the dialectical responses
The author offers multiple responses: denying the minor, denying the major, invoking necessary concomitants, and using contrapositive. Each response targets the supposed lack of necessity.
These responses show how to defend a conditional syllogism against skepticism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(٢) كذا في الأصل، وفيما بين سطوره إشارة إلى نسخة «يلتزم» بدل يلزم». فصل في القياس الاقتراني الشرطي خاصتين. هذا، والعلم الحق عند علام الغيوب. (وَ) لك أيضا أَنْ تَمْنَعَ الْكُبْرَى؛ بِنَاءً عَلَى أَنَّ الْعَامَّ لَا يَسْتَلْزِمُ الْخَاصَّ). ووجود الاثنين عام من الزوجية؛ لِأَنَّ وُجُودَ الاثْنَيْنِ الْفَرْدِ مِنْ جُمْلَةِ وُجُود الاثْنَيْنِ. نَعَمْ، تَصْدُقُ اتَّفَاقِيَّةً) ، وهي غير منتجة عند الدافع. (وَلَوْ تَشَبَّثَ) في الجواب (بكَوْنِهَا)، أي الزوجية (مِنْ) لَوَازِمِ الْمَاهِيَّةِ)، فيلزم وجود الاثنين، فيصدق الكبرى (لَلَزِمَ صِدْقُ النَّتِيجَةِ الْمَفْرُوضِ كَذِبُهَا فِي هَذَا الْجَوَابِ)، وهي قولنا كلما كان الاثنان فردا كان زوجا ؛ لأن لوازم الماهية لازمة على كل تقدير، فلا يتم أصل الشك ، (فَتَأَمَّلْ) ، فإن للمجيب أن يقول: إن لوازم الماهية لا تنفك عن ملزوماتها (۱) في نحو من أنحاء الوجود، فوجود الاثنين مستلزم لها ، وليس يجب فيها أن يلزم على تقدير منافيها ، فاتضح الفرق . (وَاخْتَارَ الرَّئِيسُ فِي الْحَلَّ بِنَاءً عَلَى رَأْيِهِ)، وهو أن المحال لا يستلزم واقعيا (أَنَّ الصُّغْرَى كَاذِبَةٌ. أَقُولُ في الجواب عن هذا الحل: إن (قَوْلَنَا كُلَّمَا لَمْ يَكُنِ الإِثْنَانِ عَدَدًا لَمْ يَكُنْ فَرْدًا يَصْدُقُ لُزُومِيَّةً)؛ فإن عددية الاثنين أعم من فرديته ؛ (فَإِنَّ انْتِفَاءَ الْعَامِّ مُسْتَلْزِمٌ لِانْتِفَاءِ الْخَاص ، وَهُوَ يَنْعَكِسُ بِعَكْسِ النَّقِيضِ إِلَى تِلْكَ الصُّغْرَى) ، وهي قولنا كلما كان الاثنان فردا كان عددا». (وَمِنْهُ يَتَبَيَّنُ ضُعْفُ مَذْهَبِهِ ، هو عدم صدق اللزومية حال كذب المقدم دون التالي. ونحن لا نجادل المصنف في صدق صغرى هذا القياس، ولا في ضعف مذهب الشيخ ، ولو جادلناه لم يَكَد سعيه يرجع إلى طائل؛ فإن الشرطية التي فرضها المصنف كاذبة على رأيه ؛ لكذب المقدم، وهو عدم عددية (۱) كذا في الأصل، وفيما بين سطوره إشارة إلى نسخة إن لوازم الماهية ما لا ينفك عن ملزومه» بدل ما أثبتناه.
And it is answered about this solution: that our statement 'Whenever it is a number, it exists' is a necessary conditional, because numberhood depends on existence. Similarly, 'Whenever it exists, it is even' is also necessary. And it is productive, in your claim, for what you denied—an indication that the answer is binding. And his school was the school of the skeptic, and he, insofar as he is a skeptic, does not accept the productivity of two necessary conditionals as necessary. So he has no right, in his claim, to answer by proving the denied premise in this manner; rather the way is binding. Thus in the gloss. And it is not far to say: The result of the aforementioned syllogism in the speech of the answerer is either necessary or not. On the second, the skepticism is completed by this. On the first, the first syllogism becomes from two necessary conditionals, so the refutation by the first is completed. I say: And you may deny the universal minor, for we do not accept that the numberhood of two odd is an effect of existence, because impossible things are not caused, and the numberhood of two odd is among the estimations of the numberhood of two. On this estimation, existence is not necessary, so the universal minor is not true, and the particular only benefits the particular which is not suitable for the occurrence of the major. And it is not far to say in answer to the original problem: That the universality of the major is denied; for among the estimations of the numberhood of two, oddness and evenness are not necessary upon it. And if the necessity of evenness on that estimation is claimed, then the truth of the result follows. And if it is taken as a particular accidental universal, the necessity of being a minor accidental is prevented; for its antecedent is impossible. And this type of syllogism is not productive, not beneficial; that is only when both are accidental. (1) Thus in Hamd Allah, and in Bahr al-Ulum 'not caused'. (2) Thus in the original, and between its lines there is an indication of a version 'he is bound' instead of 'it binds'. Chapter on conditional conjunctive syllogism: two particularities. This, and the true knowledge is with the Knower of the Unseen. And you may also deny the major, based on the fact that the universal does not entail the particular. And existence of two is more general than evenness, because the existence of two odd is part of the existence of two. Yes, it is true accidentally, and it is not productive according to the refuter. And if he clings in the answer to it (i.e., evenness) being from the necessary concomitants of the essence, then existence of two is necessary, so the major is true, then the truth of the result would follow, which was supposed false in this answer—namely our statement 'Whenever two is odd, it is even'. Because the necessary concomitants of the essence are necessary in every estimation, so the original skepticism is not completed. So ponder, for the answerer can say: The necessary concomitants of the essence do not separate from their essences in some mode of existence, so existence of two entails them, and it is not necessary that they follow upon the estimation of their opposites; so the difference is clear. And the Chief chose in the solution, based on his opinion—that the impossible does not entail a real consequent—that the minor is false. I say in answer to this solution: That our statement 'Whenever two is not a number, it is not odd' is true as a necessary conditional; for numberhood of two is more general than its oddness. For the negation of the universal entails the negation of the particular, and it reflects by contrapositive to that minor—namely our statement 'Whenever two is odd, it is a number'. And from this the weakness of his school becomes clear: it is that the necessary conditional is not true when the antecedent is false without the consequent. And we do not argue with the author about the truth of the minor of this syllogism, nor about the weakness of the chief's school. And if we argued with him, his effort would hardly return to profit; for the conditional that the author assumed is false according to his opinion, due to the falsity of the antecedent, which is the lack of numberhood of two (1) Thus in the original, and between its lines there is an indication of a version 'The necessary concomitants of the essence are what do not separate from their essences' instead of what we have written.
Source pages p. 535

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical refutation of a skeptic's objection regarding the productivity of a conditional syllogism. The example at hand is: 'Whenever something is a number, it exists; whenever something exists, it is even; therefore whenever two is odd, it is even.' The skeptic argues that this syllogism is not productive because the conditionals are not necessary (luzumiyya) but merely accidental (ittifaqiyya). The author (the commentator) presents multiple lines of response. First, he notes that the conditionals are indeed necessary because numberhood depends on existence, and existence entails evenness in this specific context. However, the skeptic, by his own position, does not accept the productivity of two necessary conditionals. The author then mentions a binding (ilzami) argument. Next, he suggests denying the minor premise: the numberhood of two odd is not caused by existence because impossible things (like a number being odd without existing) are not caused. Then he suggests denying the major premise: existence is more general than evenness, so existence does not entail evenness necessarily—only accidentally. He also discusses the possibility that evenness is a necessary concomitant of the essence (lazim al-mahiyya), which would make the conclusion true, thus defeating the skepticism. Finally, he presents the solution of the Chief (Ibn Sina), who holds that an impossible antecedent does not entail a real consequent, so the minor is false. The author criticizes this by showing that the contrapositive conditional ('If two is not a number, it is not odd') is actually true and necessary, exposing the weakness of the Chief's view. The commentary follows the author's order: each objection and answer is explained. The key logical point is the distinction between necessary and accidental conditionals and the role of impossibility.

Key Terms
لزومية
اتفاقية
لازم الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the skeptic reject the productivity of the syllogism 'Whenever a number, then exists; whenever exists, then even; therefore whenever two is odd, then even'?
A) Because the minor premise is false.
B) Because the conditionals are accidental, not necessary.
C) Because the major premise is false.
D) Because the conclusion is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between necessary and accidental conditionals is crucial for determining the validity of conditional syllogisms; only necessary conditionals (luzumiyya) guarantee a productive syllogism that yields certain knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find a conditional syllogism in a kalam text (e.g., from al-Sanusiyya's Umm al-Barahin) and classify its premises as necessary (luzumiyya) or accidental (ittifaqiyya). Justify your classification using the criteria from this lesson.
?
Construct a dialectical response to a skeptic who claims that a conditional premise in a qiyas istithna'i is merely accidental. Use one of the strategies from the passage (e.g., deny the minor, appeal to necessary concomitants).

The Chief's View on Conditional Falsehood and Its Critique
Author critiques Ibn Sina's view on conditionals with impossible antecedents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate Ibn Sina's claim that a conditional with an impossible antecedent is false, and use contraposition to demonstrate its truth.

Core Idea
An impossible antecedent does not falsify a conditional; contraposition reveals logical validity.

In this passage, the author critiques the view of 'the Chief' (Ibn Sina) that a conditional like 'If two is not a number, then it is not odd' is false because its antecedent is impossible. The author shows that this conditional is actually true as a necessary implication, using contraposition from a true universal statement: 'If two is odd, then it is a number.' This critique exposes a weakness in Ibn Sina's doctrine of conditionals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional under dispute
Find the conditional statement that the Chief claims is false. It is: 'If two is not a number, then it is not odd.' The antecedent 'two is not a number' is impossible because two is a number.
2
Understand the Chief's reasoning
According to the Chief, a conditional with an impossible antecedent is false because there is no real implication. He therefore claims the minor premise of the syllogism is false.
3
Apply contraposition
The author derives the conditional from a true one using contraposition ('aks al-naqid'). Start from 'If two is odd, then it is a number' (true). Contrapositive: 'If two is not a number, then it is not odd.' This is logically equivalent and thus true necessarily.
Contraposition preserves truth: the contrapositive of a true conditional is always true.
4
Evaluate the critique
The author argues that the Chief's view fails because it denies the truth of a necessary implication. The conditional is true as a luzumiyya (necessary implication), regardless of the impossibility of the antecedent.

(وَاخْتَارَ الرَّئِيسُ فِي الْحَلَّ بِنَاءً عَلَى رَأْيِهِ)، وهو أن المحال لا يستلزم واقعيا (أَنَّ الصُّغْرَى كَاذِبَةٌ. أَقُولُ في الجواب عن هذا الحل: إن (قَوْلَنَا كُلَّمَا لَمْ يَكُنِ الإِثْنَانِ عَدَدًا لَمْ يَكُنْ فَرْدًا يَصْدُقُ لُزُومِيَّةً)؛ فإن عددية الاثنين أعم من فرديته ؛ (فَإِنَّ انْتِفَاءَ الْعَامِّ مُسْتَلْزِمٌ لِانْتِفَاءِ الْخَاص ، وَهُوَ يَنْعَكِسُ بِعَكْسِ النَّقِيضِ إِلَى تِلْكَ الصُّغْرَى) ، وهي قولنا كلما كان الاثنان فردا كان عددا».
(وَمِنْهُ يَتَبَيَّنُ ضُعْفُ مَذْهَبِهِ ، هو عدم صدق اللزومية حال كذب المقدم دون التالي. ونحن لا نجادل المصنف في صدق صغرى هذا القياس، ولا في ضعف مذهب الشيخ ، ولو جادلناه لم يَكَد سعيه يرجع إلى طائل؛ فإن الشرطية التي فرضها المصنف كاذبة على رأيه ؛ لكذب المقدم، وهو عدم عددية الاثنين والتالي ،صادق، وهو عدم فرديته وانتفاء العام إنما يستلزم انتفاء الخاص إذا كان العموم والخصوص بحسب نفس الأمر، بل إذا كان الانتفاءان واقعيين، وقد مر التلويح من المصنف إليه في بحث الكليات. والعلم الحقيقي عند علام الغيوب.

Section on conditional conjunctive syllogism. Two properties. This, and true knowledge is with the Knower of the Unseen. (And) you may also prevent the major premise, based on the fact that the general does not entail the specific. And existence of two is general in relation to evenness; because the existence of two odd numbers is part of the existence of two. Yes, it is true accidentally, and it is not productive for the objector. (And if one clings) in the answer (to its being), i.e., evenness, (among the necessary concomitants of the essence), then the existence of two follows, so the major premise is true. (Then it would necessitate the truth of the conclusion assumed false in this answer) — namely our statement: Whenever two is odd, it is even; because the necessary concomitants of the essence are necessary in every estimation, so the original doubt is not established. (So reflect) — for the respondent may say: The necessary concomitants of the essence are not separable from their essences in some mode of existence, so the existence of two entails them, but it is not necessary that they follow upon the supposition of what contradicts them; so the difference becomes clear. (And the Chief chose in the solution, based on his view — that the impossible does not really entail — that the minor premise is false. I say in response to this solution: Indeed (our statement: Whenever two is not a number, it is not odd, is true as an implication); for the number-ness of two is more general than its oddness; (for the negation of the general entails the negation of the specific, and it reflects by contraposition to that minor premise), which is our statement: Whenever two is odd, it is a number. (From this, the weakness of his doctrine becomes clear: it is the non-truth of the implication when the antecedent is false while the consequent is not false. And we do not argue with the author about the truth of the minor premise of this syllogism, nor about the weakness of the doctrine of the Sheikh; and if we argued with him, his effort would scarcely return to benefit; for the conditional which the author assumed would be false according to his view, due to the falsity of the antecedent, which is the non-number-ness of two, while the consequent is true, which is its non-oddness; and the negation of the general only entails the negation of the specific if the generality and specificity are according to actual fact, rather if the negations are actual; and the author has already hinted at this in the discussion of universals. And true knowledge is with the Knower of the Unseen. (And the truth in the answer is to prevent the falsity of the conclusion, based on allowing entailment between two contradictories) when the antecedent is impossible, as is the case here. (And the remaining details of the research) are mentioned (in the extensive works); and due to lack of benefit, the author refrained from them.
Source pages p. 536

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of a problem in conditional conjunctive syllogism. The author presents a series of objections and responses regarding a specific syllogism: 'Whenever two is not a number, it is not odd.' The major premise claims that if something is not a number, it is not odd. The objector argues that the general (number) does not entail the specific (odd), so the major premise is false. However, the author notes that the major premise can be true accidentally, but such accidental conditionals are not productive in syllogisms. Another response involves evenness being a necessary concomitant of the essence of two, but this leads to the conclusion that the conclusion (which was assumed false) would be true, which is unacceptable. The author then considers the view of 'the Chief' (likely Ibn Sina) that an impossible antecedent makes a conditional false; thus the minor premise is false. The author counters that the conditional 'Whenever two is not a number, it is not odd' is true as an implication because the antecedent (two is not a number) is false, and the negation of the general entails the negation of the specific by contraposition. This reveals a weakness in the Chief's view: he denies the truth of implications where the antecedent is false but the consequent is not. The author finally declares that the correct answer is to deny that the conclusion is false, because entailment can occur between two contradictories when the antecedent is impossible. The discussion illustrates deep issues in the logic of conditionals: the relationship between implication, actual truth, and impossibility.

اللزومية
الاتفاقية
عكس النقيض
المحال

D) The author denies it is true.

Contraposition preserves truth; a conditional derived by contraposition from a true conditional is necessarily true, regardless of the impossibility of the antecedent.

The Final Solution: Entailment from Impossible Premises
Author presents the correct solution: preventing the falsity of the conclusion by allowing entailment from impossibles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle that an impossible antecedent allows entailment between any propositions, including contradictories, and assess its implications for syllogistic validity.

Core Idea
When the antecedent is impossible, the conditional is true whatever the consequent, resolving apparent contradictions.

After considering objections and the Chief's view, the author declares that the correct answer is to deny that the conclusion is false, because from an impossible premise any proposition follows, even a contradiction. This resolves the apparent paradox in the syllogism about two, oddness, and evenness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the impossible antecedent
In the conclusion 'Whenever two is odd, it is even', the antecedent 'two is odd' is impossible because two is even. Recognize that the antecedent cannot be true in reality.
2
Recall the principle 'ex impossibili quodlibet'
The principle states that from an impossibility, anything follows. In logic, a conditional with an impossible antecedent is true regardless of the consequent.
This principle is accepted in classical logic and is relevant here.
3
Apply the principle to the conclusion
Since the antecedent 'two is odd' is impossible, the conditional 'If two is odd, then it is even' is true. Therefore, the conclusion of the syllogism is not false, as assumed by the objector.
4
Assess why this solves the dispute
The original problem was that the syllogism seemed to produce a false conclusion. Once we accept that the conclusion is actually true due to the impossible antecedent, the syllogism is valid and the problem disappears.

(وَ) لك أيضا أَنْ تَمْنَعَ الْكُبْرَى؛ بِنَاءً عَلَى أَنَّ الْعَامَّ لَا يَسْتَلْزِمُ الْخَاصَّ). ووجود الاثنين عام من الزوجية؛ لِأَنَّ وُجُودَ الاثْنَيْنِ الْفَرْدِ مِنْ جُمْلَةِ وُجُود الاثْنَيْنِ. نَعَمْ، تَصْدُقُ اتَّفَاقِيَّةً) ، وهي غير منتجة عند الدافع. (وَلَوْ تَشَبَّثَ) في الجواب (بكَوْنِهَا)، أي الزوجية (مِنْ) لَوَازِمِ الْمَاهِيَّةِ)، فيلزم وجود الاثنين، فيصدق الكبرى (لَلَزِمَ صِدْقُ النَّتِيجَةِ الْمَفْرُوضِ كَذِبُهَا فِي هَذَا الْجَوَابِ)، وهي قولنا كلما كان الاثنان فردا كان زوجا ؛ لأن لوازم الماهية لازمة على كل تقدير، فلا يتم أصل الشك ، (فَتَأَمَّلْ) ، فإن للمجيب أن يقول: إن لوازم الماهية لا تنفك عن ملزوماتها في نحو من أنحاء الوجود، فوجود الاثنين مستلزم لها ، وليس يجب فيها أن يلزم على تقدير منافيها ، فاتضح الفرق .
(وَاخْتَارَ الرَّئِيسُ فِي الْحَلَّ بِنَاءً عَلَى رَأْيِهِ)، وهو أن المحال لا يستلزم واقعيا (أَنَّ الصُّغْرَى كَاذِبَةٌ. أَقُولُ في الجواب عن هذا الحل: إن (قَوْلَنَا كُلَّمَا لَمْ يَكُنِ الإِثْنَانِ عَدَدًا لَمْ يَكُنْ فَرْدًا يَصْدُقُ لُزُومِيَّةً)؛ فإن عددية الاثنين أعم من فرديته ؛ (فَإِنَّ انْتِفَاءَ الْعَامِّ مُسْتَلْزِمٌ لِانْتِفَاءِ الْخَاص ، وَهُوَ يَنْعَكِسُ بِعَكْسِ النَّقِيضِ إِلَى تِلْكَ الصُّغْرَى) ، وهي قولنا كلما كان الاثنان فردا كان عددا».
(وَمِنْهُ يَتَبَيَّنُ ضُعْفُ مَذْهَبِهِ ، هو عدم صدق اللزومية حال كذب المقدم دون التالي. ونحن لا نجادل المصنف في صدق صغرى هذا القياس، ولا في ضعف مذهب الشيخ ، ولو جادلناه لم يَكَد سعيه يرجع إلى طائل؛ فإن الشرطية التي فرضها المصنف كاذبة على رأيه ؛ لكذب المقدم، وهو عدم عددية الاثنين والتالي ،صادق، وهو عدم فرديته وانتفاء العام إنما يستلزم انتفاء الخاص إذا كان العموم والخصوص بحسب نفس الأمر، بل إذا كان الانتفاءان واقعيين، وقد مر التلويح من المصنف إليه في بحث الكليات. والعلم الحقيقي عند علام الغيوب.
(وَالْحَقُّ فِي الْجَوَابِ مَنْعُ كَذِبِ التَّتِيجَةِ ؛ بِنَاءً عَلَى تَجْوِيزِ الِاسْتِلْرَامِ بَيْنَ الْمُتَنَافِيَيْن) ، إذا كان المقدم محالا ، وههنا كذلك، (وَبَقَايَا الْبَحْثِ) مذكورة (فِي الْمَبْسُوطَاتِ) ، ولقلة الجدى أعرض المصنف عنها
Section on conditional conjunctive syllogism. Two properties. This, and true knowledge is with the Knower of the Unseen. (And) you may also prevent the major premise, based on the fact that the general does not entail the specific. And existence of two is general in relation to evenness; because the existence of two odd numbers is part of the existence of two. Yes, it is true accidentally, and it is not productive for the objector. (And if one clings) in the answer (to its being), i.e., evenness, (among the necessary concomitants of the essence), then the existence of two follows, so the major premise is true. (Then it would necessitate the truth of the conclusion assumed false in this answer) — namely our statement: Whenever two is odd, it is even; because the necessary concomitants of the essence are necessary in every estimation, so the original doubt is not established. (So reflect) — for the respondent may say: The necessary concomitants of the essence are not separable from their essences in some mode of existence, so the existence of two entails them, but it is not necessary that they follow upon the supposition of what contradicts them; so the difference becomes clear. (And the Chief chose in the solution, based on his view — that the impossible does not really entail — that the minor premise is false. I say in response to this solution: Indeed (our statement: Whenever two is not a number, it is not odd, is true as an implication); for the number-ness of two is more general than its oddness; (for the negation of the general entails the negation of the specific, and it reflects by contraposition to that minor premise), which is our statement: Whenever two is odd, it is a number. (From this, the weakness of his doctrine becomes clear: it is the non-truth of the implication when the antecedent is false while the consequent is not false. And we do not argue with the author about the truth of the minor premise of this syllogism, nor about the weakness of the doctrine of the Sheikh; and if we argued with him, his effort would scarcely return to benefit; for the conditional which the author assumed would be false according to his view, due to the falsity of the antecedent, which is the non-number-ness of two, while the consequent is true, which is its non-oddness; and the negation of the general only entails the negation of the specific if the generality and specificity are according to actual fact, rather if the negations are actual; and the author has already hinted at this in the discussion of universals. And true knowledge is with the Knower of the Unseen. (And the truth in the answer is to prevent the falsity of the conclusion, based on allowing entailment between two contradictories) when the antecedent is impossible, as is the case here. (And the remaining details of the research) are mentioned (in the extensive works); and due to lack of benefit, the author refrained from them.
Source pages p. 536

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of a problem in conditional conjunctive syllogism. The author presents a series of objections and responses regarding a specific syllogism: 'Whenever two is not a number, it is not odd.' The major premise claims that if something is not a number, it is not odd. The objector argues that the general (number) does not entail the specific (odd), so the major premise is false. However, the author notes that the major premise can be true accidentally, but such accidental conditionals are not productive in syllogisms. Another response involves evenness being a necessary concomitant of the essence of two, but this leads to the conclusion that the conclusion (which was assumed false) would be true, which is unacceptable. The author then considers the view of 'the Chief' (likely Ibn Sina) that an impossible antecedent makes a conditional false; thus the minor premise is false. The author counters that the conditional 'Whenever two is not a number, it is not odd' is true as an implication because the antecedent (two is not a number) is false, and the negation of the general entails the negation of the specific by contraposition. This reveals a weakness in the Chief's view: he denies the truth of implications where the antecedent is false but the consequent is not. The author finally declares that the correct answer is to deny that the conclusion is false, because entailment can occur between two contradictories when the antecedent is impossible. The discussion illustrates deep issues in the logic of conditionals: the relationship between implication, actual truth, and impossibility.

الاستلزام
المتنافي

C) The conclusion is not false because entailment from an impossible antecedent is allowed.
D) The syllogism is invalid.

Key Takeaway
When the antecedent is impossible, the conditional is true regardless of the consequent, even if it seems contradictory.

The Syllogism
intermediate
