The Conditional Syllogism: Definition, Conditions, and Valid Moods
Author defines the conditional syllogism, its composition, necessary conditions, and valid moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of a conditional syllogism, list its necessary conditions, and determine whether a given conditional inference is valid according to the rules of conjunctive conditional syllogisms.

Core Idea
A conditional syllogism's validity depends on the implicative connection and the direction of the inference.

After mastering categorical syllogisms, we now turn to conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i). Unlike the categorical, this syllogism uses a conditional premise and a premise that either posits or negates one of its parts. The passage from the matn explains its composition, the essential conditions for validity, and the moods that produce certain conclusions. Understanding these rules is crucial for evaluating arguments in Kalam and Fiqh that rely on conditional reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Find the conditional premise (shartiyya) and the positing or negating premise (wad'iyya/raf'iyya) in the Arabic. Note that the second premise must refer to one of the parts of the conditional.
2
Check the necessary conditions
The author states two conditions: (1) the conditional must be affirmative and implicative (luzumiyya) for conjunctive, or repugnant ('inadiyya) for disjunctive; (2) either the conditional or the exception must be universal. Examine why these are needed.
Non-implicatives do not guarantee a necessary connection; particularity allows different estimations.
3
Analyze the valid moods for conjunctive conditionals
The passage lists four possible inferences: positing the antecedent gives the consequent, negating the consequent gives negating the antecedent (both valid). The reverse directions are invalid. Understand the reasoning: the consequent may be more general, so positing it does not imply the antecedent; the antecedent may be more specific, so negating it does not negate the consequent.
Memorize the two valid moods: modus ponens and modus tollens.
4
Translate to an Islamic example
Construct an Islamic conditional: 'If the prayer is invalid, then wudu is nullified.' Apply the valid moods: positing 'the prayer is invalid' yields 'wudu is nullified' (valid). Negating 'wudu is nullified' yields 'the prayer is not invalid' (valid). Check the invalid moods as well.
This shows practical use in fiqh reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في القياس الاستثنائي. ولما فرغ المصنف من الاقتراني وتقسيمه شرع في الاستثنائي، (وَ) قال (الِاسْتِثْنَائِيُّ يَتَرَكَّبُ مِنْ مُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ : شَرْطِيَّةٍ) وحملية (وَوَضْعِيَّةٍ) مشتملة على وضع أحدِ جزتَيْه، (أَوْ رَفْعِيَّةٍ) مشتملة على رفع أحد جزئيه. (وَلَا بُدَّ) فيه (مِنْ كَوْنِهَا مُوجَبَةً لُزُومِيَّةً) في الاستثنائي المتصل، (أو عِنَادِيَّةً في المنفصل؛ حتى لا يستعمل فيه سالبة؛ لأن غير المتلازمين لا يوجب وضع أحدهما وضعَ الآخر، ولا رفعُ أحدهما رفع الآخر، وكذا غير المتعاندين لا يوجب الوضع الرفع، ولا العكس. (و) لا بد أيضا (مِنْ كُلِّيَّةِ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ أَوِ الِاسْتِثْنَاءِ)؛ إذ لو كانا جزئيتين، فيجوز أن يكون تقدير لزوم التالي للمقدم غير تقدير الاستثناء، فلا يلزم من الوضع أو الرفع شيء. (فَفِي الْمُتَّصِلَةِ يُنْتِجُ وَضْعُ الْمُقَدَّمِ وَضْعَ التَّالِي؛ لِأَنَّ وُجُودَ الْمَلْزُوم مُسْتَلْزِمٌ لِوُجُودِ اللَّازِم بالضرورة، وَلَا عَكْسَ، أي لا ينتج وضع التالي وضعَ المقدم؛ لِجَوَازِ أَعَمَّيَّةِ اللَّازِم، وتحقق الأعم لا يستلزم تحقق الأخص، (وَرَفْعُ التَّالِي رَفْعَ الْمُقَدَّمِ؛ فَإِنَّ انْتِفَاءَ اللَّازِمِ يَسْتَلْزِمُ انْتِفَاءَ الْمَلْزُومِ) بالضرورة، (وَلَا عَكْسَ)، أي لا ينتج رفع المقدم رفع التالي؛ لجواز الأخصية.
Section on the conditional syllogism. Having finished the categorical syllogism and its division, the author began on the conditional. He said: The conditional syllogism is composed of two premises: a conditional and a positing (containing positing of one of its parts) or a negating (containing negation of one of its parts). It is necessary that the conditional be affirmative and implicative in the conjunctive conditional, or repugnant in the disjunctive conditional, so that a negative is not used in it; because non-implicatives do not require positing one to posit the other, nor negating one to negate the other; similarly, non-repugnants do not require positing to negate, nor vice versa. It is also necessary that the conditional be universal or the exception; because if both are particular, it is possible that the estimation of the implication of the consequent upon the antecedent is different from the estimation of the exception, so nothing follows from the positing or negating. So in the conjunctive, positing the antecedent produces positing the consequent, because existence of the necessitating necessarily requires existence of the necessary, and not vice versa, i.e., positing the consequent does not produce positing the antecedent because of the possibility of the consequent being more general, and the realization of the more general does not require the realization of the more specific; and negating the consequent produces negating the antecedent, because non-existence of the necessary necessarily requires non-existence of the necessitating, and not vice versa, i.e., negating the antecedent does not produce negating the consequent because of the possibility of the antecedent being more specific.
Source pages p. 538

Commentary
This passage defines the conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i). It explains its composition: two premises, one conditional (shartiyya) and one positing or negating (wad'iyya or raf'iyya). It then states necessary conditions: the conditional must be affirmative and implicative (luzumiyya) in the conjunctive type, or repugnant ('inadiyya) in the disjunctive, to ensure a necessary connection. Negatives are not used because non-implicatives do not necessitate. Also, the conditional or the exceptional premise must be universal to avoid different estimations. Finally, it lists the valid moods: in conjunctive, positing the antecedent gives the consequent (but not vice versa due to generality), and negating the consequent gives negating the antecedent (but not vice versa due to specificity). The author uses examples from logic, but we can illustrate: For a conjunctive conditional like 'If it is a dog, it is an animal,' positing 'it is a dog' yields 'it is an animal,' but positing 'it is an animal' does not yield 'it is a dog.' Negating 'it is an animal' yields 'it is not a dog,' but negating 'it is not a dog' does not yield 'it is not an animal.'

Key Terms
القياس الاستثنائي
الشَّرْطِيَّةُ
اللُّزُومِيَّةُ
الْعِنَادِيَّةِ
وَضْعُ الْمُقَدَّمِ
رَفْعُ التَّالِي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in a conjunctive conditional syllogism, which of the following is a valid inference?
A) Positing the consequent yields the antecedent.
B) Negating the antecedent yields negating the consequent.
C) Negating the consequent yields negating the antecedent.
D) Both A and B are valid.

Key Takeaway
In a conjunctive conditional syllogism, only modus ponens (positing antecedent) and modus tollens (negating consequent) are valid; the reverse inferences are fallacious.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a conjunctive conditional syllogism on an Islamic ruling: 'If the wine is intoxicating, then it is forbidden. This beverage is an intoxicating wine. Therefore, it is forbidden.' Identify the antecedent, consequent, and verify it follows modus ponens.
?
Given the conditional 'If a sale is valid, then it is contracted by mutual consent (taradin),' determine whether the inference 'It is not contracted by mutual consent, therefore it is not a valid sale' is valid. Explain why according to the passage.

Resolving the Doubt About Denying the Consequent
Author refutes a doubt against modus tollens

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the doubt raised against modus tollens in conditional syllogisms and explain how defining entailment as universal inseparability resolves it.

Core Idea
Entailment means impossibility of separation across all times — that dissolves the doubt about modus tollens.

A subtle doubt challenges the validity of modus tollens: if the consequent is impossible to be non-existent, does that break the entailment? The author clarifies that entailment (luzum) is defined as the impossibility of separation in all possible circumstances, so the doubt actually denies the entailment itself, self-destructing as a contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the doubt
The doubt claims that if the consequent (tali) is impossible to be non-existent, then its negation does not entail negating the antecedent, because the entailment relation would not hold in that situation.
This is a denial of modus tollens: (P→Q, ¬Q) ⊢ ¬P is rejected when Q is necessary.
2
Find the author's definition of entailment
The author states: 'إن اللزوم حقيقة امتناع الانفكاك في جميع الأوقات' — entailment is the impossibility of separation in all times. 'All times' means all possible circumstances.
This definition is crucial: it makes entailment absolutely universal, not contingent on particular states.
3
Apply the definition to the doubt
If the consequent is impossible to be non-existent, that impossibility is included in 'all times' because it is a circumstance. Therefore, the entailment still holds even in that circumstance; the doubt's assumption that the entailment is absent is actually a denial of the entailment itself.
Thus the doubt reduces to assuming the entailment does not exist while the argument assumes it does — a contradiction (khulf).
4
Evaluate the author's final conclusion
The author concludes that the doubt is 'extremely foolish' because it either confuses the definition of entailment or attacks the truth of the exception premise, which is not part of the syllogism's logical validity.
If the exception premise (istithna') is true (the consequent is actually non-existent), then modus tollens is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَهَهُنَا شَكٍّ ، وَقِيلَ عَويص، قاله الجونفوري، وهو عبد الباقي، كذا في «الحاشية»، (وَهُوَ مَنْعُ اسْتِلْزَامِ الرَّفْعِ الرَّفْعَ ) ، أي منع استلزام رفع التالي رفع المقدم ؛ لِجَوَازِ اسْتِحَالَةِ انْتِفَاءِ اللَّازِمِ ، فَإِذَا وَقَعَ لَمْ يَبْقَ اللُّزُومُ مَعَهُ، فَلَا يَلْزَمُ انْتِفَاءُ الْمَلْزُومِ ؛ لأن انتفاء الملزوم إنما كان بعلاقة اللزوم، وقد ارتفع
هَذَا
أقُولُ : حَلُّهُ: إِنَّ اللُّزُومَ حَقِيقَةَ امْتِنَاعُ الاِنْفِكَاكِ فِي جَمِيعِ الْأَوْقَاتِ، فَوَقْتُ الِانْفِكَاكِ - وَهُوَ وَقْتُ عَدَمِ بَقَاءِ اللُّزُومِ - دَاخِل فِي الْجَمِيعِ) التي كان علتها ما (٢) اللزوم، فَهَذَا الْمَنْعُ) بالحقيقة (يَرْجِعُ إِلَى مَنْع اللَّزُومِ ، وَقَدْ فُرِضَ وُجُودُهُ ، خُلْفٌ). فيه أنه ليس حقيقة اللزوم الكلي إلا عدم انفكاك التالي عن المقدم في جميع التقادير الممكنة الاجتماع مع المقدم، كما نقل المصنف عن الشيخ سابقا، ووقت عدم اللزوم غير ممكن الاجتماع مع المقدم، على أن الشك إن وُجّه إلى الاستثنائي الذي فيه الشرطية جزئية والاستثناء كلي لم يكد يتم الحل . فإذن الحق في الجواب أن هذا المنع راجع إلى صدق الاستثناء؛ إذ حكم فيه بأن التالي منتف في الواقع، ونحن إنما ندعي أن الاستثنائي إنما ينتج صادقة إذا كان مقدمتاه صادقتين، وأن ارتفاع اللازم في الواقع يستلزم انتفاء الملزوم فيه، فإذن ظهر أن الشك في غاية السخافة.
ول
واعلم أن فيه تحريف الكلم عن مواضعه؛ فإن أصل الشك أورده
المفضّل الأبهري
And here there is a doubt, and it was said (to be) abstruse, said by al-Jawnpuri, and he is Abd al-Baqi, as in the commentary. It is the denial of the entailment of negation for negation, i.e., denial that negating the consequent entails negating the antecedent, due to the possibility of the impossibility of the non-existence of the consequent. For if it occurs, the entailment does not remain with it, so the non-existence of the antecedent does not follow, because the non-existence of the antecedent was only due to the relation of entailment, and it has been removed. This (doubt). I say: its resolution: that entailment in reality is the impossibility of separation in all times, so the time of separation — which is the time of the non-remaining of entailment — is included in the all (of those times) which was the cause of entailment. So this denial in reality reduces to a denial of the entailment, and its existence has been assumed, (which is) a contradiction. In it (the resolution) is that the reality of universal entailment is nothing but the non-separation of the consequent from the antecedent in all possible estimations that can coexist with the antecedent, as the author transmitted from the Shaykh earlier, and the time of non-entailment cannot coexist with the antecedent. Moreover, if the doubt is directed to the conditional syllogism in which the conditional is particular and the exception is universal, the resolution is barely achieved. So then the truth in the answer is that this denial refers to the truth of the exception, for it (the objector) judged that the consequent is actually non-existent, while we only claim that the conditional syllogism yields truth only when its two premises are true, and that the non-existence of the consequent in reality entails the non-existence of the antecedent in it. So then it appears that the doubt is extremely foolish. And know that in it (the doubt) is a distortion of words from their places; for the origin of the doubt was given by al-Fadl al-Abhari.
Source pages p. 539

Commentary
This passage addresses a doubt (shakk) raised about the conditional syllogism, specifically about the validity of the mood that negates the consequent. The doubt is attributed to al-Jawnpuri (Abd al-Baqi) and is also mentioned in the gloss (hashiya). The doubt is that denying the consequent does not necessarily entail denying the antecedent because it is possible that the non-existence of the consequent is impossible. In that case, if the consequent cannot be non-existent, then the entailment relation does not hold in that situation, so the antecedent's non-existence does not follow. The author (the commentator) resolves the doubt by clarifying the definition of entailment (luzum). Entailment is the impossibility of separation (imtina' al-infikak) in all times ('all times' meaning all possible circumstances). Therefore, the time of separation (when the consequent is impossible) is included in the 'all times', so the seeming non-entailment is actually a denial of the entailment itself, which contradicts the assumed existence of entailment. The author further notes that if the doubt is applied to a conditional syllogism where the conditional premise is particular and the exception is universal, the resolution is difficult. However, the correct answer is that the doubt actually concerns the truth of the exceptional premise: the objector assumes that the consequent is actually non-existent, but the syllogism only claims truth if the premises are true. Once the consequent is truly absent, the entailment holds because the antecedent must also be absent. Thus the doubt is 'extremely foolish' (fi ghayat al-sakhafa). The author concludes by noting that the doubt originates from al-Abhari, but has been distorted. An Islamic example: Consider the conditional 'If it is wine, it is forbidden.' The entai

منع استلزام الرفع الرفع

C) By stating the conditional must be particular.
D) By denying the antecedent.

Key Takeaway
Entailment (luzum) in conditional logic is defined as the impossibility of separation across all possible circumstances, a definition that renders the doubt about modus tollens self-contradictory.

Resolving a Doubt About Conditional Syllogism
Author explains a doubt about conditional syllogism and resolves it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and resolve a doubt about the validity of modus tollens in conditional syllogism, using the definition of implication as impossibility of separation.

Core Idea
The doubt that impossibility of the consequent destroys the implication is self-contradictory: implication covers all times, including the time of separation.

This lesson examines a subtle doubt raised by al-Jawnfuri about conditional syllogism: if the consequent is impossible, does the implication still hold? The commentator provides a rigorous resolution based on the definition of logical implication. By understanding this resolution, you will grasp the foundation of modus tollens and see why the doubt is baseless.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Logical Form at Stake
Recognize that the doubt targets modus tollens: from 'If A then B' and 'not B', we infer 'not A'.
This is the standard form of conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i).
2
Extract the Doubt's Reasoning
The doubt claims: If the consequent (B) is impossible (cannot exist), then the implication (luzum) between A and B may disappear, so denying B does not necessarily deny A because the implicative link is gone.
The doubt suggests that the impossibility of B severs the logical connection.
3
Apply the Definition of Implication
Recall that implication (luzum) is defined as the impossibility of separation in all times where the antecedent exists. The time when implication fails is included in 'all times', so denying the implication contradicts its assumed existence.
This shows the doubt is internally inconsistent if the implication is granted.
4
Refine the Resolution: Truth of the Exception
The commentator further argues that the doubt actually reduces to questioning whether the exception premise (not B) is true in reality. If the premises are true, the inference holds.
Thus, the doubt is not a logical flaw but a factual question about the truth of the minor premise.
5
Conclude the Doubt's Status
The doubt is deemed 'extremely senseless' because it confuses the logical form with the factual truth of the premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَهَهُنَا شَكٍّ، وَقِيلَ عَويص، قاله الجونفوري، وهو عبد الباقي، كذا في «الحاشية»، وَهُوَ مَنْعُ اسْتِلْزَامِ الرَّفْعِ الرَّفْعَ، أي منع استلزام رفع التالي رفع المقدم؛ لِجَوَازِ اسْتِحَالَةِ انْتِفَاءِ اللَّازِمِ، فَإِذَا وَقَعَ لَمْ يَبْقَ اللُّزُومُ مَعَهُ، فَلَا يَلْزَمُ انْتِفَاءُ الْمَلْزُومِ؛ لأن انتفاء الملزوم إنما كان بعلاقة اللزوم، وقد ارتفعت. أقول: حَلُّهُ: إِنَّ اللُّزُومَ حَقِيقَةَ امْتِنَاعُ الاِنْفِكَاكِ فِي جَمِيعِ الْأَوْقَاتِ، فَوَقْتُ الِانْفِكَاكِ - وَهُوَ وَقْتُ عَدَمِ بَقَاءِ اللُّزُومِ - دَاخِل فِي الْجَمِيعِ، فَهَذَا الْمَنْعُ بِالْحَقِيقَةِ يَرْجِعُ إِلَى مَنْع اللَّزُومِ، وَقَدْ فُرِضَ وُجُودُهُ، خُلْفٌ. فِيهِ أَنَّهُ لَيْسَ حَقِيقَةَ اللُّزُومِ الْكُلِّيِّ إِلَاّ عَدَمُ انْفِكَاكِ التَّالِي عَنِ الْمُقَدَّمِ فِي جَمِيعِ التَّقَادِيرِ الْمُـمْكِنَةِ الاجْتِمَاعِ مَعَ الْمُقَدَّمِ، كَمَا نَقَلَ الْمُصَنِّفُ عَنِ الشَّيْخِ سَابِقًا، وَوَقْتُ عَدَمِ اللُّزُومِ غَيْرُ مُمْكِنِ الاجْتِمَاعِ مَعَ الْمُقَدَّمِ، عَلَى أَنَّ الشَّكَّ إِنْ وُجِّهَ إِلَى الِاسْتِثْنَائِيِّ الَّذِي فِيهِ الشَّرْطِيَّةُ جُزْئِيَّةٌ وَالِاسْتِثْنَاءُ كُلِّيٌّ لَمْ يَكَدْ يَتِمَّ الْحَلُّ. فَإِذَنِ الْحَقُّ فِي الْجَوَابِ أَنَّ هَذَا الْمَنْعَ رَاجِعٌ إِلَى صِدْقِ الِاسْتِثْنَاءِ؛ إِذْ حُكِمَ فِيهِ بِأَنَّ التَّالِيَ مُنْتَفٍ فِي الْوَاقِعِ، وَنَحْنُ إِنَّمَا نَدَّعِي أَنَّ الِاسْتِثْنَائِيَّ إِنَّمَا يُنْتِجُ صَادِقَةً إِذَا كَانَتْ مُقَدِّمَتَاهُ صَادِقَتَيْنِ، وَأَنَّ ارْتِفَاعَ اللَّازِمِ فِي الْوَاقِعِ يَسْتَلْزِمُ انْتِفَاءَ الْمَلْزُومِ فِيهِ، فَإِذَن ظَهَرَ أَنَّ الشَّكَّ فِي غَايَةِ السَّخَافَةِ.
And here is a doubt, and it was said to be difficult, stated by al-Jawnfuri, and he is Abd al-Baqi, as in the Hashiya. It is the prevention of the implication of the negation of the consequent implying the negation of the antecedent, i.e., prevention of the implication of raising the consequent raising the antecedent, due to the possibility of the impossibility of the non-existence of the necessary. So if it occurs, the implication does not remain with it, so the non-existence of the necessitated does not follow, because the non-existence of the necessitated was only due to the relation of implication, and it is removed. I say: its solution: implication in reality is the impossibility of separation in all times. So the time of separation - and it is the time when the implication does not remain - is included in 'all'. So this prevention in reality returns to preventing the implication, and its existence has been assumed, which is a contradiction. In it [it is replied] that the reality of universal implication is nothing other than the non-separation of the consequent from the antecedent in all possible scenarios that can coexist with the antecedent, as the author transmitted from the Shaykh earlier, and the time of non-implication cannot coexist with the antecedent. Moreover, if the doubt is directed to the conditional [syllogism] in which the conditional premise is particular and the exception is universal, the solution would hardly be complete. So the truth in the answer is that this prevention returns to the truthfulness of the exception, for it is judged therein that the consequent is non-existent in reality, and we only claim that the conditional syllogism yields a true conclusion only if its two premises are true, and that the non-existence of the necessary in reality necessitates the non-existence of the necessitated therein. So then it appears that the doubt is extremely senseless.
Source pages p. 539

Commentary
This passage addresses a doubt (shakk) about the validity of the conditional syllogism's inference form: from 'if A then B' and 'not B' we conclude 'not A'. The doubt claims that if the consequent (B) is impossible to exist, then the implication (luzum) between A and B might be removed, so denying B does not deny A because the implication no longer holds. The commentator first states the doubt and then provides a solution. The solution is that implication is defined as the impossibility of separation in all times. The time when the implication fails is part of 'all times', so the prevention of the implication itself amounts to denying the implication, which contradicts the assumed existence of the implication. However, a finer point is raised: the universal implication only requires non-separation in all possible scenarios where the antecedent exists. The time when implication fails is not a scenario where the antecedent exists (since the implication is defined relative to the antecedent's existence), so the doubt is invalid. Finally, the commentator argues that the doubt actually concerns the truth of the exception premise (that the consequent is indeed non-existent in reality), not a flaw in the logical form. If the premises are true, the inference holds. Thus the doubt is baseless.

لزوم
استثنائي

Types of Disjunctive Syllogisms and Their Valid Conclusions
Author enumerates the valid conclusions for each type of disjunctive syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the three types of disjunctive syllogisms (mani'at al-jam', mani'at al-khuluw, haqiqiyya) and determine which inferences are valid for each.

Core Idea
Not all disjunctions behave the same: knowing whether the disjuncts can both be true or both be false determines which inferences are valid.

In disjunctive syllogisms, the relationship between the disjuncts determines whether positing one allows you to negate the other, or negating one allows you to posit the other. The classical logicians identified three types: exclusive (mani'at al-jam'), inclusive (mani'at al-khuluw), and true disjunction (haqiqiyya). This passage explains the valid and invalid inferences for each type.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
Note that the passage discusses mani'at al-jam' (exclusive disjunction), mani'at al-khuluw (inclusive disjunction), and haqiqiyya (true disjunction). Each has a different constraint on the truth values of the disjuncts.
These terms are standard in mantiq and appear in many Islamic logic textbooks.
2
Understand the constraint of each type
For mani'at al-jam', the disjuncts cannot both be true. For mani'at al-khuluw, they cannot both be false. For haqiqiyya, they can neither both be true nor both be false.
These constraints determine which inferences are valid.
3
Apply the inference rules
In mani'at al-jam', positing one disjunct allows you to negate the other. In mani'at al-khuluw, negating one allows you to posit the other. In haqiqiyya, both inferences are valid.
The invalid directions are also explained: negating in exclusive does not affirm, and positing in inclusive does not negate.
4
Test with Islamic examples
Consider 'This act is either obligatory or forbidden' (exclusive). If it is obligatory, it is not forbidden. Consider 'The world is either eternal or created' (inclusive). If it is not eternal, it is created. Consider 'This number is either even or odd' (true). All four inferences hold.
These examples show the practical application in fiqh and kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَفِي الْمُنْفَصِلَةِ يُنْتِجُ الْوَضْعُ، أي وضعُ كُلِّ الرَّفْعَ، أي رفع الآخر. كَمَانِعَةِ الْجَمْعِ، ولا ينتج الرفع الوضعَ؛ لجواز ارتفاع الجزئين، وَالرَّفْعُ الْوَضْعَ، أي ينتج رفعُ كل وضعَ الآخر، كَمَانِعَةِ الْخُلُو، ولا ينتج الوضعُ الرفع؛ لجواز اجتماع الجزئين، وَالْحَقِيقِيَّةُ تُنْتِجُ النَّتَائِجَ الْأَرْبَعَ؛ إذ وضع كلِّ يُنتج رفع الآخر، ورفع كلِّ وضعَ الآخر.
And in the disjunctive [syllogism], the positing produces the negation, i.e., positing each [disjunct] produces the negation of the other, as in the exclusive [disjunction] (maniat al-jam'), and the negation does not produce the positing due to the possibility of both parts being negated. And the negation produces the positing, i.e., negating each produces the positing of the other, as in the inclusive [disjunction] (maniat al-khuluw), and the positing does not produce the negation due to the possibility of both parts being affirmed. And the true [disjunction] (haqiqiyya) produces all four conclusions, since positing each produces the negation of the other, and negating each produces the positing of the other.
Source pages p. 540

Commentary
This passage classifies disjunctive syllogisms into three types and states their valid conclusions. The first type is 'maniat al-jam'' (exclusive disjunction) where the two disjuncts cannot be simultaneously true. In such a disjunction, if you posit (affirm) one disjunct, you can validly conclude the negation of the other. However, if you negate one disjunct, you cannot conclude the affirmation of the other because both could be false. The second type is 'maniat al-khuluw' (inclusive disjunction) where the two disjuncts cannot both be false simultaneously. Here, if you negate one disjunct, you validly conclude the affirmation of the other. But if you posit one, you cannot conclude the negation of the other because both could be true. The third type is 'haqiqiyya' (true disjunction) which combines both properties: the two disjuncts cannot both be true nor both false. In this case, all four inferences are valid: positing one yields the negation of the other, and negating one yields the affirmation of the other. An example from fiqh: 'This act is either obligatory or forbidden' could be maniat al-jam' if it cannot be both; if it is obligatory, it is not forbidden. But if it is not obligatory, it could be forbidden or permissible, so the inference is not valid in maniat al-jam'. In maniat al-khuluw, 'The world is either eternal or created' – it cannot be neither (assuming it exists), so if it is not eternal, it must be created. In haqiqiyya, 'This number is either even or odd' – it cannot be both or neither, so all four inferences are valid.

Key Terms
مانعة الجمع (mani'at al-jam'): A disjunction that prevents both disjuncts from being true together (exclusive or). [Note: Standard in mantiq.]
مانعة الخلو (mani'at al-khuluw): A disjunction that prevents both disjuncts from being false together (inclusive or). [Note: Standard.]
حقيقية (haqiqiyya): A true disjunction that prevents both truth and both falsehood (exhaustive and exclusive). [Note: Standard.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The other disjunct is also true.
B) The other disjunct is false.
C) The other disjunct is possible.
D) Nothing can be concluded.

Key Takeaway
The validity of inferences in disjunctive syllogisms depends on the specific type of disjunction, which determines whether the disjuncts can be both true or both false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Consider the disjunction: 'This act is either wajib or haram.' What type of disjunction is this likely to be (exclusive, inclusive, or true)? If it is wajib, what can you conclude about haram? If it is not wajib, can you conclude it is haram? Why or why not?

Compound Syllogisms: Connected vs Separated Conclusions
Definition and division of compound syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mawsul al-nata'ij and mafusul al-nata'ij compound syllogisms, and construct each type using Islamic legal examples.

Core Idea
A compound syllogism chains valid syllogisms; its power lies in whether intermediate conclusions are hidden or explicit.

Having mastered simple syllogisms, we now combine them. The author defines the compound syllogism and divides it into two types: connected conclusions (sorites) and separated conclusions. This structure is crucial for constructing lengthy chains of reasoning in kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Note that the compound syllogism is defined as 'consisting of multiple syllogisms' (qiyas murakkab min aqisatin). Each component is a valid simple syllogism.
2
Recognize the two types
Find the two types: mawsul al-nata'ij (connected conclusions) where intermediate conclusions are implicit, and mafusul al-nata'ij (separated conclusions) where each conclusion is explicitly stated and reused.
In Arabic, 'mawsul' means connected, 'mafusul' means separated.
3
Examine the examples with variables
In the mawsul example, premises are listed without 'so' statements. In the mafusul example, each syllogism ends with 'fa-kull...' stating the conclusion before moving on.
Variables: ج, ب, أ, هـ, ز represent terms.
4
Translate to an Islamic example
Construct a compound syllogism on a fiqh topic: e.g., Every khamr is intoxicating, every intoxicating thing is forbidden, so every khamr is forbidden. Then extend: every forbidden thing must be avoided, so every khamr must be avoided. Write this in both forms.
Use the same terms to see the difference.
5
Verify each link's validity
Check that each simple syllogism in the chain is valid (first figure preferred). A compound syllogism is only as strong as its weakest link.
If any component is invalid, the whole chain collapses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَمَّا فَرَغَ عَنِ الْقِيَاسِ الْبَسِيطِ بِأَقْسَامِهِ شَرَعَ فِي الْمُرَكَّبِ، وَقَالَ: الْقِيَاسُ الْمُرَكَّبُ مِنْ أَقِيسَةٍ مَوْصُولُ النَّتَائِجِ، نَحْوَ «كُلُّ (ج) (ب)، وَكُلُّ (ب) (أ)، وَكُلُّ (أ) (هـ)، وَكُلُّ (هـ) (أ)»، أَوْ مَفْصُولُهَا، نَحْوَ «كُلُّ (ج) (ب)، وَكُلُّ (ب) (أ)، فَكُلُّ (ج) (أ)، وَكُلُّ (أ) (هـ)، فَكُلُّ (ج) (هـ)، وَكُلُّ (هـ) (أ)، فَكُلُّ (ج) (ز)». أَقْسَةٌ مُتَعَدِّدَةٌ يَرْجِعُ إِلَى الْأَقْسَامِ الْمَذْكُورَةِ كُلُّ مِنْهَا.
And when he finished with the simple syllogism with its divisions, he began with the compound [syllogism]. He said: The compound syllogism consists of multiple syllogisms: either with connected conclusions, like 'Every (ج) is (ب), and every (ب) is (أ), and every (أ) is (هـ), and every (هـ) is (أ)' – or with separated conclusions, like 'Every (ج) is (ب), and every (ب) is (أ), so every (ج) is (أ); and every (أ) is (هـ), so every (ج) is (هـ); and every (هـ) is (أ), so every (ج) is (ز)'. Multiple syllogisms each returning to the mentioned divisions.
Source pages p. 540

Commentary
This passage defines the compound syllogism (qiyas murakkab) as a chain of multiple simple syllogisms. It divides it into two types: 'mawsul al-nata'ij' (connected conclusions) where the conclusion of one syllogism becomes a premise for the next, and 'mafusul al-nata'ij' (separated conclusions) where each syllogism's conclusion is stated explicitly without being reused as a premise. The examples use variables (ج, ب, أ, هـ, ز) representing terms. In the connected type, the chain does not show intermediate conclusions; rather, the premises are listed sequentially and the final conclusion is implied. In the separated type, each intermediate conclusion is explicitly drawn and then used in the next syllogism. This is analogous to a sorites in Western logic. An Islamic example: 'Every khamr is intoxicating, every intoxicating thing is forbidden, so every khamr is forbidden' is a simple syllogism. A compound syllogism would be: 'Every khamr is intoxicating, every intoxicating thing is harmful, so every khamr is harmful. Every harmful thing is forbidden, so every khamr is forbidden.' This is the separated type. The connected type would combine these: 'Every khamr is intoxicating, every intoxicating thing is harmful, every harmful thing is forbidden' without stating the intermediate step, concluding directly 'Every khamr is forbidden'.

Key Terms
قياس مركب
موصول النتائج
مفصول النتائج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes mawsul al-nata'ij from mafusul al-nata'ij?
A) The number of premises is greater in mawsul.
B) Intermediate conclusions are not explicitly stated in mawsul.
C) Mafusul can only be used with three terms.
D) Mawsul requires a different figure.

Key Takeaway
A compound syllogism chains multiple valid syllogisms, and the key distinction is whether intermediate conclusions are hidden (mawsul) or explicit (mafusul).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Then, every exchange of food items with increase is a transaction; every forbidden transaction is prohibited; therefore every exchange of food items with increase is prohibited.
?
Take the above chain and rewrite it as a mawsul al-nata'ij (sorites) without stating intermediate conclusions.

Reductio ad Absurdum: Its Logical Reduction to Standard Forms
Author defines reductio and reduces it to a conjunctive conditional and an exceptive syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reduce any reductio ad absurdum argument to a standard conjunctive conditional and exceptive syllogism, and identify the two possible reductions.

Core Idea
Reductio ad absurdum is not a separate form; it reduces to a conditional and an exceptive syllogism.

In this passage, the author defines reductio (al-khalf) and then performs a crucial logical operation: he shows that what appears to be a distinct proof method is actually a combination of two standard forms—a conjunctive conditional and an exceptive syllogism. Understanding this reduction allows you to analyse any reductio argument in terms of its constituent parts, and even to see an alternative reduction to two exceptive syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Find the definition and the aim
The author defines reductio as proving a claim by refuting its contradictory. Identify the claim (the desired conclusion) and its contradictory (nāqiḍ) in the text.
The contradictory is what you assume to derive an absurdity.
2
Extract the conditional premise
The author states: 'if the desired conclusion were not established, then its contradictory would be established; and whenever its contradictory is established, an absurdity is established.' This is a conditional linking the assumption (not-p) to an absurdity (mustahīl). Write it as: If not-p then absurdity.
The conditional may be compound; the key is the implication from the assumption to the absurdity.
3
Identify the exceptive syllogism
The author then says 'the consequent is false' (i.e., the absurdity is false). This is the exceptive premise: absurdity is false (mustahīl is bāṭil). Combine with the conditional to conclude the opposite of the assumption.
This forms a modus tollens: if not-p then absurdity, absurdity is false, therefore not not-p (i.e., p).
4
Reconstruct the reduction as a compound syllogism
The author reduces reductio to a conjunctive conditional (the conditional premise) and an exceptive syllogism (the modus tollens step). Write out the complete argument in standard form.
This is the main reduction attributed to the Shaykh.
5
Consider the alternative reduction
The author also proposes a reduction to two exceptive syllogisms. Think how that might work: first exceptive syllogism establishes that the contradictory assumption leads to absurdity, second exceptive syllogism negates the absurdity. Try to outline it.
This alternative shows flexibility in logical analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمِنْهُ الْخُلْفُ ، وَهُوَ: مَا يُقْصَدُ فِيهِ إِثْبَاتُ المطلوب بإبطال نقيضه. من القياس الاستثنائي قال الشيخ في وجه تسميته خلفا إنه يعطي النتيجة من خلفه، فعلى هذا ينبغي أن يقرأ «الخَلْفُ» بفتح الخاء، والمشهور الضم بمعنى الردي، وإليه ميل. وَمَرْجِعُهُ إِلَى اقْتِرَانِيٍّ شَرْطِيٍّ وَاسْتِثْنَائِيٍّ ; فإن الحاصل: لو لم يكن المطلوب ثابتا كان نقيضه ثابتا ، وكلما كان نقيضه ثابتا ثبت المحال ، والتالي باطل. هذا إذا كانت الشرطية المقدمة الثانية من الاقتراني ضرورية، وكذا استثناء الاستثنائي، وأما إذا كان إحداهما نظرية يحتاج إلى قياس آخر لإثباتها. ثم هذا الإرجاع مذهب الشيخ، ويمكن إرجاعه إلى قياسين استثنائيين، بأن يقال لو لم يثبت المدعى ثبت نقيضه، والتالي باطل؛ إذ لو لم يبطل لزم المحال، والتالي باطل. وههنا شك، وهو: أنه جرت العادة بإثبات استحالة المستحيلات بالقياسات الخلفية :هكذا لو لم يكن هذا الشيء محالا كان ممكنا، ولو كان ...
And among them is al-khalf (reductio). It is that which aims to prove the desired conclusion by refuting its contradictory. It is a type of exceptive syllogism. The Shaykh said about the reason for calling it 'khalf' that it gives the result from behind it; thus it should be read with fat7a on the kha', but the common pronunciation is with damm meaning 'bad', and preference is towards that. Its reduction is to a conjunctive conditional and an exceptive [syllogism]. For the result is: if the desired conclusion were not established, then its contradictory would be established; and whenever its contradictory is established, an absurdity is established; and the consequent is false. This is when the second premise of the conjunctive conditional is necessary, and likewise the exception of the exceptive. But if one of them is theoretical, it needs another syllogism to prove it. This reduction is the view of the Shaykh. It is also possible to reduce it to two exceptive syllogisms, by saying: if the claim is not proven, its contradictory is proven, and the consequent is false; for if it were not false, an absurdity would follow, and the consequent is false. Here there is a doubt: that the custom is to prove the impossibility of impossibilities by reductio arguments thus: if this thing were not impossible, it would be possible, and if it were...
Source pages p. 541

Commentary
This passage defines reductio ad absurdum (قياس الخلف) and analyzes its logical structure. The author first gives the definition: it is a proof that establishes the desired conclusion by refuting its contradictory (nāqiḍ). It is a kind of exceptive syllogism (qiyās istithnā'ī). There is a philological note: the word 'khalf' can be pronounced with fat7a (khalf) meaning 'behind' or with dammah (khulf) meaning 'bad'. The Shaykh (al-Tūsī?) comments that it gives the result from behind, so the fat7a is appropriate, but the common pronunciation is with damm. The core logical analysis: reductio reduces to a conjunctive conditional (iqṭirānī shartī) and an exceptive syllogism (istithnā'ī). The argument: assume the contrary, derive an absurdity; the conditional is 'if not-p then q' where q is absurd, and by exceptive we negate q, conclude not-not-p, i.e., p. The author notes that if the second premise of the conditional (i.e., the link between the assumption and the absurdity) is not necessary but theoretical, a further syllogism is needed. Then he presents an alternative reduction to two exceptive syllogisms. Finally, he raises a doubt: although customarily reductio is used to prove impossibility by assuming possibility and deriving contradiction, the exact formulation may vary. This passage is a typical logical commentary on the structure of proof by contradiction, familiar in Islamic theology for proving the temporality of the world, impossibility of infinite regress, etc.

Key Terms

القياس الاستثنائي (al-qiyās al-istithnā'ī)
اقتران شرطي (iqṭirānī shartī)

Key Takeaway
Reductio ad absurdum is not a primitive form; it is logically reducible to a conditional premise linking the negation of the claim to an absurdity, and an exceptive step negating that absurdity to affirm the claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Definition and Naming of Qiyas al-Khalf
Definition of qiyas al-khalf and its naming

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define qiyas al-khalf and explain the reasoning behind its two possible pronunciations.

Core Idea
Qiyas al-khalf proves a conclusion by nullifying its contradictory through conditional reasoning.

In this lesson, we explore the definition of qiyas al-khalf (proof by contradiction), a special type of exceptive syllogism. The passage begins by defining it and discussing the variant pronunciations of its name. Understanding this foundation is crucial for grasping its later logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Locate the definition in the Arabic: 'وهو: ما يقصد فيه إثبات المطلوب بإبطال نقيضه'. This tells us that qiyas al-khalf aims to prove the desired conclusion by invalidating its contradictory.
2
Understand the naming
The passage mentions two pronunciations: 'الخَلْف' (fatha, meaning opposite/behind) and 'الخُلْف' (damma, meaning bad). Avicenna prefers the former because the argument gives the result from its opposite. Note the author's preference for 'الخُلْف'.
This distinction shows awareness of semantic nuance in technical terms.
3
Relate to exceptive syllogism
The passage classifies qiyas al-khalf as a type of exceptive syllogism (al-qiyas al-istithna'i). This means it involves a conditional premise and an exception (istithna').

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وَمِنْهُ الْخُلْفُ (١) ، وَهُوَ: مَا يُقْصَدْ فِيهِ إِثْبَاتُ المطلوب بإبطال نقيضه) . من القياس الاستثنائي قال الشيخ في وجه تسميته خلفا إنه يعطي النتيجة من خلفه، فعلى هذا ينبغي أن يقرأ «الخَلْفُ» بفتح الخاء، والمشهور الضم بمعنى الردي، وإليه ميل (٢). (وَمَرْجِعُهُ إِلَى اقْتِرَانِيَّ شرطي (وَاسْتِثْنَائِي (٣)) ؛ فإن الحاصل: لو لم يكن المطلوب ثابتا كان نقيضه ثابتا ، وكلما كان نقيضه ثابتا ثبت المحال ، والتالي باطل. (2) هذا إذا كانت الشرطية المقدمة الثانية من الاقتراني ضرورية )، وكذا استثناء الاستثنائي، وأما إذا كان إحداهما نظرية يحتاج (9) إلى قياس آخر لإثباتها. ثم هذا الإرجاع مذهب الشيخ، ويمكن إرجاعه إلى قياسين استثنائيين، بأن يقال لو لم يثبت المدعى ثبت نقيضه، والتالي باطل؛ إذ لو لم : يبطل لزم المحال ، والتالي باطل. وههنا شك، وهو: أنه جرت العادة بإثبات استحالة المستحيلات بالقياسات الخلفية :هكذا لو لم يكن هذا الشيء محالا كان ممكنا، ولو كان ممكنا لزم من وقوعه المحال، وهو باطل، فإمكانه باطل. ويرد عليه أن الممكن ربما يستلزم محالا ، كعدم المعلول الأول عدم الباري تعالى - جلّ مجده وذكره تعالى عنه علوا كبيرا ـ فمن لزوم المحال لا يلزم استحالة. أجاب المحقق الدواني، بأن الممكن إنما يستلزم محالا إذا كان ممتنعا بالغير، والمطلوب من القياسات الخلفية ليس إلا مطلق الاستحالة، وهي لازمة من استحالة المحال. هذا والظاهر أن المحال اللازم إن كان عدمه على فرض وجوده فيفيد استحالة الملزوم؛ لأن فرض وجوده حينئذ صار في قوة اجتماع النقيضين، وهو مستحيل بالذات، وفيما عدا ذلك يحتمل الوجهين . هذا، والله أعلم بالصواب.
(And among the types of hypothetical syllogism is 'al-khalf' (1), and it is: that in which the intended conclusion is proved by nullifying its contradictory.) It is from the exceptional syllogism. The Shaykh said regarding the reason for its naming 'khalf' that it gives the result from its opposite, so on this basis it should be read 'al-khalf' with a fatha on the kha, but the well-known reading is with a damma, meaning 'bad', and this is preferred (2). (And its reference is to a hypothetical categorical syllogism and an exceptional one (3)); for the result is: if the desired conclusion were not established, then its contradictory would be established; and whenever its contradictory is established, an impossibility is established; and the consequent is false. (2) This is if the conditional major premise of the categorical syllogism is necessary, and likewise the exception of the exceptional syllogism. But if one of them is theoretical, it needs (9) another syllogism to prove it. Then this reduction is the Shaykh's doctrine, and it is possible to reduce it to two exceptional syllogisms, by saying: if the claim were not established, its contradictory would be established; and the consequent is false, for if it were not false, an impossibility would be necessary, and the consequent is false. And here there is a doubt, which is: it is customary to prove the impossibility of impossibilities by arguments from contradiction in this way: if this thing were not impossible, it would be possible; and if it were possible, then from its occurrence an impossibility would follow, and that is false, so its possibility is false. And it is objected that the possible sometimes entails an impossibility, like the non-existence of the first effect [would entail] the non-existence of the Most High Creator—glory be to Him, exalted is He above that greatly—so from the entailment of impossibility it does not follow that it is impossible. The investigator al-Dawani replied that the possible only entails an impossibility if it is impossible through another, and the required from the arguments from contradiction is only absolute impossibility, which follows from the impossibility of the impossible. This, and it appears that the necessary impossible if its non-existence is conditional upon its existence, then it yields the impossibility of the substratum, because the assumption of its existence then becomes equivalent to the conjunction of two contradictories, which is impossible by essence. And besides that, it admits two possibilities. This, and God knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 541

Commentary
This passage defines 'qiyas al-khalf' (argument by contradiction or reductio ad absurdum) and explains its logical structure. The author begins by stating that it is a type of exceptional syllogism (al-qiyas al-istithna'i), where one aims to prove a conclusion by nullifying its contradictory. The name 'khalf' (with a fatha) literally means 'behind' or 'opposite', i.e., it gives the result from its opposite, but the common pronunciation is with a damma meaning 'bad'. The key point is that qiyas al-khalf can be reduced to a combination of a conditional categorical syllogism (iqtirani sharti) and an exceptional syllogism (istithna'i). The reduction works as follows: if the desired conclusion were false, its contradictory would be true; and if the contradictory were true, an impossibility would follow; that impossibility is false, so the contradictory is false, and thus the original conclusion is true. The author notes a condition: the conditional major premise must be necessary, otherwise another syllogism is needed to prove it. He mentions that this reduction is the view of Shaykh al-Rais (Avicenna), and one could also reduce it to two exceptional syllogisms. The passage then presents a doubt (shakk) concerning the use of qiyas al-khalf to prove impossibility: normally, to prove something is impossible, one assumes it is possible, then derives an impossibility from that assumption, thus concluding its impossibility. But the objection is that a possible thing can sometimes entail an impossibility (e.g., the possibility of the non-existence of the first effect would entail the non-existence of God, which is impossible, but the non-existence of the first effect itself is not impossible). The response by al-Dawani clarifies that a possible only entails an impossibility if it is impossible through another (mumtani' bi al-ghayr), and the required impossibility from these arguments is absolute impossibility (mustahil bi al-dhat). The author adds his own remark: if the impossible necessary (al-muhal al-lazim) is such that its non-existence is conditional upon its existence, then it does imply the impossibility of the substratum because assuming its existence becomes like assuming the conjunction of contradictories. Otherwise, it remains open. The commentary serves to refine the understanding of when qiyas al-khalf yields a valid conclusion of impossibility.

Key Terms
الخلف
الاستثنائي

Key Takeaway
Qiyas al-khalf is defined as proving a conclusion by nullifying its contradictory, and its name reflects this logical operation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a qiyas al-khalf to prove that nabidh (date wine) is forbidden: assume it is permissible, then derive that an intoxicant is permissible, which contradicts the known ruling that all intoxicants are forbidden. Conclude that nabidh is forbidden.

Reduction of Qiyas al-Khalf and the Doubt on Impossibility
Reduction of qiyas al-khalf and the doubt on impossibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reduce qiyas al-khalf to a conditional categorical and an exceptive syllogism, and evaluate the objection concerning its use to prove impossibility.

Core Idea
Qiyas al-khalf reduces to a conditional categorical and an exceptive syllogism, but proving impossibility requires distinguishing necessary from theoretical premises.

This lesson delves into the logical reduction of qiyas al-khalf as explained by Avicenna, and examines a doubt concerning its use to prove impossibility. The response by al-Dawani clarifies the conditions under which the argument is valid. Understanding this reduction strengthens your ability to analyze complex arguments in Islamic scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the reduction components
The passage states that qiyas al-khalf reduces to a conditional categorical syllogism (iqtirani sharti) and an exceptive syllogism (istithna'i). The combined reasoning: if the contradictory of the conclusion is true, then an impossibility follows; that impossibility is false; therefore, the contradictory is false and the conclusion true.
The conditional categorical syllogism has a conditional major premise and a categorical minor premise.
2
Distinguish necessary vs. theoretical premises
The conditional major premise must be necessary (daruri); if it is theoretical (nazari), it requires another syllogism to prove it. This affects the argument's independence.
Necessary premises are self-evident; theoretical premises require proof.
3
Analyze the doubt and response
The doubt: qiyas al-khalf is used to prove something impossible by assuming it possible and deriving an impossibility. But an objection arises: a possible thing can entail an impossibility (e.g., the possible non-existence of the first effect would entail God's non-existence, but the non-existence of the effect is not impossible). Al-Dawani responds: a possible only entails an impossibility if it is impossible through another (mumtani' bi al-ghayr), whereas the argument aims at absolute impossibility (mustahil bi al-dhat).
The author adds: if the impossibility's non-existence depends on the existence of the substratum, then it does imply impossibility of the substratum.
4
Apply the condition to a theological example
Consider proving that the world is temporally originated: assume it is eternal, then it would require an infinite regress of causes, which is impossible. Here, the premise 'if the world is eternal, then infinite regress occurs' must be necessary; if it is theoretical, another syllogism is needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وَمِنْهُ الْخُلْفُ (١) ، وَهُوَ: مَا يُقْصَدْ فِيهِ إِثْبَاتُ المطلوب بإبطال نقيضه) . من القياس الاستثنائي قال الشيخ في وجه تسميته خلفا إنه يعطي النتيجة من خلفه، فعلى هذا ينبغي أن يقرأ «الخَلْفُ» بفتح الخاء، والمشهور الضم بمعنى الردي، وإليه ميل (٢). (وَمَرْجِعُهُ إِلَى اقْتِرَانِيَّ شرطي (وَاسْتِثْنَائِي (٣)) ؛ فإن الحاصل: لو لم يكن المطلوب ثابتا كان نقيضه ثابتا ، وكلما كان نقيضه ثابتا ثبت المحال ، والتالي باطل. (2) هذا إذا كانت الشرطية المقدمة الثانية من الاقتراني ضرورية )، وكذا استثناء الاستثنائي، وأما إذا كان إحداهما نظرية يحتاج (9) إلى قياس آخر لإثباتها. ثم هذا الإرجاع مذهب الشيخ، ويمكن إرجاعه إلى قياسين استثنائيين، بأن يقال لو لم يثبت المدعى ثبت نقيضه، والتالي باطل؛ إذ لو لم : يبطل لزم المحال ، والتالي باطل. وههنا شك، وهو: أنه جرت العادة بإثبات استحالة المستحيلات بالقياسات الخلفية :هكذا لو لم يكن هذا الشيء محالا كان ممكنا، ولو كان ممكنا لزم من وقوعه المحال، وهو باطل، فإمكانه باطل. ويرد عليه أن الممكن ربما يستلزم محالا ، كعدم المعلول الأول عدم الباري تعالى - جلّ مجده وذكره تعالى عنه علوا كبيرا ـ فمن لزوم المحال لا يلزم استحالة. أجاب المحقق الدواني، بأن الممكن إنما يستلزم محالا إذا كان ممتنعا بالغير، والمطلوب من القياسات الخلفية ليس إلا مطلق الاستحالة، وهي لازمة من استحالة المحال. هذا والظاهر أن المحال اللازم إن كان عدمه على فرض وجوده فيفيد استحالة الملزوم؛ لأن فرض وجوده حينئذ صار في قوة اجتماع النقيضين، وهو مستحيل بالذات، وفيما عدا ذلك يحتمل الوجهين . هذا، والله أعلم بالصواب.
(And among the types of hypothetical syllogism is 'al-khalf' (1), and it is: that in which the intended conclusion is proved by nullifying its contradictory.) It is from the exceptional syllogism. The Shaykh said regarding the reason for its naming 'khalf' that it gives the result from its opposite, so on this basis it should be read 'al-khalf' with a fatha on the kha, but the well-known reading is with a damma, meaning 'bad', and this is preferred (2). (And its reference is to a hypothetical categorical syllogism and an exceptional one (3)); for the result is: if the desired conclusion were not established, then its contradictory would be established; and whenever its contradictory is established, an impossibility is established; and the consequent is false. (2) This is if the conditional major premise of the categorical syllogism is necessary, and likewise the exception of the exceptional syllogism. But if one of them is theoretical, it needs (9) another syllogism to prove it. Then this reduction is the Shaykh's doctrine, and it is possible to reduce it to two exceptional syllogisms, by saying: if the claim were not established, its contradictory would be established; and the consequent is false, for if it were not false, an impossibility would be necessary, and the consequent is false. And here there is a doubt, which is: it is customary to prove the impossibility of impossibilities by arguments from contradiction in this way: if this thing were not impossible, it would be possible; and if it were possible, then from its occurrence an impossibility would follow, and that is false, so its possibility is false. And it is objected that the possible sometimes entails an impossibility, like the non-existence of the first effect [would entail] the non-existence of the Most High Creator—glory be to Him, exalted is He above that greatly—so from the entailment of impossibility it does not follow that it is impossible. The investigator al-Dawani replied that the possible only entails an impossibility if it is impossible through another, and the required from the arguments from contradiction is only absolute impossibility, which follows from the impossibility of the impossible. This, and it appears that the necessary impossible if its non-existence is conditional upon its existence, then it yields the impossibility of the substratum, because the assumption of its existence then becomes equivalent to the conjunction of two contradictories, which is impossible by essence. And besides that, it admits two possibilities. This, and God knows best what is correct.

Commentary
This passage defines 'qiyas al-khalf' (argument by contradiction or reductio ad absurdum) and explains its logical structure. The author begins by stating that it is a type of exceptional syllogism (al-qiyas al-istithna'i), where one aims to prove a conclusion by nullifying its contradictory. The name 'khalf' (with a fatha) literally means 'behind' or 'opposite', i.e., it gives the result from its opposite, but the common pronunciation is with a damma meaning 'bad'. The key point is that qiyas al-khalf can be reduced to a combination of a conditional categorical syllogism (iqtirani sharti) and an exceptional syllogism (istithna'i). The reduction works as follows: if the desired conclusion were false, its contradictory would be true; and if the contradictory were true, an impossibility would follow; that impossibility is false, so the contradictory is false, and thus the original conclusion is true. The author notes a condition: the conditional major premise must be necessary, otherwise another syllogism is needed to prove it. He mentions that this reduction is the view of Shaykh al-Rais (Avicenna), and one could also reduce it to two exceptional syllogisms. The passage then presents a doubt (shakk) concerning the use of qiyas al-khalf to prove impossibility: normally, to prove something is impossible, one assumes it is possible, then derives an impossibility from that assumption, thus concluding its impossibility. But the objection is that a possible thing can sometimes entail an impossibility (e.g., the possibility of the non-existence of the first effect would entail the non-existence of God, which is impossible, but the non-existence of the first effect itself is not impossible). The response by al-Dawani clarifies that a possible only entails an impossibility if it is impossible through another (mumtani' bi al-ghayr), and the required impossibility from these arguments is absolute impossibility (mustahil bi al-dhat). The author adds his own remark: if the impossible necessary (al-muhal al-lazim) is such that its non-existence is conditional upon its existence, then it does imply the impossibility of the substratum because assuming its existence becomes like assuming the conjunction of contradictories. Otherwise, it remains open. The commentary serves to refine the understanding of when qiyas al-khalf yields a valid conclusion of impossibility.

A) Whenever an impossibility follows from assuming its possibility
B) Only when the impossibility is absolute (mustahil bi al-dhat) and not merely through another
C) Only when the premises are theoretical
D) Only when the argument uses two exceptional syllogisms

Key Takeaway
Qiyas al-khalf reduces to a conditional categorical and an exceptive syllogism, and its use to prove impossibility requires that the impossibility derived be absolute, not merely conditional on another.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the kalam argument: 'If the universe had no creator, it would exist without a cause. That is impossible. Therefore, the universe has a creator.' Identify whether the conditional premise is necessary or theoretical. If theoretical, what additional syllogism would be needed?
?
Construct a qiyas al-khalf for a fiqh ruling: prove that gambling (maysir) is forbidden by assuming it is permissible and deriving that it involves unjust enrichment, which contradicts the general prohibition of consuming wealth wrongfully.

Refining Reductio: Absolute vs Extrinsic Impossibility
Objection and response on reductio validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the distinction between absolute and extrinsic impossibility to evaluate the validity of a reductio argument in Islamic logic and theology.

Core Idea
A possible implies impossibility only when the impossibility is absolute, not merely extrinsic.

This passage addresses a subtle challenge to reductio arguments: something possible (like the non-existence of the first effect) can imply something impossible (non-existence of God), yet the possible remains possible. Al-Dawani refines the reductio principle by distinguishing intrinsic impossibility from impossibility due to another. This distinction is essential for correctly applying reductio in theological proofs, such as proving the world's temporal origin.

Guided Path
1
Identify the reductio principle under attack
Find the sentence: if a possible thing entails an impossibility, then the possible is impossible. This is the standard reductio formulation.
2
Understand the objection
The objection says that a possible (e.g., non-existence of the first effect) can imply an impossibility (non-existence of the Creator) without becoming impossible itself. This challenges the principle.
The example appeals to a theological truth: the Creator's existence is necessary, so His non-existence is absolutely impossible, but the non-existence of the first effect is merely extrinsically impossible.
3
Analyze al-Dawani's resolution
He says the possible only entails impossibility when it is 'impossible by another' (mumtani' bi al-ghair). In reductio, we seek absolute impossibility (istihalah mutlaqah). If the consequent is only extrinsically impossible, the inference fails.
This distinction prevents false positives in reductio reasoning.
4
Examine the author's own clarification
The author adds that if the impossible consequent, when assumed, leads to a conjunction of opposites, then the antecedent is intrinsically impossible, guaranteeing the validity of the reductio.
This strengthens the condition: only when assuming the antecedent generates a contradiction does the reductio hold.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في القياس الاستثنائي ممكنا لزم من وقوعه المحال، وهو باطل، فإمكانه باطل. ويرد عليه أن الممكن ربما يستلزم محالا، كعدم المعلول الأول عدم الباري تعالى - جل مجده وذكره تعالى عنه علوا كبيرا - فمن لزوم المحال لا يلزم استحالة. أجاب المحقق الدواني، بأن الممكن إنما يستلزم محالا إذا كان ممتنعا بالغير، والمطلوب من القياسات الخلفية ليس إلا مطلق الاستحالة، وهي لازمة من استحالة المحال. هذا والظاهر أن المحال اللازم إن كان عدمه على فرض وجوده فيفيد استحالة الملزوم؛ لأن فرض وجوده حينئذ صار في قوة اجتماع النقيضين، وهو مستحيل بالذات، وفيما عدا ذلك يحتمل الوجهين. هذا، والله أعلم بالصواب.
Section on the conditional syllogism. If it is possible, then from its occurrence an impossible thing follows, and it is false, so its possibility is false. And there is an objection against it: that the possible sometimes implies an impossible thing, like the non-existence of the first effect (which would imply) the non-existence of the Exalted Creator - exalted be His majesty and mention, may He be exalted above that greatly - so from the implication of impossibility, impossibility does not necessarily follow. Al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawani answered: that the possible only implies an impossibility when it is impossible by another, and what is sought from the reductio arguments is only absolute impossibility, and it follows from the impossibility of the impossible. This, and it appears that the impossible which is implied, if its non-existence is upon the assumption of its existence, then it benefits the impossibility of the implicans; because the assumption of its existence then becomes in the strength of the conjunction of two contradictories, which is impossible by essence, and beyond that it admits both possibilities. This, and Allah knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 542

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion about a specific point in conditional syllogism, specifically reductio arguments (burhan khalfi). The author begins by stating the principle: if a possibility implies an impossibility, then that possibility is itself impossible. An objection is raised: something possible can sometimes imply an impossibility, like the non-existence of the first effect would imply the non-existence of the Creator (which is impossible), but that doesn't mean the non-existence of the first effect is impossible. The objection is that implication of impossibility does not entail the impossibility of the implicans. The author then presents the response of al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawani: a possible only implies impossibility when it is impossible through another (mumtani' bi al-ghair). In reductio arguments, what is sought is absolute impossibility (istihalah mutlaqah), and that impossibility is derived from the impossibility of the consequent. Then the author adds his own clarification: if the impossible consequent would be impossible upon the assumption of the antecedent's existence, then that antecedent's existence leads to a conjunction of opposites, which is intrinsically impossible, so the antecedent is impossible. In other cases, it is uncertain. This is a nuanced discussion about the conditions under which reductio reasoning is valid, distinguishing between intrinsic and extrinsic impossibility. The author ends with 'Allahu a'lam' indicating humility about the final judgment.

Key Terms

المحال
الممتنع بالغير
الاستحالة المطلقة

Key Takeaway
In reductio arguments, a possibility implies impossibility only when the impossibility is absolute; otherwise, the possibility may remain viable despite implying an extrinsic impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Consider the reductio argument: 'If the world were eternal, then an infinite number of past events would exist, which is impossible. Therefore, the world is not eternal.' Apply al-Dawani's criterion: Is the impossibility entailed absolute or extrinsic? Explain why.
?
In kalam, the possibility of 'adam (non-existence) of the world before creation is debated. Some say assuming the world's non-existence implies a change in God's will, which they consider impossible. Use the distinction between intrinsic and extrinsic impossibility to evaluate whether this reductio is valid.

Sabr wa Taqsim: The Method of Division and Elimination
Author defends sabr wa taqsim against al-Tusi's critique and explains its epistemic value.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the method of sabr wa taqsim and identify the conditions under which it yields certainty or probability.

Core Idea
Sabr wa taqsim finds the cause by eliminating all but one attribute—but certainty requires airtight enumeration.

In usul al-fiqh, establishing the effective cause ('illa) of a ruling is crucial for analogical reasoning. One method is sabr wa taqsim: surveying all possible attributes and eliminating all but one. This lesson unpacks the method's definition, its epistemic value, and the conditions that determine whether its result is certain or merely probable.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of sabr wa taqsim
The passage says: 'وهو تتبع الأوصاف، وإبطال بعضها لتعين الباقي للعلية' – it is tracking attributes and invalidating some so the remaining one is determined as the cause. Identify the two core actions: tattabu' (surveying) and ibtal (elimination).
2
Recognize the epistemic outcome
The outcome depends on the certainty of the enumeration and invalidation. If both are certain, the result is certain (qat'); if probable, the result is probable (zann). So sabr wa taqsim is not inherently certain.
3
Identify the needed external guidance
In religious matters, enumeration of attributes must be aided by nass (text) or ijma' (consensus) because humans cannot independently survey all possible attributes.
4
Connect to usul al-fiqh
The passage ends by referring details to usul al-fiqh. This method is fundamental for legal analogy (qiyas).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالتَّرْدِيدُ ، وَيُسَمَّى بِالسَّبْرِ وَالتَّقْسِيمِ، وَهُوَ تَتَبَّعُ الْأَوْصَافِ، وَإِبْطَالُ بَعْضِهَا ؛ لِتَعَيُّنِ الْبَاقِي) للعلية. وهذا الطريق حجة بالاتفاق، لكن لا بد في الحصر للأوصاف من الاستعانة بالنص أو الإجماع عندنا في الشرعيات، وإن كان هذا الحصر وإبطال الباقي ظنيين أفاد الظن، وإلا أفاد القطع . والنصير الطوسي لما تقرر في رأيه الكاسد أن الحق ما كان مخالفا لسائر الملل والنحل من أمة محمد صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وأزواجه وسلم قال: «وهم يطالبون أولا بكون الحكم معللا ، وثانيا بحصر الأقسام، وثالثا بالسبر في المزدوجات الثنائية فما فوقها، مما يمكن ولو سلم الجميع لما أفاد القطع أيضا ؛ لأن الجامع إنما يكون علة للحكم في الأصل؛ لكونه أصلا ثم دون الفرع ، وربما ينقسم إلى قسمين، يكون أحدهما علة [للحكم أينما وقع ] ، دون الثاني، وقد اختص الأصل بالأول. ثم إن صح كون الجامع علة في الفرع كان الاستدلال به برهانا، والتمثيل بالأصل حشوا» . اعلم: أن الأئمة القائلين بالقياس التمثيلي شرطوا أن لا يكون الأصل مخصوصا بالحكم، وأن يعلم أن الحكم معلل بالعلة المعينة، وهي موجودة في الفرع ، فإن ثبت هذه الأمور بالقطعيات ثبت الحكم قطعا، وإن ثبت ظنا ثبت الحكم ظنا، وإن لم يثبت أصلا لم يصح التمثيل فقوله «وهم يطالبون إلخ» · قلنا: نحن نثبت إن تم صح التمثيل، وإلا ،لا كما أنكم تطالبون بمقدمات أقيستكم، إن صحت صح، وإلا لا وقوله ولو سلم الجميع لما أفاد «اليقين قلنا إن أراد بعد تسليم قطعية الجميع وقطعية ما شرطوا فذلك سفاهة، وإلا فلا نزاع. وقوله «لأن الجامع إلخ قلنا: نحن نثبت ذلك فيما يستعمل فيه التمثيل، فإن تم تم، وإلا لا في مقدمات أقيستكم. وقوله «ثم إن صح إلخ » قلنا: ربما لا يثبت علية العلة إلا بعد ملاحظة الأصل، كما لا يخفى على من تتبع كتب الفقه، فلا حشو . والمصنف رحمة الله تبع الشيخ، أو نظر إلى الأقيسة المستعملة كثيرا، وقال: (وَهُوَ يُفِيدُ الظَّنَّ، وَالتَّفْصِيلُ فِي أُصُولِ الْفِقْهِ، فاطلب من الكتب
And al-tardid, called sabr wa taqsim, is the examination of attributes and the invalidation of some of them so that the remaining one is determined (for causality). This path is a proof by agreement, but in the enumeration of attributes, one must seek help from text or consensus in our view in religious matters. If this enumeration and invalidation of the remainder are probable, it yields probability; otherwise it yields certainty. Al-Tusi, given his corrupt opinion that truth is what opposes all other sects and creeds of the Ummah of Muhammad (s), said: 'They demand first that the ruling be caused, second the enumeration of categories, third the examination in binary pairs and above, which even if all were conceded would not yield certainty either; because the common factor is a cause for the ruling in the original due to it being original, then it falls short of the branch, and perhaps it divides into two parts, one of which is a cause [for the ruling wherever it occurs], not the second, and the original is characterized by the first. Then if it is valid that the common factor is a cause in the branch, the inference through it would be a demonstration, and the analogy with the original would be redundant.' Know that the imams who hold analogical reasoning (qiyas tamthili) stipulate that the original not be particularized by the ruling, and that it be known that the ruling is caused by the specified cause, and that it exists in the branch. If these matters are established by certainties, the ruling is established certainly; if established by probability, the ruling is established probably; if not established at all, the analogy is invalid. So his statement 'they demand etc.' We say: we establish; if completed, the analogy is valid, otherwise not, just as you demand premises for your syllogisms; if they are sound, they are sound, otherwise not. And his statement 'even if all were conceded would not yield certainty' we say: if he intends after the concession of the certainty of all and the certainty of what they stipulated, that is foolishness; otherwise there is no dispute. And his statement 'because the common factor etc.' we say: we establish that in what analogy is used for; if completed, it is completed, otherwise not in the premises of your syllogisms. And his statement 'then if it is valid etc.' we say: perhaps the causality of the cause is not established except after consideration of the original, as is not hidden from one who examines books of fiqh; so it is not redundant. And the author (rahimahullah) followed the shaykh, or considered the analogies frequently used, and said: 'It yields probability, and the details are in usul al-fiqh, so seek them from the books.'
Source pages p. 549

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary defending the method of sabr wa taqsim (division and elimination) against objections raised by Nasir al-Din al-Tusi. The author first defines the method: it involves surveying the attributes of an original case and invalidating all but one to establish it as the cause ('illa) of the legal ruling. This method is accepted as a proof, but in religious matters, the enumeration of possible attributes must be guided by revealed texts (nass) or consensus (ijma'). The epistemic result depends on the premises: if the enumeration and elimination are certain, the result is certain (qat'); if probable, the result is probable (zann). Al-Tusi's critique argues that even if all demands are met, certainty is impossible because the common factor (al-jami') may not truly be the cause in the branch, or the original may have a unique attribute. The author responds by stating the conditions set by the Hanafi imams for analogical reasoning (qiyas tamthili): the original must not be particularized, the cause must be known and present in the branch. If these conditions are met with certainty, the conclusion is certain; with probability, probable. The author refutes each of al-Tusi's points by comparing them to the requirements for syllogistic premises and asserting that analogy is not redundant because the cause's causality is often grasped only through the original case. Finally, the author notes that the matn author (al-Laqani) followed earlier scholars, concluding that tamthil yields probability, with full detail found in usul al-fiqh.

Key Terms
السبر والتقسيم
التمثيل
العلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does sabr wa taqsim yield certainty?
A) When the enumeration is complete and invalidation is certain.
B) When the ruling is from a revealed text.
C) When al-Tusi approves it.
D) When it is used in non-religious matters.

Key Takeaway
Sabr wa taqsim is a valid method for discovering causes, but its epistemic value depends entirely on the certainty of its steps.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Given a fiqh scenario: a ruling is established in an original case; list three possible attributes that could be the 'illa. Apply sabr wa taqsim hypothetically: eliminate two based on evidence and determine the remaining as the probable cause. Explain whether your result is certain or probable.

Al-Tusi's Critique of Sabr wa Taqsim and Its Refutation
Author's refutation of al-Tusi's critique on sabr wa taqsim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Tusi's objections to sabr wa taqsim and evaluate the author's responses.

Core Idea
Al-Tusi claimed sabr wa taqsim can never yield certainty; the author retorts that his objection applies equally to syllogisms.

Nasir al-Din al-Tusi, known for his skeptical views, launched a threefold critique against the method of sabr wa taqsim used by jurists. He argued that even if all conditions are met, certainty is impossible because the common factor may not be the true cause in the branch. In this lesson, we will examine his objections and the author's pointed responses, which challenge al-Tusi's consistency.

Guided Path
1
Identify al-Tusi's three demands
Al-Tusi says they demand: 1) that the ruling be caused, 2) enumeration of categories, 3) examination of binary pairs. Even if conceded, he says, certainty is not achieved.
2
Understand al-Tusi's reasoning
He argues that the common factor (al-jami') might be a cause in the original but not in the branch, or the original might have a unique attribute. So the analogy fails.
3
Analyze the author's first response
The author replies that if the conditions are met with certainty, the analogy is valid, just as syllogistic premises must be sound. This is a tu quoque argument: al-Tusi's own syllogisms have the same requirement.
4
Analyze the author's second response
The author argues that the causality of the cause is often established only through the original case, so it is not redundant to mention the original. He cites fiqh practice.
5
Evaluate the overall debate
The author concludes that tamthil yields probability, but certainty is possible if the premises are certain. The criticism of al-Tusi is thus deflected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالتَّرْدِيدُ ، وَيُسَمَّى بِالسَّبْرِ وَالتَّقْسِيمِ، وَهُوَ تَتَبَّعُ الْأَوْصَافِ، وَإِبْطَالُ بَعْضِهَا ؛ لِتَعَيُّنِ الْبَاقِي) للعلية. وهذا الطريق حجة بالاتفاق، لكن لا بد في الحصر للأوصاف من الاستعانة بالنص أو الإجماع عندنا في الشرعيات، وإن كان هذا الحصر وإبطال الباقي ظنيين أفاد الظن، وإلا أفاد القطع . والنصير الطوسي لما تقرر في رأيه الكاسد أن الحق ما كان مخالفا لسائر الملل والنحل من أمة محمد صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وأزواجه وسلم قال: «وهم يطالبون أولا بكون الحكم معللا ، وثانيا بحصر الأقسام، وثالثا بالسبر في المزدوجات الثنائية فما فوقها، مما يمكن ولو سلم الجميع لما أفاد القطع أيضا ؛ لأن الجامع إنما يكون علة للحكم في الأصل؛ لكونه أصلا ثم دون الفرع ، وربما ينقسم إلى قسمين، يكون أحدهما علة [للحكم أينما وقع ] ، دون الثاني، وقد اختص الأصل بالأول. ثم إن صح كون الجامع علة في الفرع كان الاستدلال به برهانا، والتمثيل بالأصل حشوا» . اعلم: أن الأئمة القائلين بالقياس التمثيلي شرطوا أن لا يكون الأصل مخصوصا بالحكم، وأن يعلم أن الحكم معلل بالعلة المعينة، وهي موجودة في الفرع ، فإن ثبت هذه الأمور بالقطعيات ثبت الحكم قطعا، وإن ثبت ظنا ثبت الحكم ظنا، وإن لم يثبت أصلا لم يصح التمثيل فقوله «وهم يطالبون إلخ» · قلنا: نحن نثبت إن تم صح التمثيل، وإلا ،لا كما أنكم تطالبون بمقدمات أقيستكم، إن صحت صح، وإلا لا وقوله ولو سلم الجميع لما أفاد «اليقين قلنا إن أراد بعد تسليم قطعية الجميع وقطعية ما شرطوا فذلك سفاهة، وإلا فلا نزاع. وقوله «لأن الجامع إلخ قلنا: نحن نثبت ذلك فيما يستعمل فيه التمثيل، فإن تم تم، وإلا لا في مقدمات أقيستكم. وقوله «ثم إن صح إلخ » قلنا: ربما لا يثبت علية العلة إلا بعد ملاحظة الأصل، كما لا يخفى على من تتبع كتب الفقه، فلا حشو . والمصنف رحمة الله تبع الشيخ، أو نظر إلى الأقيسة المستعملة كثيرا، وقال: (وَهُوَ يُفِيدُ الظَّنَّ، وَالتَّفْصِيلُ فِي أُصُولِ الْفِقْهِ، فاطلب من الكتب

D) That certainty is impossible in any reasoning.

Key Takeaway
Al-Tusi's critique fails because it imposes a double standard: his own syllogisms require the same epistemic conditions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a mini-debate: one person argues that qiyas tamthili can yield certainty, the other objects using al-Tusi's point about the common factor. Then respond as the author does. Write a short dialogue.
?
Find a modern fatwa that uses qiyas. Identify the 'illa and the original case. Evaluate whether the conditions from the passage (original not particularized, cause known, present in branch) are met.

The Syllogism
