The Relationship Between Legal Analogy and Logical Syllogism: A Critical Refinement
Commentator refutes the author's distinction between legal analogy and logical syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the commentator's argument that qiyas fiqhi becomes qiyas mantiqi when the cause is certain, and identify the first-figure categorical syllogism in the nabidh example.

Core Idea
Certainty of the cause transforms legal analogy into a logical syllogism.

In this passage, the commentator Bahr al-Ulum challenges the author's claim that legal analogy (qiyas fiqhi) is fundamentally different from logical syllogism (qiyas mantiqi). The author had argued that even with certain premises, the transfer of ruling remains conjectural due to potential conditions or impediments. The commentator responds by showing that if the cause ('illa) is completely certain, the reasoning becomes a first-figure categorical syllogism that yields certainty. This debate highlights the epistemological foundations of qiyas in Islamic legal theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's claim about qiyas fiqhi
Locate in the passage where the author states that qiyas fiqhi yields only conjecture (zann), not certainty, even if the premises are certain. The reason given is the possibility of conditions (shurūṭ) or impediments (mawāni‘).
The author's view is cited from his work Fawatih al-Rahmut.
2
Extract the commentator's objection
Notice the commentator's strong refutation: 'This is nothing' (laysa bi-shay’). He argues that returning to logical qiyas is not shameful but rather the most correct approach.
3
Analyse the logical structure of the nabidh example
Break down the commentator's example: nabidh contains intoxicating strength (the cause), and everything with that cause is forbidden. This is a standard first-figure categorical syllogism: Major premise: Every intoxicant is forbidden. Minor premise: Nabidh is an intoxicant. Conclusion: Nabidh is forbidden.
This form is known as Barbara in the first figure.
4
Understand the source of certainty vs. conjecture
The commentator clarifies that conjecture (zann) comes from the material (madda) of the premises, i.e., whether the cause is certain or probable. If the cause is certain, the conclusion is certain; if not, it is conjectural. The logical form itself is certain.
This resolves the apparent tension between the two types of qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شرح بحر العلوم على سلم العلوم المصنفة فيه، وتوكل على الله تعالى؛ فإنه ملهم الصواب. هو رأيه في كتابه الأصولي فواتح الرحموت». وهذا هو نص عبارتهما من المسلم وشرحه الفواتح (٢٤٩/٢): (وحكمه ثبوت حكم الأصل في الفرع، والظن به بعد النظر، لا القطع، وإن قطع بمقدماته ومواده، وذلك لأن طريق الإيصال فيه ظني؛ فإنه لا يرفع احتمال كون الأصل شرطا في حكمه وتأثير علته، أو الفرع مانعا عن الحكم، فلا يصل إليه الحكم. ولما كان يرد عليه أن القياس إنما ينتج بملاحظة أن كلما توجد يوجد المعلول، وهذه مقدمة قطعية توجب القطع إن كانت العلة قطعية، وإذا جوز كون الأصل شرطا والفرع مانعا فقد منع علية العلة، وكان الكلام عند قطعية المقدمات قال (ولو قطع بكون العلة علة تامة وبني الإنتاج على تلك المقدمة رجع إلى القياس المنطقي)، ولم يبق قياسا فقهيا (فتفكر)». وهذا الذي قاله المصنف من أن ما رجع إلى القياس المنطقي، وليس تمثيلا هو ما قاله غيره أيضا، كالإصفهاني في تسديد القواعد: ولكن الشارح رد على ما تمسك به المصنف في الفواتح قائلا: «هذا ليس بشيء؛ فإن رجوعه إلى القياس المنطقي لا شناعة فيه، بل هو الأحق بالقبول؛ فإن حاصله أن النبيذ توجد فيه الشدة المطربة التي هي علة الحرمة، وكل ما توجد فيه علة الحرمة فهو حرام، فطريق الإيصال فيه شكل أول قطعي الإنتاج، وإنما يجيئ الظن من المادة من مظنونية العلة، فإذا قطع بالعلية وجب القطع البتة.
Commentary of Bahr al-Ulum on Sullum al-Ulum: '...and rely on Allah Almighty; for He is the inspirer of correctness. This is his opinion in his usul work Fawatih al-Rahmut. And this is the text of their phrase from al-Muslim and its commentary al-Fawatih (2/249): 'And its ruling is the establishment of the original's ruling in the branch, and conjecture about it after consideration, not certainty, even if one is certain of its premises and materials. That is because the method of conveyance in it is conjectural; for it does not remove the possibility that the original is a condition for its ruling and the effect of its cause, or that the branch is an impediment to the ruling, so the ruling does not reach it. And when it is objected that qiyas only yields a result by noting that whenever the cause exists the effect exists, and this is a certain premise that necessitates certainty if the cause is certain, and if he allows the original to be a condition and the branch to be an impediment, he has denied the causality of the cause, and the discussion concerns the certainty of the premises, he said: 'And if he is certain that the cause is a complete cause, and he builds the production on that premise, it returns to logical qiyas,' and it is no longer fiqh qiyas (so reflect).' And this that the author said, that what returns to logical qiyas is not tamthil, is what others also said, like al-Isfahani in Tasdid al-Qawa'id. But the commentator refuted what the author held in al-Fawatih, saying: 'This is nothing; for its return to logical qiyas has no shame in it, rather it is the more deserving of acceptance. For its summary is that nabidh contains the intoxicating strength which is the cause of prohibition, and everything in which the cause of prohibition exists is forbidden, so the method of conveyance is the first figure which produces certainty. Conjecture only comes from the material, from the probability of the cause. So if one is certain of causality, certainty necessarily follows.'
Source pages p. 551

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of the relationship between legal analogy (qiyas fiqhi) and logical syllogism (qiyas mantiqi). The commentator first cites the author's statement from Fawatih al-Rahmut, where the author claims that qiyas fiqhi yields only conjecture (zann) because there may be conditions or impediments preventing the transfer of the ruling. Even if the premises are certain, the method of conveyance remains conjectural because it does not eliminate the possibility that the original has a condition (shart) or the branch has an impediment (mani'). The author then responds to an objection: if one notes that whenever the cause exists the effect exists, that premise is certain and would produce certainty if the cause were certain. If one allows conditions or impediments, that denies the causality of the cause. So if one is certain that the cause is a complete cause and builds the production on that premise, then it becomes a logical syllogism (qiyas mantiqi), not a fiqh analogy. The commentator then refutes the author's view that this return to logical qiyas is problematic. The commentator argues that returning to logical qiyas is not shameful; rather it is the most correct. He gives the example of nabidh (date wine) containing intoxicating strength (al-shidda al-mutriba) which is the cause of prohibition. The reasoning is: every intoxicant is forbidden; nabidh is intoxicating, therefore it is forbidden. This is a first figure categorical syllogism that produces certainty. The uncertainty only arises from the material (madda) of the premises, i.e., whether the cause is certain. If the causality is certain, the conclusion is certain. Thus, the commentator defends the unity of reasoning between legal analogy and logical syllogism when the cause is fully certain.

Key Terms
القياس الفقهي
القياس المنطقي
العِلَّة

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The cause may not be certain.
C) The premises are always conjectural.
D) There may be conditions or impediments that block the transfer of the ruling.

Key Takeaway
The commentator argues that legal analogy and logical syllogism are not fundamentally different; the difference is epistemological, depending on the certainty of the cause.

Sensed Particulars as Premises in Demonstration
Author discusses whether sensed particulars can be premises in demonstrative syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why sensed particulars cannot serve as demonstrative premises and how the intellect abstracts universal premises from sensed particulars.

Core Idea
Sensed particulars are unstable, but the intellect extracts universal premises from them.

This passage tackles a fundamental question in Islamic logic: can our direct sense experiences serve as premises in a demonstrative proof (burhan)? The answer is no, but the role of sense is not negated—it provides material from which the intellect abstracts universal judgments that can be used in demonstration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Notice that the author first states that logicians say sensed particulars cannot be premises in demonstration, then presents a caveat that the intellect can take a universal matter from them.
Look for the words 'قالوا: لا تقع' and 'نعم، للعقل أن يأخذ أمرا كليا'.
2
Understand why sensed particulars are rejected
The reason given is that sensed knowledge is particular and vanishes when sense stops, so it does not yield firm and stable assent required for demonstration.
Key phrase: 'لأنها علوم جزئية زائلة بزوال الحس'.
3
Analyze the intellect's role
The intellect, with the help of sense, abstracts a universal concept (kulli mushtarak) common among sensed particulars, then makes a universal judgment similar to sense's particular judgment.
Focus on: 'بمعونة الحس، ويحكم عليه حكما'.
4
Apply to demonstrative syllogisms
This universal judgment—e.g., 'All fire is hot'—can serve as a premise in demonstration because it is universal and stable, even though it originated from sensed particulars.
Conclude: 'فهذا الحكم يقع مقدمة في البرهان'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن المحسوسات هل تقع مقدمة برهانية أم لا؟ قالوا: لا تقع؛ لأنها علوم جزئية زائلة بزوال الحس، فلا تفيد تصديقا جازما ثابتا، فتأمل فيه. نعم، للعقل أن يأخذ أمرا كليا مشتركا في المحسوسات بمعونة الحس، ويحكم عليه حكما حكم الحس على الجزئيات المحسوسة جزئيتها، وغير ذلك، فهذا الحكم يقع مقدمة في البرهان، فللحس مدخل ما.
Then, do sensed things occur as a premise in demonstration or not? They said: they do not occur, because they are particular pieces of knowledge that vanish with the disappearance of sense, so they do not yield firm and stable assent. Reflect on that. Yes, the intellect can take a universal matter common among sensed things with the help of sense, and make a judgment on it just as sense judges about the particularity of particular sensed things, and other than that; so this judgment occurs as a premise in demonstration. Thus sense has some input.
Source pages p. 561

Commentary
This passage addresses a key question in logic: Can particular sensed judgments serve as premises in a demonstrative syllogism (burhan)? The answer given is that they cannot, because sensed knowledge is particular and transient—it disappears when the sense stops. But the intellect, aided by sense, can extract a universal from sensed particulars (e.g., from observing fire repeatedly, the intellect judges 'all fire is hot'). This universal judgment can serve as a premise in demonstration. Thus sense plays a role, but only as a contributor to the universal premise. The passage distinguishes between empirical particulars and intellectual universals.

Key Terms
مقدمة برهانية
كلي مشترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) Because the intellect cannot verify it.

Key Takeaway
Sensed particulars are particular and transient, so they cannot serve as premises in demonstration; but the intellect abstracts universal premises from them, which can.

Identifying Non-Syllogistic Compounds
Author defines non-syllogistic compound and gives an example of a fallacious conjunction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a non-syllogistic compound and distinguish it from a valid syllogism.

Core Idea
Not every conjunction of true premises is a valid logical argument.

In this passage, the author introduces the concept of a non-syllogistic compound (مركب غير قياسي). Unlike a syllogism, which follows a strict structure with a middle term, a non-syllogistic compound merely joins several propositions without producing a necessary conclusion. This distinction is crucial to avoid conflating mere conjunction with valid inference in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the compound statement
Find a sentence that combines two or more propositions, often using 'and' or 'and also'. In the example, the statement joins 'Muhammad (SAW) alone is the Seal of the Prophets' and 'The Seal of the Prophets is a human'.
2
Check for syllogistic structure
A valid syllogism must have two premises sharing a middle term. Here, the term 'Seal of the Prophets' appears in both premises but does not function as a middle term linking them to produce a necessary conclusion. Instead, the conclusion implied is not derived by syllogistic rules.
If there is no middle term connecting the subjects and predicates in a standard way, it is likely non-syllogistic.
3
Determine productivity
In Islamic logic, a syllogism is 'productive' (muntij) if it yields a conclusion necessarily. The author states this compound is 'not productive' (ghayr muntij) because it is merely a conjunction, not a valid inference.
Contrast this with a real syllogism: 'All humans are mortal; Muhammad is human; therefore Muhammad is mortal.' That is productive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإما أن يتعلق بالمعنى، ولا يتأتى إلا في المركب، فهذا المركب إما مركب غير قياسي كجمع القضايا في سلك قضية واحدة، نحو «محمد صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ فقط خاتم النبيين، وخاتم النبيين إنسان ; فإن حاصل الصغرى موجبة، وهو محمد صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ خاتم النبيين، وهو مع الكبرى ينتج نتيجة صادقة وسالبة، وهو ليس غيره خاتم النبيين»، وهو غير منتج.
And if it pertains to meaning, and it is only realized in a compound, then this compound is either a non-syllogistic compound, like the collection of propositions into a single statement, such as: 'Muhammad صلى الله عليه وسلم only is the Seal of the Prophets, and the Seal of the Prophets is a human'; for the conclusion of the minor premise is affirmative, i.e., Muhammad صلى الله عليه وسلم is the Seal of the Prophets, and it together with the major premise produces a true and negative result, i.e., 'No one other than him is the Seal of the Prophets' – and it is not productive.
Source pages p. 590

Commentary
This passage defines a non-syllogistic compound (مركب غير قياسي). The author explains that when a compound pertains to meaning, it must be a compound. One type of compound is non-syllogistic, which is simply the collection of multiple propositions into a single statement without a syllogistic structure. The example given is: 'Muhammad (peace be upon him) alone is the Seal of the Prophets, and the Seal of the Prophets is a human.' This is not a valid syllogism because it does not follow the form of a categorical syllogism; rather, it is a conjunction that yields a true but non-deductive conclusion. The author points out that the minor premise is affirmative and the major premise, when combined, produces a true negative conclusion ('No other is the Seal of the Prophets'), but this is not a valid logical inference. This guards against the misunderstanding that any combination of true premises yields a valid logical argument.

Key Terms
مركب غير قياسي
جمع القضايا

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the conclusion is false
D) Because the premises are not true

Key Takeaway
A non-syllogistic compound is a conjunction of propositions without the necessary logical structure to produce a valid inference; it is not a productive syllogism.

Material Fallacies in Syllogistic Compounds
Author explains material fallacies where premises cannot be simultaneously true and in syllogistic form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to detect a material fallacy in a syllogistic compound by verifying whether the premises can be both true and in proper syllogistic form.

Core Idea
A material fallacy occurs when premises cannot be simultaneously true and in a valid syllogistic form.

In this lesson, we explore a nuanced type of error in syllogisms: material fallacies. Unlike formal fallacies which concern the structure, material fallacies arise from the content of the premises. The author uses two key examples to demonstrate how premises may be true individually but fail to form a valid syllogism, or may form a valid syllogism but contain a false premise. This lesson will equip you to identify such fallacies in Islamic scholarly arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
A valid syllogism requires both true premises and a correct syllogistic form (e.g., proper distribution of the middle term). Recognize that both conditions must hold simultaneously.
2
Test condition 1: Assume form is valid
For the given pair of premises, arrange them in a valid syllogistic form and check if they can both be true. If one premise becomes false, condition 1 fails.
3
Test condition 2: Assume premises are true
Take the premises as true and check whether they form a valid syllogistic structure (e.g., middle term repeated). If the structure is invalid, condition 2 fails.
4
Identify the material fallacy
If the premises cannot satisfy both conditions simultaneously, the argument suffers from a material fallacy. Note which condition fails and why.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإما مركب قياسي، فإما أن يكون في القضايا، فإما أن يكو الغلط بالقياس إلى النتيجة، أو لا بالقياس إليها، والثاني إما من جهة المادة، كما إذا كان بحيث لو أخذت إحدى المقدمتين صادقة لم يكن على هيئة قياس، وإن أخذ على هيئة قياس لم يكن صادقة ، نحو الغلط غلط ، والغلط فصيح»، فإن أخذ موضوع الكبرى لفظ الغلط صدقت، لكن لا تكون الهيئة هيئة قياس؛ لعدم تكرر الأوسط. وإن أخذ ما يصدق عليه الغلط كذبت، ولو كان الهيئة هيئة قياس ، نحو: وَلَوْ عَلِمَ اللهُ فِيهِمْ خَيْرًا لَأَسْمَعَهُمْ وَلَوْ أَسْمَعَهُمْ لَتَولوا [الأنفال/ ٢٣]؛ فإنه إن أخذت الكبرى مهملة - كما أخذها الله تعالى - صدقت ، لكن لم تكن الهيئة هيئة قياس، وإن أخذت كلية كذبت ; لأن من تقادير الإسماع علم الله فيهم خيرا، ولم تكن مرادة الله تعالى. وما قال العلامة إن المراد بالإسماع إسماع القبول في الصغرى، وإسماع الصماخ في الكبرى، فلم يتكرر الأوسط، فهو صحيح في هذه الآية، لكنه غير قالع لمادة الشبهة(۱) ، فتأمل. (۱) قال في المنهية: وذلك لأن سمع الصماخ من لوازم سمع القبول، فإن علم الله فيهم خيرا لأسمعهم سمع القبول، فيصدق لو علم الله فيهم خيرا لأسمعهم سمع الصماخ ، فيضم مع الكبرى، فيصير قياسا مع كذب النتيجة ، فإذن يحتاج في فك عقدة المغالطة إلى ما ذكرناه ، فافهم
And if it is a syllogistic compound, then it is either concerning the premises; and either the error is with respect to the conclusion or not with respect to it. The second is either from the perspective of matter, as when if one premise is taken as true, it does not have a syllogistic form, and if taken in syllogistic form, it is not true; such as: 'Error is error, and error is eloquent.' For if the subject of the major premise is taken as the word 'error', it is true, but the form is not syllogistic due to the lack of repetition of the middle. And if one takes what is predicated of error, it is false, even if the form is syllogistic. Another example: 'And if Allah had known any good in them, He would have made them hear; and if He had made them hear, they would have turned away' [al-Anfal 23]. For if the major premise is taken as indefinite, as Allah took it, it is true, but the form is not syllogistic; and if taken as universal, it is false, because among the assumptions of hearing is that Allah knows good in them, but that was not intended by Allah. And what the commentator said, that what is meant by hearing in the minor premise is the hearing of acceptance, and in the major premise the hearing of the ear, so the middle term is not repeated – this is correct regarding this verse, but it does not uproot the material of the sophism. (1) He said in al-Munhiyya: That is because the hearing of the ear is a necessary consequence of the hearing of acceptance; for if Allah had known good in them, He would have made them hear the hearing of acceptance; so it is true that if Allah had known good in them, He would have made them hear the hearing of the ear, so it joins with the major premise and becomes a syllogism with a false conclusion. So one needs, in order to untie the knot of sophism, what we have mentioned; so understand.
Source pages p. 590

Commentary
This passage discusses material fallacies in syllogistic compounds. The author distinguishes two types of errors: those with respect to the conclusion and those without. Focusing on the latter, he explains material fallacies where the premises cannot be simultaneously true and in a valid syllogistic form. The first example: 'Error is error, and error is eloquent.' If we take the subject of the major premise as the word 'error' itself, the premise is true but the form lacks a repeated middle term. If we take the subject as something to which error applies (i.e., a particular error), the premise becomes false. The second example is the Quranic verse from al-Anfal. If the major premise 'If He had made them hear, they would have turned away' is taken as indefinite (muhmala) as in the Quran, it is true but not in syllogistic form. If taken as universal, it is false because it implies that Allah knew good in them. The commentator (al-Allama) suggests that the term 'hearing' is ambiguous: hearing of acceptance in the minor and hearing of the ear in the major, so the middle term is not repeated. However, the author notes that this interpretation does not fully resolve the sophism, because the latter even if true, would make the premises true and the conclusion false. This passage guards against the misunderstanding that any true conditional premise can be used in a syllogism without proper form.

Key Terms
الغلط المادي (al-ghalat al-madi): A fallacy occurring in the matter (content) of the premises, where they cannot be simultaneously true and in proper syllogistic form. [Note: This term is standard in logic; no significant shift.]
الأوسط (al-awsat): The middle term of a syllogism; it must be repeated in both premises for a valid categorical syllogism. [Note: Standard term.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first example 'Error is error, and error is eloquent', why is it a material fallacy?
A) Because the term 'error' is ambiguous.
B) Because the premises cannot be both true and in valid syllogistic form.
C) Because the conclusion is false.
D) Because the middle term is not repeated in both premises.

Key Takeaway
A valid syllogism requires both true premises and a correct form; material fallacies occur when these conditions cannot coexist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the statement: 'All knowledge is acquired; some acquired things are beneficial; therefore some knowledge is beneficial.' Check if the premises can be simultaneously true and in proper syllogistic form. Is there a material fallacy? (Hint: The conclusion may be true, but check the conditions.)
?
Analyze the following from a Kalam debate: 'If Allah had willed, He would have created the world; if He had created the world, it would be temporal; therefore if Allah had willed, the world is temporal.' Identify whether this is a valid syllogism and whether it suffers from a material fallacy.

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Identifying Non-Productive Moods in Syllogisms
Author illustrates a formal fallacy with a second-figure, two-affirmative syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a non-productive mood in the second figure of a categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
A second figure with two affirmatives is never productive.

This lesson examines a formal fallacy where a syllogism in the second figure has two affirmative premises, making it invalid. Understanding this helps avoid misapplications of syllogistic form in theological and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure
The example has two premises: 'Time encompasses events' and 'The celestial sphere encompasses events'. The middle term 'encompasses events' is the predicate of both premises, placing it in the second figure.
In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate of both premises.
2
Identify the mood
Both premises are affirmative (A or I). The mood is AA with two universal affirmatives (A).
In standard notation, this is AAA in the second figure.
3
Check productivity conditions
A valid second-figure syllogism requires one premise to be negative. With both premises affirmative, the conclusion does not follow necessarily.
The condition: in the second figure, the major premise must be negative.
4
Recognize why it fails
The conclusion 'Time is the celestial sphere' is not entailed by the premises. The premises only state that both time and the sphere encompass events, but they could be distinct.
This is an example of a formal fallacy (ghalat suri).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإما من جهة الصورة، كما إذا لم يكن على هيئة ضرب منتج، نحو الزمان محيط بالحوادث، والفلك أيضا محيط بها، فالزمان هو الفلك»، وهو شكل ثان من موجبتين، والذي بالقياس إلى النتيجة فإما بأخذها بعينها في القياس، ويسمى مُصَادَرَةً على المطلوب ، نحو أسامة الأسد، والأسد حيوان مفترس»، أو يكون القياس غير منتج للنتيجة المطلوبة، كما إذا قيل «مس الذكر ناقض للوضوء؛ لأنه حال البول حدث، وكل حدث ناقض»؛ فإن ما لزم منه مس الذكر حال البول ناقض غير المطلوب ، ويسمى هذا أخذ ما ليس بعلة علةً.
And as for the perspective of form, such as when it is not in a productive mood, like: 'Time encompasses events, and the celestial sphere also encompasses them, so time is the celestial sphere' – this is a second figure with two affirmatives. And that which is with respect to the conclusion: either it takes the conclusion itself in the syllogism, called begging the question (musadara 'ala al-matlub), such as 'Usama is a lion, and the lion is a predatory animal'; or the syllogism does not produce the desired conclusion, as when it is said: 'Touching the penis invalidates ablution because it is a state of urine and every state of urine is an invalidating event' – for what follows from it, i.e., touching the penis during urination is invalidating, is not the desired conclusion, and this is called taking what is not a cause as a cause.
Source pages p. 591

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of fallacies in syllogistic compounds, now focusing on formal fallacies and fallacies related to the conclusion. First, a formal fallacy: the example 'Time encompasses events, the celestial sphere encompasses events, so time is the celestial sphere' is an invalid syllogism in the second figure with two affirmative premises, which is not productive. Second, fallacies related to the conclusion: the first is 'begging the question' (مصادرة على المطلوب), where the conclusion is taken as a premise; the example 'Usama is a lion, and the lion is a predatory animal' presupposes the conclusion. The second is taking what is not a cause as a cause (أخذ ما ليس بعلة علة), as in the argument that touching the penis invalidates wudu because it occurs during urination, and urination is an invalidating event – but the actual cause is urine itself, not touching. The syllogism produces a conclusion that is not the desired one. This guards against confusing valid and invalid syllogistic forms and against circular reasoning or misattribution of causes.

Key Terms
مُصَادَرَةً على المطلوب (musadara 'ala al-matlub): Begging the question; taking the conclusion itself as a premise in the syllogism. [Note: Standard logical fallacy.]
أخذ ما ليس بعلة علة (akhdhu ma laysa bi-'illatin 'illatan): Taking what is not a cause as a cause; a fallacy where the true cause is confused with an accidental attribute. [Note: Standard in Islamic logic.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What makes the syllogism 'Time encompasses events; the celestial sphere encompasses events; therefore time is the celestial sphere' invalid?
A) It is in the first figure.
B) It has two negative premises.
C) It is in the second figure with two affirmative premises.
D) The middle term is not distributed.

Key Takeaway
A second-figure syllogism with two affirmative premises is always invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a valid second-figure categorical syllogism on a kalam topic (e.g., 'No temporal thing is eternal; the world is temporal; so the world is not eternal'). Then modify it to have two affirmatives and show it is invalid.
?
Identify whether the following is a valid second-figure mood: 'Every act of worship is commanded; no act of disobedience is commanded; so no act of disobedience is an act of worship.' (Answer: valid, EAE).

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Detecting Begging the Question (Musadara)
Author explains the fallacy of begging the question with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify begging the question in a syllogism.

Core Idea
Begging the question hides the conclusion in the premises.

This lesson focuses on the fallacy of begging the question (musadara 'ala al-matlub), where the argument assumes what it intends to prove. Recognizing this fallacy prevents circular reasoning in Islamic legal and theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion
Determine what the argument is trying to prove. In the example 'Usama is a lion, and the lion is a predatory animal', the implied conclusion is 'Usama is a predatory animal'.
The conclusion may not be explicitly stated but is deduceable.
2
Check if the conclusion appears as a premise
One premise is 'Usama is a lion', which already asserts that Usama is a predatory animal (if we accept that lions are predatory). The conclusion is thus contained in the premise.
The argument does not provide new support; it merely restates the claim.
3
Label as begging the question
Since the conclusion is assumed in the premises, the argument is circular and commits the fallacy of musadara.
This fallacy often occurs when a key term is defined in a question-begging way.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإما من جهة الصورة، كما إذا لم يكن على هيئة ضرب منتج، نحو الزمان محيط بالحوادث، والفلك أيضا محيط بها، فالزمان هو الفلك»، وهو شكل ثان من موجبتين، والذي بالقياس إلى النتيجة فإما بأخذها بعينها في القياس، ويسمى مُصَادَرَةً على المطلوب ، نحو أسامة الأسد، والأسد حيوان مفترس»، أو يكون القياس غير منتج للنتيجة المطلوبة، كما إذا قيل «مس الذكر ناقض للوضوء؛ لأنه حال البول حدث، وكل حدث ناقض»؛ فإن ما لزم منه مس الذكر حال البول ناقض غير المطلوب ، ويسمى هذا أخذ ما ليس بعلة علةً.
And as for the perspective of form, such as when it is not in a productive mood, like: 'Time encompasses events, and the celestial sphere also encompasses them, so time is the celestial sphere' – this is a second figure with two affirmatives. And that which is with respect to the conclusion: either it takes the conclusion itself in the syllogism, called begging the question (musadara 'ala al-matlub), such as 'Usama is a lion, and the lion is a predatory animal'; or the syllogism does not produce the desired conclusion, as when it is said: 'Touching the penis invalidates ablution because it is a state of urine and every state of urine is an invalidating event' – for what follows from it, i.e., touching the penis during urination is invalidating, is not the desired conclusion, and this is called taking what is not a cause as a cause.

Commentary

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the argument 'Usama is a lion, and the lion is a predatory animal' commit the fallacy of begging the question?
A) Because it uses an invalid syllogistic figure.
B) Because the conclusion 'Usama is a predatory animal' is already contained in the premise 'Usama is a lion'.
C) Because it involves an equivocation on the term 'lion'.
D) Because it relies on an analogy.

Key Takeaway
Begging the question occurs when the argument assumes what it is supposed to prove.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Examine this argument: 'The Quran is the word of Allah because it is revealed from Allah.' Does it commit begging the question? Explain why or why not.
?
Find an example of musadara in a contemporary Islamic debate (e.g., about the permissibility of music) and rewrite it as a non-circular argument.

Avoiding False Cause (Akhdhu Ma Laysa Bi-'Illatin 'Illatan)
Author explains false cause fallacy with a fiqh example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the false cause fallacy in a syllogism and distinguish the true cause.

Core Idea
A false cause error confuses an accidental attribute with the actual cause.

This lesson examines the fallacy of taking what is not a cause as a cause (أخذ ما ليس بعلة علة). Using a fiqh example about wudu invalidation, we learn to separate genuine causes from incidental circumstances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the syllogism's structure
The argument: 'Touching the penis during urination is invalidating because it is a state of urine, and every state of urine is invalidating.' Identify the conclusion sought: 'Touching the penis invalidates wudu.'
The desired conclusion is general, not limited to urination.
2
Find the actual cause
The true cause (illa) of invalidation is the urine itself, not the act of touching. The argument mistakenly treats 'state of urine' as equivalent to 'urine'.
The fallacy confuses a concomitant circumstance with the cause.
3
Check if the conclusion matches the premises
The premises only support that touching during urination is invalidating, not that touching in general (outside urination) is invalidating. The argument fails to prove the desired general conclusion.
This is why the syllogism is non-productive for the intended conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإما من جهة الصورة، كما إذا لم يكن على هيئة ضرب منتج، نحو الزمان محيط بالحوادث، والفلك أيضا محيط بها، فالزمان هو الفلك»، وهو شكل ثان من موجبتين، والذي بالقياس إلى النتيجة فإما بأخذها بعينها في القياس، ويسمى مُصَادَرَةً على المطلوب ، نحو أسامة الأسد، والأسد حيوان مفترس»، أو يكون القياس غير منتج للنتيجة المطلوبة، كما إذا قيل «مس الذكر ناقض للوضوء؛ لأنه حال البول حدث، وكل حدث ناقض»؛ فإن ما لزم منه مس الذكر حال البول ناقض غير المطلوب ، ويسمى هذا أخذ ما ليس بعلة علةً.
And as for the perspective of form, such as when it is not in a productive mood, like: 'Time encompasses events, and the celestial sphere also encompasses them, so time is the celestial sphere' – this is a second figure with two affirmatives. And that which is with respect to the conclusion: either it takes the conclusion itself in the syllogism, called begging the question (musadara 'ala al-matlub), such as 'Usama is a lion, and the lion is a predatory animal'; or the syllogism does not produce the desired conclusion, as when it is said: 'Touching the penis invalidates ablution because it is a state of urine and every state of urine is an invalidating event' – for what follows from it, i.e., touching the penis during urination is invalidating, is not the desired conclusion, and this is called taking what is not a cause as a cause.
Source pages p. 591

Commentary

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the argument about touching the penis and wudu commit the false cause fallacy?
A) Because it uses an invalid syllogistic figure.
B) Because it mistakenly treats 'state of urine' as the cause of invalidation, while the actual cause is the urine itself.
C) Because it begs the question.
D) Because it relies on analogy.

Key Takeaway
The false cause fallacy confuses an incidental condition with the real cause, leading to an irrelevant conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
